Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
The Nature of Goodness and Badness: Hanafi vs Mu'tazila
Dispute over the nature of husn and qubh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi/Maturidi position on al-husn wa al-qubh, explain why abrogation (naskh) is possible, and evaluate the Mu'tazila counterargument using particularities.

Core Idea
Goodness and badness are not fixed in acts; they are determined by divine law and can change, making naskh possible.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute: are moral values inherent in actions, or are they assigned by revelation? The Hanafi/Maturidi school takes a broad view that allows for change, while the Mu'tazila insist on inherent moral qualities. This difference has direct consequences for the possibility of abrogation (naskh). We will unpack the arguments and see why the Hanafi position is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rival positions on husn/qubh
List the views mentioned: early scholars (essence), later scholars (real attribute), some (only badness real), al-Jubba'i (considerations), and the Hanafi/Maturidi general view.
The Hanafi view is the broadest: it accepts any of these bases.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila core claim
The Mu'tazila hold that goodness and badness are inherent in the act's essence, so they cannot change. This leads them to reject naskh.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rational argument
If husn/qubh were inherent, then abrogation (e.g., the change in qibla or wine prohibition) would be impossible. Since naskh is a fact of Shari'a, the Mu'tazila position is untenable.
The Hanafi argument is aqli (rational), not naqli.
4
Examine the Mu'tazila response via particularities
They argue that particularities (khususiyyat) change over time, so an act can be good in one era and bad in another. This would preserve naskh.
But this leads to al-Jubba'i's view that husn/qubh are mere considerations, not inherent.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi/Maturidi position is more consistent: it allows for naskh and does not require inherent moral qualities. The Mu'tazila response collapses into the Hanafi view.
Thus the relied-upon position is that husn/qubh are determined by Shari'a, not reason alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا فيما بينهم (فقال القدماء منهم الحسن والقبح كلاهما لذات الفعل و) قال (المتأخرون) لا ( بل) كلاهما لصفة حقيقية توجبه أي كلا من الحسن والقبح (فيهما) أي في الفعل الحسن والقبيح (و) قال (قوم لصفة حقيقية في القبح فقط) دون الحسن والحسن عدم القبح فلا يناط بصفة حقيقية، ونقل عن العلامة لا يظهر لهذا القول سبب صحيح (والجبائي قال : ليس الحسن والقبح (صفة حقيقية بل اعتبارات ووجوه (والحق عندنا) معشر أهل السنة من الصوفية والماتريدية الإطلاق الأعم من كونهما لذات الفعل أو صفته، أو لوجوه و اعتبارات فعند بطلانها يبطل الحسن ويتغير وأما المعتزلة القائلون بكونهما لذات الفعل لا يصح عندهم بطلان الحسن فيرد النسخ عليهم وسيجيء الدفع على رأيهم أيضاً إن شاء الله تعالى وأجيب من قبلهم أن الخصوصيات التي كانت في أول الزمان معتبرة في محل الحسن والقبح، فالفعل كان في الزمان الأول معه خصوصيات معها كان حسناً واجباً، خصوصيات الزمان الثاني يكون قبيحاً وحراماً فيصح النسخ ، ولا يخفى أنه حينئذ يكون قليل الجدوى أو آئلاً إلى قول الجبائية
Then they differed among themselves: the early scholars said both goodness and badness are due to the essence of the act, and the later scholars said no, but both are due to a real attribute that necessitates them, i.e., each of goodness and badness (are) in the act that is good or bad. And some people said: a real attribute only in badness, not in goodness, and goodness is the absence of badness, so it is not attached to a real attribute. And it is reported from the scholar that no sound reason appears for this view. And al-Jubba'i said: goodness and badness are not real attributes but rather considerations and aspects. And the truth with us, the community of Ahl al-Sunna from the Sufis and Maturidis, is the absolute general view, whether they are due to the essence of the act, or its attribute, or aspects and considerations; for when they are invalidated, goodness is invalidated and changes. As for the Mu'tazila who say they are due to the essence of the act, according to them nullification of goodness is not valid, so abrogation is refuted against them, and a refutation of their view will also come, God willing. And it was answered on their behalf that the particularities that existed in the first time are considered in the locus of goodness and badness: the act in the first time had particularities by which it was good and obligatory; the particularities of the second time make it bad and forbidden, so abrogation is valid. And it is not hidden that then it is of little benefit or returns to the view of al-Jubba'iyya.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) among scholars regarding the nature of al-husn wa al-qubh (goodness and badness). The author presents several views: the early scholars (qudama') held that both are inherent in the essence of the act; the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) said they are due to a real attribute (sifa haqiqiyya) that necessitates them; some said only badness is due to a real attribute, while goodness is merely absence; al-Jubba'i said they are not real attributes but considerations (i'tibarat). The Hanafi/Maturidi position (al-haqq 'indana) is the broadest: they accept all possibilities, holding that goodness and badness can be due to essence, attribute, or consideration. The key point is that these qualities are not fixed but can change with context, allowing for abrogation (naskh). The passage then addresses the Mu'tazila who insist goodness/badness are inherent and thus reject abrogation; they are answered that particularities (khususiyyat) change over time, so abrogation is valid. This refutes the Mu'tazila position and aligns with the Hanafi view that moral values are determined by divine law, not inherent in acts.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح (al-husn wa al-qubh): Moral goodness and badness of actions, which are qualities that can be known either by reason (aql) or by revelation (shar).
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revelation; the possibility depends on whether goodness/badness are inherent or mutable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi/Maturidi position on the nature of al-husn wa al-qubh (goodness and badness) according to this passage?
A) They are both inherent in the essence of the act.
B) They are both due to a real attribute in the act.
C) They are not fixed but can be based on essence, attribute, or considerations, and are known by revelation.
D) They are purely rational and unchanging.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi/Maturidi view on husn and qubh as flexible, revelation-dependent qualities is essential for justifying the validity of naskh (abrogation) in Islamic law.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
Three Positions on Husn/Qubh and the Mountain Peak Case
Summary of three schools' views and their application to a person unreached by revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions on husn/qubh in Hanafi usul, apply them to the mountain peak case, and evaluate the rational arguments for rational moral knowledge.

Core Idea
Moral values: Ash'ari say revealed only; Hanafis say rational in essentials; Ibn al-Humam says rational but non-necessitating.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut culminates the discussion on moral epistemology by laying out three distinct positions within the Islamic tradition. Understanding these positions is crucial for usul al-fiqh because they determine whether a person who has never received prophetic revelation is accountable before God. The text also presents two rational arguments supporting the view that some moral values are known by reason, which is the preferred Hanafi approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage to extract the Ash'ari, Hanafi/Mu'tazila rationalist, and Ibn al-Humam positions on whether husn/qubh are Shari or rational, and whether they entail rulings.
Focus on the key phrases: 'الأول : مذهب الأشعرية', 'الثاني : أنهما عقليان وهما مناطان', 'الثالث: أن الحسن والقبح عقليان وليسا موجبين للحكم'.
2
Apply to the mountain peak case
For each position, determine the accountability of a person who never received revelation. Note that Hanafis hold him accountable for kufr and leaving iman, but not necessarily other acts; Ash'aris and Ibn al-Humam hold him not accountable at all.
The case is 'شاهق الجبل' – a hypothetical used to test the implications of each theory.
3
Analyze the two rational proofs
Examine the two aqli arguments: (1) the arbitrariness argument (tarjih bila murajjih) and (2) the mercy argument (revelation as rahma, not bala'). Understand how each supports rational moral knowledge.
These proofs are intended to refute the purely Shari position.
4
Distinguish Hanafi from Mu'tazila
Note that while both affirm rational moral knowledge, Hanafis limit its scope to essentials like iman and shukr, and do not require punishment for rational evil. This is opposed to the Mu'tazila who extend it further and necessitate justice.
The text says: 'هذا خلاف' between them lies in the range of acts and the obligation of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه لا فرق بين قول هؤلاء الكرام وقول المعتزلة، فإنهم كانوا قائلين إن حسن بعض الأشياء مما يدرك بالعقل ولا يتوقف على البعثة وهؤلاء الكرام أيضاً قالوا بذلك ، فلو كان خلاف لكان في تعيين ذلك البعض من الأحكام والظاهر من كلماتهم أن ذلك البعض هو الإيمان والشكر ونحوهما، وعند المعتزلة كثير، ويفهم من كلام الإمام فخر الإسلام أن حاصل النزاع بيننا وبينهم أن العقل عندهم علة موجبة للحكم، وعند الأشعرية مهدرة لا اعتبار لها، وعندنا لا هذا ولا ذاك، بل العقل يوجب أهلية الحكم وتعلق الحكم من العليم الخبير، والنزاع هكذا لا يليق أن يقع بين أهل الإسلام، لما مر أن إجماع المسلمين على أن لا حكم إلا الله تعالى فخرج حاصل البحث أن ههنا ثلاثة أقوال : الأول : مذهب الأشعرية أن الحسن والقبح في الأفعال شرعي، وكذلك الحكم. الثاني : أنهما ،عقليان وهما مناطان لتعلق الحكم، فإذا أدرك في بعض الأفعال كالإيمان والكفر والشكر والكفران يتعلق الحكم منه تعالى بذمة العبد وهو مذهب هؤلاء الكرام والمعتزلة إلا أنه عندنا لا تجب العقوبة بحسب القبح العقلي كما لا تجب بعد ورود الشرع لاحتمال العفو بخلاف هؤلاء بناء على وجوب العدل عندهم بمعنى إيصال الثواب إلى من أتى بالحسنات، وإيصال العقاب إلى من أتى بالقبائح. الثالث: أن الحسن والقبح عقليان وليسا موجبين للحكم ولا كاشفين عن تعلقه بذمة العبد، وهو مختار الشيخ ابن الهمام صاحب التحرير، وتبعه المصنف، ورأيت في بعض الكتب وجدت مشايخنا الذين لاقيتهم قائلين مثل قول الأشعرية وبما حررنا من المذاهب يتفرع عليه مسألة البالغ في شاهق (الجبل) أي الذي لم تبلغه الدعوة، فعند المعتزلة مؤاخذ بترك الحسنات وفعل القبائح ومثاب بالحسنات وعند هؤلاء المشايخ يؤاخذ بإتيان الكفر مطلقاً وبترك الإيمان عند مضي مدة التأمل والمؤاخذة بترك ما سوى الإيمان وأمثاله من الشكر لم يعلم حالها برواية صريحة بأنهم هل يعذرون بعدم درك العقل إياها للدلائل أم لا، وعند الأشعرية والشيخ ابن الهمام لا يؤاخذون ولو أتوا بالشرك والعياذ بالله تعالى. ثم اعلم أن مسألة الحسن والقبح وكذا استلزامهما للحكم يمكن أن تكون كلامية راجعة إلى أن الله تعالى لا يحكم إلا بما هو حسن أو قبيح، وإن حكم الله ملزومهما، وأن تكون أصولية راجعة إلى أن الأمر الإلهي يدل على الحسن ،اقتضاء والنهي الإلهي يدل على القبح كذلك، وأن تكون فقهية راجعة إلى أن الفعل الواجب يكون حسناً والحرام قبيحاً، فقد بان أن الأولى أن تسرد في المقاصد دون المبادىء (لنا) في إثبات نفس الحسن والقبح العقليين أعم من استلزامهما الحكم، أوّلاً أنه لو كانا لكانت الصلاة والزنا متساويين في نفس الأمر قبل بعثة الرسل، فجعل أحدهما واجباً والآخر حراماً ليس أولى من العكس، وهو ترجيح من غير مرجح مناف لحكمة الأمر وهو حكيم البتة قطعاً. ولنا فيه ثانياً لو كانا شرعيين لكان إرسال الرسل بلاء وفتنة لا رحمة، لأنهم كانوا قبل ذلك في رفاهية لعدم صحة المؤاخذة بشيء مما يستلذه الإنسان ثم بعد مجيء الرسل صاروا ببعض تلك الأفاعيل في عذاب أبدي، فأي فائدة في إرسال الرسل إلا التضييق وتعذيب عباده، فصار بلاء هذا خلف لأنه رحمة يمنّ الله تعالى به على عباده في كثير من مواضع تنزيله واعلم أن هذا الدليل كما يدل على الحسن والقبح العقليين كذلك يدل على أن وجوب
Then know that there is no difference between the statement of these noble people and the statement of the Mu'tazila, for they were saying that the goodness of some things is perceived by reason and does not depend on the mission; and these noble people also said that. So if there were a difference, it would be in the specification of that part of the rulings. And the apparent from their words is that that part is faith and gratitude and the like, while for the Mu'tazila it is many. And it is understood from the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam that the essence of the dispute between us and them is that according to them, reason is a cause necessitating the ruling, while according to the Ash'aris, it is disregarded with no consideration; and according to us, it is neither this nor that, rather reason necessitates the capacity for the ruling and the attachment of the ruling from the All-Knowing, All-Aware. And a dispute like this is not fitting to occur among the people of Islam, because it has passed that the consensus of Muslims is that there is no ruling except from God. So the outcome of the research is that here there are three statements: First: the Ash'ari school that goodness and badness in actions are Shari (revealed), and likewise the ruling. Second: they are rational and are the loci for the attachment of the ruling; so when it is perceived in some acts like faith, disbelief, gratitude, and ingratitude, the ruling from God attaches to the responsibility of the servant; this is the school of these noble people and the Mu'tazila, except that according to us punishment is not obligatory according to rational badness, just as it is not obligatory after the arrival of the law due to the possibility of pardon, contrary to them based on their principle of the obligation of justice, meaning delivering reward to one who does good deeds, and delivering punishment to one who does bad deeds. Third: goodness and badness are rational but do not necessitate the ruling nor disclose its attachment to the servant's responsibility; this is the choice of Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, author of al-Tahrir, and the author followed him; and I have seen in some books that our teachers whom I met held views like the Ash'aris. And according to what we have formulated of the schools, it branches into the case of an adult on a mountain peak, i.e., one whom the call has not reached. According to the Mu'tazila, he is held accountable for leaving good deeds and doing bad deeds, and is rewarded for good deeds. According to these masters (the Hanafis mentioned), he is held accountable for committing disbelief absolutely, and for leaving faith after the passing of the period of reflection; as for holding accountable for leaving what is other than faith and the like of gratitude, its state is not known from a clear narration—whether they excuse due to reason not perceiving them for the proofs or not. According to the Ash'aris and Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, they are not held accountable even if they commit polytheism, God forbid. Then know that the issue of goodness and badness and their entailment of rulings can be a kalam issue returning to God does not rule except by what is good or bad, and that God's ruling is entailed by them; and it can be a usul issue returning to the fact that the divine command indicates goodness by implication, and the divine prohibition indicates badness likewise; and it can be a fiqh issue returning to the fact that the obligatory act is good and the forbidden act is bad. So it appears that it is better to list it in the objectives rather than the principles. (We have) to establish rational goodness and badness themselves, regardless of whether they entail rulings. First: if they were [purely rational], then prayer and fornication would be equal in reality before the sending of messengers, so making one obligatory and the other forbidden would not be more preferable than the opposite, which is a preference without a preferrer, contradictory to the wisdom of the command—and He is absolutely Wise. And we have second: if they were Shari (purely revealed), then sending messengers would be a calamity and trial, not mercy, because before that they were in ease due to the invalidity of being held accountable for anything that pleases man; then after the coming of messengers, they become from some of those acts into eternal torment. What benefit is there in sending messengers except restriction and tormenting His servants? So it becomes a calamity—this is contrary to fact, because it is a mercy that God bestows on His servants in many places of His revelation. And know that this proof indicates rational goodness and badness and also indicates the obligation...
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by summarizing three major positions on the nature of husn/qubh and their implications for a specific case. First, the author notes that the position of the 'noble people' (Hanafis who accept rational moral knowledge) is essentially that of the Mu'tazila on the principle—both affirm that some moral values are known by reason—but they differ in scope: Hanafis limit this to Iman, shukr, etc., while Mu'tazila extend it further. The essence of the dispute according to Fakhr al-Islam is about the function of reason: Mu'tazila see it as a cause ('illa) that necessitates the ruling; Ash'aris disregard it; Hanafis see it as making the person eligible (ahliyya) for the ruling to attach from God. Then three distinct views are presented: (1) Ash'ari: husn/qubh and rulings are purely Shari. (2) Rationalist (Hanafis and Mu'tazila): both are rational and serve as loci for rulings, but Hanafis do not require punishment for rational evil due to possibility of pardon. (3) Ibn al-Humam: both are rational but do not necessitate or reveal rulings; they are merely qualities. The case of a person on a mountain peak (who never received prophetic call) illustrates the differences: Mu'tazila hold him accountable for all; Hanafi masters hold him accountable for kufr and leaving Iman after reflection, but unclear on other acts; Ash'aris and Ibn al-Humam hold him not accountable at all. Finally, the author notes that the issue can be categorized as kalam (God's justice), usul (command implying goodness), or fiqh (wajib=good, haram=bad), and recommends it be treated under objectives (maqasid) rather than principles (mabadi'). The text then presents two rational proofs for rational husn/qubh: (1) the 'tarjih bila murajjih' argument that if moral values were purely revealed, God's choice would be arbitrary; (2) the argument from mercy that if values were purely divine, revelation would be a calamity, contradicting the Quranic description of it as mercy.

Key Terms
شاهق الجبل (shahiq al-jabal): A person on a mountain peak who has never received any prophetic call; a hypothetical case to test theories of moral epistemology.
استلزام الحكم (istilzam al-hukm): The entailment of a divine ruling by moral goodness or badness; whether the latter necessitates the former.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third position (Ibn al-Humam), what is the relationship between rational goodness/badness and divine rulings?
A) They necessitate the ruling.
B) They disclose the ruling's attachment to the servant.
C) They neither necessitate nor disclose the ruling.
D) They are identical to the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The three positions on husn/qubh lead to different rulings on the accountability of a person unreached by revelation: Ash'ari and Ibn al-Humam deny accountability, while the Hanafi rationalist position affirms it for rationally known evils like kufr.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
The Dispute on Rational vs. Revealed Good and Evil
Exposition of three positions on husn and qubh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the nature of good and evil, explain the Hanafi position, and apply the dispute to the case of the unreached adult.

Core Idea
Good and evil are rational but do not automatically impose divine rulings — revelation alone establishes law.

The nature of good (husn) and evil (qubh) is a foundational usul dispute that impacts how we understand moral accountability before revelation. This passage presents three distinct positions: the Ash'ari view that only revelation defines good and evil, the Mu'tazili view that reason alone can determine and obligate, and the Hanafi middle path that reason knows moral value but does not create legal obligation. Understanding this nuance is critical for correctly deriving rulings in cases where revelation is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools of thought on husn and qubh
The passage presents three positions: Ash'ari (good/evil purely revealed), Mu'tazili (rational and necessitate reward/punishment), and the Hanafi/Ibn al-Humam position (rational but do not necessitate ruling).
The Hanafi position is distinct from Mu'tazili: reason knows but does not compel divine action.
2
Understand the Hanafi position in detail
Reason causes eligibility (ahliyya) for ruling but the ruling itself comes from revelation. The Hanafi view is a middle path.
Imam Fakhr al-Islam: 'al-aql yujib ahliyyat al-hukm' – reason makes the servant eligible, not the ruling itself.
3
Analyze the four rational proofs
The text provides four aqli proofs: 1) Without rationality, actions are equal, making revelation arbitrary. 2) Sending messengers would be a punishment if no prior rationality. 3) Universal agreement on beneficence and ingratitude. 4) Reason's preference for truth over falsehood.
These proofs establish the existence of rational good/evil, not their legal entailment.
4
Examine the Ash'ari objection and Hanafi response
Ash'ari argues that if good/evil were intrinsic, lying could never become good, but it does to save a prophet. Hanafi response: lying remains intrinsically evil; it is only accidentally good as a lesser evil.
This is the principle of 'necessities permit prohibitions' – goodness by another (bi'l-ghayr) does not negate intrinsic evil.
5
Apply to the branch case of the unreached adult
The thamarah al-khilaf: Ash'ari and Ibn al-Humam say no accountability; Mu'tazili says full accountability; Hanafi mashayikh say accountable for kufr and leaving faith after reflection, but other acts are unclear.
This case tests the practical consequence of each position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو كان خلاف لكان في تعيين ذلك البعض من الأحكام والظاهر من كلماتهم أن ذلك البعض هو الإيمان والشكر ونحوهما، وعند المعتزلة كثير، ويفهم من كلام الإمام فخر الإسلام أن حاصل النزاع بيننا وبينهم أن العقل عندهم علة موجبة للحكم، وعند الأشعرية مهدرة لا اعتبار لها، وعندنا لا هذا ولا ذاك، بل العقل يوجب أهلية الحكم وتعلق الحكم من العليم الخبير، والنزاع هكذا لا يليق أن يقع بين أهل الإسلام، لما مر أن إجماع المسلمين على أن لا حكم إلا الله تعالى فخرج حاصل البحث أن ههنا ثلاثة أقوال :
الأول : مذهب الأشعرية أن الحسن والقبح في الأفعال شرعي، وكذلك الحكم. الثاني : أنهما عقليان وهما مناطان لتعلق الحكم، فإذا أدرك في بعض الأفعال كالإيمان والكفر والشكر والكفران يتعلق الحكم منه تعالى بذمة العبد وهو مذهب هؤلاء الكرام والمعتزلة إلا أنه عندنا لا تجب العقوبة بحسب القبح العقلي كما لا تجب بعد ورود الشرع لاحتمال العفو بخلاف هؤلاء بناء على وجوب العدل عندهم بمعنى إيصال الثواب إلى من أتى بالحسنات، وإيصال العقاب إلى من أتى بالقبائح.
(6
الثالث: أن الحسن والقبح عقليان وليسا موجبين للحكم ولا كاشفين عن تعلقه بذمة العبد، وهو مختار الشيخ ابن الهمام صاحب التحرير، وتبعه المصنف، ورأيت في بعض الكتب وجدت مشايخنا الذين لاقيتهم قائلين مثل قول الأشعرية وبما حررنا من المذاهب يتفرع عليه مسألة البالغ في شاهق (الجبل) أي الذي لم تبلغه الدعوة، فعند المعتزلة مؤاخذ بترك الحسنات وفعل القبائح ومثاب بالحسنات وعند هؤلاء المشايخ يؤاخذ بإتيان الكفر مطلقاً وبترك الإيمان عند مضي مدة التأمل والمؤاخذة بترك ما سوى الإيمان وأمثاله من الشكر لم يعلم حالها برواية صريحة بأنهم هل يعذرون بعدم درك العقل إياها للدلائل أم لا، وعند الأشعرية والشيخ ابن الهمام لا يؤاخذون ولو أتوا بالشرك والعياذ بالله تعالى. ثم اعلم أن مسألة الحسن والقبح وكذا استلزامهما للحكم يمكن أن تكون كلامية راجعة إلى أن الله تعالى لا يحكم إلا بما هو حسن أو قبيح، وإن حكم الله ملزومهما، وأن تكون أصولية راجعة إلى أن الأمر الإلهي يدل على الحسن اقتضاء والنهي الإلهي يدل على القبح كذلك، وأن تكون فقهية راجعة إلى أن الفعل الواجب يكون حسناً والحرام قبيحاً، فقد بان أن الأولى أن تسرد في المقاصد دون المبادىء (لنا) في إثبات نفس الحسن والقبح العقليين أعم من استلزامهما الحكم، أوّلاً أنه لو كانا لكانت الصلاة والزنا متساويين في نفس الأمر قبل بعثة الرسل، فجعل أحدهما واجباً والآخر حراماً ليس أولى من العكس، وهو ترجيح من غير مرجح مناف لحكمة الأمر وهو حكيم البتة قطعاً. ولنا فيه ثانياً لو كانا شرعيين لكان إرسال الرسل بلاء وفتنة لا رحمة، لأنهم كانوا قبل ذلك في رفاهية لعدم صحة المؤاخذة بشيء مما يستلذه الإنسان ثم بعد مجيء الرسل صاروا ببعض تلك الأفاعيل في عذاب أبدي، فأي فائدة في إرسال الرسل إلا التضييق وتعذيب عباده، فصار بلاء هذا خلف لأنه رحمة يمنّ الله تعالى به على عباده في كثير من مواضع تنزيله واعلم أن هذا الدليل كما يدل على الحسن والقبح العقليين كذلك يدل على أن وجوب الإيمان وحرمة الكفر أيضاً عقلي لأنه لو كان الكافر قبل بلوغ الدعوة معذوراً لكان بعثة الرسل في حقه بلاء، هذا ظاهر جداً فافهم ولنا فيه ثالثاً أن حسن الإحسان وقبح مقابلته بالإساءة مما اتفق عليه العقلاء حتى من لا يقول بإرسال الرسل كالبراهمة فلولا أنه ذاتي) أي غير متوقف على الشرع (لم يكن كذلك أي لما اتفق عليه والجواب بأنه يمنع لذات (الفعل) وليس كذلك بل الدعوى عدم التوقف على الشرع) سواء كان بالذات أو بالعرض ( ومنع الاتفاق على أنه مناط حكمه (تعالى) أي التقريب غير تام لأنه لا اتفاق على كونهما مناطاً للحكم (لا يمسنا) هذا المنع والكذب في المقصود آثر العقل الصدق) فلولا أن حسنه ذاتي لما آثر وفيه أنه إن أراد الاستواء في المقصود مع حصول جميع الأغراض وموافقة الجبلة فنقول (لا استواء في نفس الأمر، لأن لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض) متغايرة فهو تقدير مستحيل، فيمنع الإيثار على ذلك التقدير) وإن أراد الاستواء في مقصود معين فلا يلزم منه ذاتية الحسن، لجواز أن يكون الإيثار لمرجح آخر ، ولا أقل أن يكون ذلك هو الاعتياد، وبما قررنا ظهر لك اندفاع أنه لا توجه له بالدليل، فإنه إنما أخذ الاستواء نظراً إلى المقصود دون جميع العوارض واللوازم وتحققه يقيني ثم إن هاتين الحجتين مع عن الدلالة على كلية المطلوب لا تخلوان عن نوع خطابة، إذ لقائل أن به الثواب فافهم. الأشعرية (قالوا لنفي عقلية الحسن والقبح.
:أولاً : لو كان كل منهما ذاتياً لم يتخلف فإن ما بالذات لا يبطل (وقد تخلف، فإن الكذب مثلاً يجب لعصمة نبي) عن يد ظالم (وإنقاذ بريء عن سفاك) فصار حسناً وقد كان قبيحاً (والجواب) أنا لا نسلم أنه تخلف ههنا بل الكذب باق على قبحه، والوجوب جاء للاجتناب عن أعظم منه قبحاً، فحينئذ ههنا ارتكاب أقل (القبيحين) الكذب وهلاك نبي أو بريء (لا) أن الكذب صار حسناً قيل :) في حواشي ميرزاجان يرد عليه أن هذا الكذب واجب) وكل واجب حسن (فيدخل في حد (الحسن) والحسن لا يكون عند الخصم إلا ذاتياً، فهذا الحسن ذاتي فلا يجامع القبح، وبما قررنا بأن بطلان هذا الإيراد من غير ارتياب (أقول) في دفعه ليس ههنا حسن الكذب بالذات بل بواسطة حسن إنقاذ نبي بالعرض و الحسن بالغير لا ينافي القبح لذاته، وهذا معنى قولهم : الضرورات تبيح المحظورات أي لأجل عروض ضرورة يجيء فيه الحسن بواسطة دفعها فيعامل معاملة المباح غاية الأمر أنه يلزم القول بأن كلا منهما كما أنه) يكون بالذات، كذلك الغير، ولعلهم يلتزمونه فلا يسلمون أن كلاً حسن بالذات، تحقيقه أن
If there were disagreement, it would be about specifying that part of the rulings. The apparent meaning of their words is that that part is faith, gratitude and the like. According to the Mu'tazila it is many. It is understood from the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam that the essence of the dispute between us and them is that reason according to them is a cause necessitating the ruling, while according to the Ash'arites it is disregarded and has no consideration. According to us it is neither this nor that, rather reason necessitates the eligibility for ruling and the attachment of the ruling from the All-Knowing, All-Aware. Dispute like this is not fit to occur among the people of Islam, because it has passed that the consensus of the Muslims is that there is no ruling except from Allah. So the outcome of the investigation is that there are three statements here: First: The school of the Ash'arites is that good and evil in actions are revealed, and likewise the ruling. Second: That they are rational and they are the loci for attaching the ruling. So when it is perceived in some actions like faith, disbelief, gratitude, and ingratitude, the ruling from Him Almighty attaches to the conscience of the servant. This is the school of these noble ones and the Mu'tazila. However, according to us, punishment is not obligatory according to rational evil, just as it is not obligatory after the advent of revelation due to the possibility of pardon, contrary to them based on their view of the obligation of justice in the sense of bestowing reward on those who perform good deeds and inflicting punishment on those who commit evil deeds. Third: That good and evil are rational but they are not necessitators of the ruling nor revealers of its attachment to the conscience of the servant. This is the choice of Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, the author of al-Tahrir, and the author (of this book) followed him. I saw in some books that I found our mashayikh whom I met, saying like the saying of the Ash'arites. Based on what we have recorded of the schools, the issue of the adult on a mountain peak (i.e., who has not received the call) branches from it. According to the Mu'tazila, he is taken to task for abandoning good deeds and doing evil deeds, and is rewarded for good deeds. According to these mashayikh, he is taken to task for committing disbelief absolutely and for abandoning faith after the passage of a period of reflection. The taking to task for abandoning other than faith and its likes such as gratitude is not known by a clear report whether they are excused due to the lack of reason grasping them for the evidences or not. According to the Ash'arites and Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, they are not taken to task even if they committed polytheism, God forbid. Then know that the issue of good and evil and their entailment of the ruling can be theological (kalamiyah) reverting to the fact that Allah does not rule except by what is good or evil, and that Allah's ruling is entailed by them; and it can be usuli reverting to the fact that the divine command indicates good as a requirement and the divine prohibition indicates evil likewise; and it can be fiqhi reverting to the fact that the obligatory act is good and the forbidden is evil. So it has become clear that the first should be included in the objectives (maqasid) not the preliminaries. For us in proving rational good and evil themselves, regardless of their entailment of the ruling: Firstly, that if they were (both) the same, then prayer and adultery would be equal in themselves before the sending of messengers, so making one obligatory and the other forbidden would not be more appropriate than the opposite, which is an arbitrary preference without a preponderant, contrary to the wisdom of the Commander, and He is absolutely wise for sure. Secondly, if they were revealed, sending messengers would be a trial and tribulation, not mercy, because before that they were at ease due to the invalidity of being held accountable for anything that pleases a person, then after the coming of messengers they became, due to some of those acts, in eternal punishment. So what benefit is there in sending messengers except restriction and tormenting His servants, making it a trial. This is contrary to the fact that it is a mercy that Allah Almighty bestows upon His servants in many places of His revelation. And know that this proof, just as it indicates rational good and evil, also indicates that the obligation of faith and prohibition of disbelief are also rational, because if the disbeliever before receiving the call were excused, then sending messengers would be a trial for him. This is very apparent, so understand. Thirdly, that the goodness of beneficence and the evil of responding to it with harm is agreed upon by rational people, even those who do not believe in sending messengers like the Barahima. So if it were not intrinsic (i.e., not dependent on revelation), it would not be agreed upon. The answer is that it is not conceded that the agreement is due to the intrinsic nature of good and evil, but rather it is permissible that their judgment of them is for a general interest, which does not harm us, because observance of general interest at that time is good by necessity, otherwise beneficence would not become good because of it. It would harm us only if we claimed that goodness is essential to the act, which we do not; rather the claim is non-dependence on revelation, whether essential or accidental. And the objection that agreement is not complete on it being the locus of His (Almighty's) ruling, i.e., that it is not a complete basis because there is no agreement on them being the locus of the ruling, does not affect us, because we do not say that they entail a ruling from Him Almighty; rather that is by revelation, and the proof was only brought to prove the rationality of good and evil. Fourthly, what he mentioned, changing the style indicating doubt by his saying: 'And he argued that if truth and falsehood are equal in the intended purpose, reason prefers truth.' So if its goodness were not intrinsic, it would not be preferred. The objection: If he means equality in the intended purpose with the attainment of all goals and conformity of nature, then we say there is no equality in reality, because each has differing necessities and accidents; it is an impossible estimation, so preference is denied upon that estimation. If he means equality in a specific intended purpose, then essential goodness does not necessarily follow from it, because it is possible that preference is for another preponderant, at the very least that is habit. With what we have recorded, it is clear that the objection is not directed at him, for he considered equality with respect to the intended purpose without all accidents and necessities, and its verification is certain. Then these two proofs, despite indicating the generality of the sought, are not devoid of a certain rhetorical nature, for a speaker may say: It is possible that agreement on the goodness of beneficence and the evil of responding to it with harm is in the sense of them being a perfection attribute for human reality and a deficiency attribute, not in the disputed sense. Likewise, preferring truth is because it is a perfection attribute, not because it deserves reward. So understand. The Ash'arites said to negate the rationality of good and evil: Firstly, if each were intrinsic it would not change; for what is essential cannot cease. Yet it has changed, for example, lying is obligatory to protect a prophet from the hand of a tyrant and to save an innocent from a bloodshedder, so it becomes good after being evil. The response: We do not concede that it changed here; rather lying remains evil, and the obligation came to avoid something more evil. So here there is committing the lesser of two evils (lying and the destruction of a prophet or innocent), not that lying became good. It was said in the marginalia of Mirzajan: It is objected that this lying is obligatory, and every obligatory is good, so it enters the definition of good, and good according to the opponent is only essential. So this good is essential and thus does not coexist with evil. With what we have recorded, the invalidity of this objection is without doubt. I say in response: Here there is no goodness of lying essentially, but rather by means of the goodness of saving a prophet accidentally, and goodness by another does not contradict essential evil. This is the meaning of their saying: Necessities permit prohibitions, i.e., due to the occurrence of necessity, goodness comes through it by means of repelling it, so it is treated like the permissible. The upshot is that it requires saying that each, just as it is essential, also is accidental. Perhaps they would adhere to it, so they would not concede that each is essentially good. Its verification is that...

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the nature of good (husn) and evil (qubh) in actions—whether they are rational (aqli) or dependent on revelation (shar'i). The text opens by contextualizing the debate: the Ash'arites hold that good and evil are entirely defined by divine law; actions are not inherently good or evil. The Mu'tazilites hold that they are rational and that reason alone can determine moral value, and that this rational value directly necessitates divine reward or punishment. The author then presents a third position, favored by Ibn al-Humam and the author himself, that good and evil are indeed rational (innate to reason) but they do not automatically create a legal obligation or prohibition; rather, the divine ruling (hukm) is only known through revelation. This third view is distinct: reason knows moral value, but law comes from revelation. The text then derives a branch issue (furu'): the case of a person who reaches adulthood in isolation (on a mountain peak) without having received any prophetic call. According to the Mu'tazila, such a person is accountable for all moral duties. According to the Hanafi mashayikh (the earlier scholars), he is accountable for committing kufr and for failing to believe after due reflection, but accountability for other acts (like gratitude) is unclear. According to the Ash'arites and Ibn al-Humam, he is not accountable at all, even if he commits shirk. The passage then clarifies that the debate can be seen from three angles: theological (kalami), usuli (methodological), and fiqhi. The author then presents four rational proofs (dalil aqli) supporting rational good and evil, without claiming they entail divine rulings. The first proof: if good and evil were not rational, before revelation all acts would be equal, and distinguishing between them would be arbitrary. The second: if they were purely revealed, sending messengers would be a punishment, not a mercy, because people would be comfortable before and tormented after. The third: universal agreement of rational people (including atheist Brahmins) on the goodness of beneficence and evil of ingratitude shows it is innate. The fourth: reason's preference for truth over falsehood (when both achieve the same purpose) indicates rationality. The text then addresses the Ash'arite objection: if good/evil were intrinsic, they could never change, yet we see that lying becomes good to save a prophet. The Hanafi response: lying remains evil inherently, but it becomes permissible (not essentially good) because it avoids a greater evil; it is 'the lesser of two evils'. The marginalia from Mirzajan raises that obligatory lying must be essentially good, but the response clarifies that the goodness is accidental (bi'l-ghayr), not intrinsic (bi'l-dhat), thus not contradicting intrinsic evil. This aligns with the principle 'necessities permit prohibitions'.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): The quality of an action that makes it praiseworthy; here debated as intrinsic (dhati) or revealed (shar'i).
القبح (al-qubh): The quality of an action that makes it blameworthy; likewise debated.
البالغ في شاهق الجبل (al-baligh fi shahiq al-jabal): A person who reaches adulthood isolated on a mountain without ever receiving prophetic call (da'wah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on the nature of good (husn) and evil (qubh)?
A) They are purely revealed and have no rational basis.
B) They are rational and directly necessitate divine reward or punishment.
C) They are rational but do not inherently cause a divine legal ruling; rulings come from revelation.
D) They are neither rational nor revealed; God arbitrarily assigns them.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that good and evil are rationally known, but legal rulings (ahkam) are derived exclusively from divine revelation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Rationality of Good and Evil: Mu'tazila vs Ash'ariyya Dispute
Dispute on whether good/evil are intrinsically rational or revelation-dependent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Mu'tazili and Ash'ari/Hanafi positions on rational good and evil and apply the Hanafi critique to arguments for intrinsic moral values.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not purely rational; revelation determines legal obligation.

This passage dives into one of the most debated issues in usul al-fiqh: can human reason independently know that an act is good or evil? The Mu'tazila argue yes, citing universal agreement on beneficence and the intellect's preference for truth. The Ash'ariyya, with whom the Hanafis align, counter that such agreements may stem from habit or public interest, and that acts like lying can change their moral standing due to contingent circumstances without altering their intrinsic nature. Understanding this dispute is key to grasping the Hanafi position on the role of reason in law.

Guided Path
1
State the Mu'tazili arguments
Identify the two main proofs: (1) universal rational agreement on goodness of beneficence and evil of harm, and (2) intellect's preference for truth over falsehood when consequences are equal.
2
Analyze the Ash'ari/Hanafi rebuttal
The commentator counters argument 1: agreement may be due to public interest (maslaha) not intrinsic nature. For argument 2: equality of consequences is unrealistic; preference could be habitual.
Note the commentator's use of 'it is possible' (jāza) to show these are not definitive proofs.
3
Apply the intrinsic/accidental distinction
Examine the example of lying to save a prophet. The commentator insists lying remains intrinsically evil (qabīḥ bi al-dhāt) but becomes permissible due to a greater accidental good. Distinguish between dhātī (intrinsic) and ʿaraḍī (accidental).
This distinction is central to the Hanafi/Ash'ari position.
4
Extend to the problem of abrogation (naskh)
Understand how the distinction explains changes in rulings: e.g., marriage to one's sister was permitted due to the accidental good of preserving progeny; when that good ceased, the intrinsic evil reasserted itself, making it forbidden.
The Mu'tazila struggle with this because they cannot conceive of an act changing moral status without changing its essence.
5
Evaluate coherence
Assess the Hanafi/Ash'ari position: it preserves the objectivity of moral values while allowing flexibility for contexts like necessity and abrogation. Compare with the Mu'tazili position.
Consider the practical implications for fiqh rulings.

الإيمان وحرمة الكفر أيضاً ،عقلي لأنه لو كان الكافر قبل بلوغ الدعوة معذوراً لكان بعثة الرسل في حقه بلاء، هذا ظاهر جداً فافهم ولنا فيه ثالثاً أن حسن الإحسان وقبح مقابلته بالإساءة مما اتفق عليه العقلاء حتى من لا يقول بإرسال الرسل كالبراهمة فلولا أنه ذاتي) أي غير متوقف على الشرع (لم يكن كذلك أي لما اتفق عليه والجواب بأنه يمنع كون الاتفاق لأجل ذاتية الحسن والقبح بل (يجوز أن يكون حكمهم بهما لمصلحة عامة لا يضرنا لأن رعاية المصلحة العامة) حينئذ (حسن بالضرورة وإلا لما صار الإحسان لأجلها حسناً وإنما يضرنا لو ادعينا أنه) أي الحسن لذات (الفعل) وليس كذلك بل الدعوى عدم التوقف على الشرع) سواء كان بالذات أو بالعرض ( ومنع الاتفاق على أنه مناط حكمه (تعالى) أي التقريب غير تام لأنه لا اتفاق على كونهما مناطاً للحكم (لا يمسنا) هذا المنع فإنا لا نقول باستلزامه حكماً منه تعالى، بل ذلك بالسمع ولم يورد الدليل إلا لإثبات نفس عقلية الحسن والقبح، ولنا رابعاً ما أورده مغيراً للأسلوب إشارة إلى التمريض بقوله : ( واستدل بأنه إذا استوى الصدق والكذب في المقصود آثر العقل الصدق) فلولا أن حسنه ذاتي لما آثر وفيه أنه إن أراد الاستواء في المقصود مع حصول جميع الأغراض وموافقة الجبلة فنقول (لا استواء في نفس الأمر، لأن لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض) متغايرة فهو تقدير مستحيل، فيمنع الإيثار على ذلك التقدير) وإن أراد الاستواء في مقصود معين فلا يلزم منه ذاتية الحسن، لجواز أن يكون الإيثار لمرجح آخر ، ولا أقل أن يكون ذلك هو الاعتياد، وبما قررنا ظهر لك اندفاع أنه لا توجه له بالدليل، فإنه إنما أخذ الاستواء نظراً إلى المقصود دون جميع العوارض واللوازم وتحققه يقيني ثم إن هاتين الحجتين مع عن الدلالة على كلية المطلوب لا تخلوان عن نوع خطابة، إذ لقائل أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون الاتفاق على حسن الإحسان وقبح الإساءة في مقابلته بمعنى كونها صفة كمالية للحقيقة الإنسانية، وصفة نقص لا بالمعنى المتنازع فيه وكذا إيثار الصدق أيضاً، لكونه صفة

بخلاف الثاني، فلعل المعتزلة أرادوا بكون الحسن والقبح مقتضى الذات المعنى الأول، فلا يعد في التخلف في بعض المواضع لعروض عارض، فحينئذ نقول: الكذب ولو كان قبيحاً بالذات لكن استلزامه الحسن بالذات عاقه وأزال عنه القبح فصار حسناً بحسن ملزومه، ولا يلزم اجتماع الحسن والقبح في ذات ،واحدة لكن على هذا لا يبقى بين قولهم وبين قول الجبائي كبير فرق، هكذا ينبغي أن تفهم هذه المباحث (وبه) أي بما ذكرنا أن الحسن الذاتي لا ينافي القبح الغيري أمكن لهم التخلص عن النسخ فإنه لما جاز أن يكون الحسن بالذات قبيحاً بالغير والقبيح بالغير حسناً بالذات أمكن انقلاب الوجوب إلى الحرمة والحرمة إلى الوجوب، وعلى هذا فنكاح الأخت كان قبيحاً بالذات حسناً بحسن إبقاء النسل، فكان مباحاً، والآن لما زال استلزامه لذلك الحسن بقي على قبحه فصار حراماً، وهذا لا يصح على التوجيه الأوّل إذ يلزم حينئذ كون نكاح الأخت حراماً ومباحاً والتزامه بعيد كيف وقد بقي مباحاً إلى مجيء شريعة أخرى مع ارتفاع الضرورة قبله ؛ وأبعد منه التزام ما يلتزم في التوجه إلى البيت المقدس والكعبة، فإنه يلزم أن يكون أحدهما حراماً وواجباً ولا يجترى عليه مسلم، وأما على التوجيه الثاني فلا بعد في نكاح الأخت، فإنه يجوز أن يكون القبح مقتضى الذات، لكن المانع المذكور أزاله وجعله حسناً، لكن التزام هذا في الكعبة والبيت مشكل، فإن التوجه إلى الكعبة كان مستمراً في شريعة والآن مستمر في شريعة أفضل البشر عليه وعلى آله أفضل الصلاة والسلام والتوجه إلى البيت كان مستمراً في شريعة موسى فكيف يجترىء مسلم على القول باستمرار وجوب أمر مقتضاه القبح بالذات من غير بيان المانع المستمر، بل الذي يجب أن يعتقد أن التوجه إلى البيت كان حسناً بحسن عارض بالذات أي من غير واسطة في العروض والتوجه إلى الكعبة كان مانعاً عنه فصار قبيحاً بالعرض، ثم زال حسنه عند هذه الشريعة الغراء، وبقي غير حسن كما كان قبل وحسن التوجه إلى الكعبة مجيء
The Second Article on Rulings. Faith and the prohibition of disbelief are also rational, because if the disbeliever before the arrival of the call were excused, then sending messengers would be a calamity for him. This is very clear, so understand. And we have a third argument: that the goodness of beneficence and the evil of responding with harm is something agreed upon by rational people, even those who do not believe in sending messengers like the Barahima. If it were not intrinsic (i.e., not dependent on revelation) it would not be so (i.e., agreed upon). The answer is that it is denied that the agreement is due to the intrinsic nature of good and evil; rather, it is possible that their judgment of them is for a public interest, which does not harm us, because observing the public interest is then necessarily good, otherwise beneficence would not become good for its sake. It would only harm us if we claimed that it (goodness) is for the essence of the act, which is not the case; rather, the claim is non-dependence on revelation, whether intrinsically or accidentally. And the denial that agreement is the criterion of His (exalted) judgment—i.e., the approximation is not complete because there is no agreement that they are the criterion for judgment—does not touch us, for we do not say that it necessitates a judgment from Him, but that is by revelation, and the proof was only brought to establish the rational nature of good and evil. And we have a fourth argument: what he presented changing the style, indicating weakness, by saying: "He argued that if truth and falsehood are equal in purpose, the intellect prefers truth." If its goodness were not intrinsic, it would not prefer. It contains that if he means equality in purpose with the attainment of all objectives and conformity to nature, then we say: there is no real equality because each has differing implications and accidents; it is an impossible estimation, so preference is prevented on that estimation. If he means equality in a specific purpose, then the intrinsic nature of goodness does not follow, because preference could be for another preponderant, and at least that could be habit. By what we have established, it becomes clear to you that the objection that he has no proof is repelled; he only assumed equality considering the purpose without all accidents and implications, and its realization is certain. Then these two proofs, together with [؟] indicating the universality of the desired, are not free from a type of rhetoric. For one could say: it is possible that the agreement on the goodness of beneficence and the evil of responding harmfully is in the sense of them being a perfection attribute of the human essence and an imperfection attribute, not in the disputed sense. Similarly, the preference for truth is because it is an attribute of perfection, not because it deserves reward through it. So understand. The Ash'ariyya said to negate the rationality of good and evil: First: if each were intrinsic, it would not change, for what is intrinsic does not become invalid. Yet it has changed, for lying, for example, becomes obligatory to protect a prophet from a tyrant and to save an innocent from a murderer, so it becomes good after being evil. The answer: we do not concede that it changed here; rather, lying remains on its evilness, and the obligation came to avoid a greater evil. So here there is commission of the lesser of two evils (lying vs. death of a prophet or innocent) not that lying became good. It was said in the glosses of Mirzajan: it is objected that this lying is obligatory, and every obligatory act is good, so it enters the definition of good, and good is only intrinsic according to the opponent, so this good is intrinsic and thus incompatible with evil. By what we have established, the invalidity of this objection is beyond doubt. I say in its refutation: there is no goodness of lying by itself, but rather through the accidental goodness of saving a prophet, and accidental goodness does not negate essential evil. This is the meaning of their saying: "Necessities permit prohibitions," i.e., due to the occurrence of a necessity, goodness enters through its removal, so it is treated like permissible. The most that follows is that one must say that each of them, just as it is intrinsic, so too accidental, and perhaps they commit to it and do not concede that each is intrinsically good. Its verification: the occurrence of an attribute may be real for something, and may be real for another thing related to it by some connection, so it is attributed to this accidentally. In other disciplines, it is said to be described via mediation in occurrence. Here lying is intrinsically evil and entails an intrinsic good (protection of a prophet) and removes another evil (death of a prophet) whose evilness exceeds this lying. The coexistence of accidental with intrinsic is actual and no impossibility. So it is not objected that good and evil for them were for the essence of the act; how then is it removed by an accidental occurrence? And if not removed, then contradiction would arise. So ponder. Then it follows from this that this lying is forbidden and obligatory from two aspects, due to the persistence of evil with the occurrence of good. This is acceptable to us, but not possible from the Mu'tazila, for they do not allow the coexistence of obligation and prohibition in one thing, even prohibiting prayer in usurped land. Rather, they do not allow their coexistence even in the same genus. This [؟] except with difficulty. This is a comprehensive answer. And it can be formulated as follows: what is meant by 'the requirement of the essence' may be what, if the thing were left to its nature, would entail it, as coldness is intrinsic to water; or what the essence entails necessarily, like evenness for four. The first can be overridden by an accidental occurrence, as water heats up near fire. Unlike the second. Perhaps the Mu'tazila meant by goodness and evil being a requirement of the essence the first meaning, so it is not considered a change in some cases due to an accidental occurrence. Then we say: lying, even if intrinsically evil, its entailment of intrinsic good impedes and removes evilness, so it becomes good through the goodness of its consequent, and there is no need for coexistence of good and evil in one essence. But on this, there remains no great difference between their statement and the statement of al-Jubba'i. Thus you should understand these investigations. And by this (i.e., what we mentioned that intrinsic good does not contradict accidental evil), they can extricate themselves from the issue of abrogation, because since it is possible for what is intrinsically good to be accidentally evil and what is accidentally evil to be intrinsically good, it is possible for obligation to change to prohibition and prohibition to obligation. On this, marriage to one's sister was intrinsically evil, good by the goodness of preserving progeny, so it was permissible. Now, when its entailment of that good ceased, it remained on its evilness and became forbidden. This is not valid on the first interpretation, for then marriage to sister would be forbidden and permissible, and committing to it is far-fetched, especially since it remained permissible until another law came despite the removal of necessity before it. More far-fetched is what is committed regarding facing the Bayt al-Maqdis and the Ka'ba, for it would necessitate that one of them is forbidden and obligatory, and no Muslim would dare to say that. As for the second interpretation, there is no problem with marriage to sister, for it is possible that evil is a requirement of the essence, but the aforementioned impediment removed it and made it good. However, committing to this regarding the Ka'ba and the Bayt is problematic, for facing the Ka'ba was continuous in one law and now continuous in the law of the best of mankind (upon him and his family be the best of prayers and peace), and facing the Bayt was continuous in the law of Moses. How can a Muslim dare to say the continuation of obligation of a matter whose requirement is intrinsic evil without explaining a continuous impediment? Rather, what must be believed is that facing the Bayt was good by an accidental good intrinsically (i.e., without mediation in occurrence), and facing the Ka'ba was an impediment to it, so it became evil accidentally. Then its goodness ceased at this radiant law, and it remained not good as before, and the goodness of facing the Ka'ba came.

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed theological and legal dispute between the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya (with the Hanafi commentator inclining towards the Ash'ari position) on whether good (husn) and evil (qubh) are intrinsically discernible by reason or known only through revelation. The Mu'tazila argue that human reason can independently determine the moral value of acts, as evidenced by universal rational agreement on the goodness of beneficence and evil of harm, and by the intellect's preference for truth over falsehood when consequences are equal. The Ash'ariyya counter by pointing out that lying, though generally evil, becomes obligatory in cases like protecting a prophet, so its moral value changes; if it were intrinsically evil, it should always be evil. The commentator refines the Ash'ari response by distinguishing between intrinsic (dhati) and accidental (aradi) attributes: lying remains intrinsically evil, but a greater good (saving a prophet) makes it permissible by way of choosing the lesser evil. This distinction allows the commentator to reconcile apparent contradictions without abandoning the intrinsic nature of good and evil. The discussion extends to the problem of abrogation (naskh): how can an act that was once good (e.g., marriage to a sister in early sharia) become forbidden later? The commentator argues that the act has an intrinsic quality (e.g., marriage to sister is intrinsically evil) but was temporarily rendered good by a contingent benefit (preserving progeny); once that benefit ceases, the intrinsic evil reasserts itself. This avoids the Mu'tazilite difficulty of positing that the act itself changes moral value. The commentator also applies this logic to changes in the direction of prayer (qibla). Overall, the passage is a sophisticated exercise in usul al-fiqh, demonstrating the Hanafi commitment to revelation as the ultimate source of obligation while acknowledging a limited role for reason in discerning secondary moral qualities.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): Goodness or moral rightness of an act; can be intrinsic (dhāti) or accidental (ʿaraḍī).
القبح (al-qubh): Evil or moral wrongness of an act; can be intrinsic or accidental.
ذاتي (dhāti): Intrinsic, belonging to the essence of something
عرضي (ʿaraḍī): Accidental, contingent quality that can change without altering the essence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentator, what happens to the intrinsic evil of lying when a prophet's life is at stake?
A) The lying becomes intrinsically good.
B) The lying remains intrinsically evil but becomes permissible to avoid a greater evil.
C) The lying becomes both good and evil at the same time.
D) The lying loses all moral quality.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi/Ash'ari distinction between intrinsic (dhati) and accidental (aradi) qualities allows for a coherent account of moral change without undermining the objectivity of moral values.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Liar Paradox and Essential Good/Evil: A Hanafi Usul Refutation
Refuting the Mu'tazila liar paradox argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Mu'tazila liar paradox argument against essential good/evil and explain the Hanafi response using the distinction between essential and accidental ruling transfer.

Core Idea
The ruling of a necessary concomitant does not transfer essentially; only accidentally.

The Mu'tazila argued that if good and evil are essential attributes of acts, then the statement 'I will surely lie tomorrow' leads to a contradiction: its truth (good) entails lying (evil), and its falsehood (not good) entails not lying (good), making truth both good and evil. The Hanafi response, rooted in usul al-fiqh reasoning, distinguishes between essential and accidental qualities. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its significance for the theory of moral value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
State the objection clearly: truth of 'I will surely lie tomorrow' entails lying (evil), falsehood entails not lying (good). Thus truth would be both good and evil, a contradiction.
Focus on their assumption that the ruling of the concomitant transfers identically.
2
Understand the Hanafi refutation
The Hanafi response rejects that the ruling transfers essentially. It may transfer accidentally. Example: a messenger causing destruction—the act is good essentially despite accidental evil.
Key: the malzum (concomitant) does not carry the lazim's (necessary) ruling essentially.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Distinguish between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) goodness/evil. Truth is essentially good (correspondence), but may accidentally entail evil. No contradiction.
The distinction allows one thing to be both good and evil under different aspects.
4
Consider the two-aspects counterargument
Even if truth were identical to its referent, 'I will surely lie tomorrow' can be viewed as realized (good) or not realized (evil). Two considerations avoid contradiction.
This rebuts the Mu'tazila assumption that one thing cannot be both wajib and haram even by two aspects.

و) قالوا : أنه وليس من ثانياً : (لو كان كل منهما ذاتياً لاجتمع النقيضان في مثل لأكذبن غداً، فإن صدقه يستلزم الكذب) في الغد الذي هو المحكي عنه (وبالعكس) أي كذبه يستلزم عدم الكذب في الغد فصدقه ملزوم الكذب القبيح بالذات وكذبه ملزوم عدمه الحسن بالذات وللملزوم حكم اللازم) فيكون صدقه قبيحاً مع كونه حسناً، وكذبه حسناً مع كونه قبيحاً، ولا ينقلب عليهم بأنه يلزم عليهم أن يكون صدقه حسناً شرعياً وقبيحاً شرعياً، وهما ضدان فأين المفر، لأن لهم أن يقولوا يجوز أن يبطل أحدهما، فإنه بجعل الشارع، بخلاف المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون إنهما لذات الفعل فتأمل فيه (وربما يمنع ذلك أي كون حكم الملزوم حكم اللازم بعينه بالذات (ألا ترى أن المفضي إلى الشر لا يكون شراً بالذات كيف ومجيء الرسول موجب لهلاك الناس الكثير مع خير كثير أعظم (قال) (الشيخ أبو علي في الإشارات: الشر داخل في القدر بالعرض) فإن التقدير الإلهي إنما تعلق أولاً وبالذات بالخير، لكنه قد كان متوقفاً على وجود الشر القليل، شأن الحكيم أن يترك الخير الكثير لأجل الشر القليل، فلذا قدر الشر وأوجده هذا، ثم لا يخفى على المتأدب بالآداب الشرعية أن الصواب ترك التأييد بكلام ابن سينا، فإنه ليس من رجال هذا المقال أقول هذا الجواب يرشدك إلى الالتزام المذكور سابقاً) من أنهما كما يكونان بالذات يكونان بالغير، فإن حسن الملزوم وإن لم يكن مستلزماً لحكم اللازم بالذات لكنه مستلزم له بالعرض البتة، وكذا قبحه يستلزم قبحه بالعرض (فافهم) فإنه لا سترة فيه، وقد يقال في تقرير الدليل أن صدق لأكذبن غداً هو نفس تحقق مصداقه الذي هو الكذب في الغد وهو قبيح بالذات عندهم فالصدق قبيح مع كونه حسناً، فحينئذ لا يتوجه هذا الجواب أصلاً، وفيه أنا لا نسلم أن الصدق نفس تحقق المصداق، بل المطابقة للمصداق الواقعي، فواقعية المصداق لازمة له لا أنه هي، ولو سلم فلأكذبن الغد اعتباران اعتبار أنه تحقق مصداق الخبر، واعتبار أن مصداقه غير متحقق قلنا إن نقول أنه بالاعتبار الأوّل حسن بالذات، وبالاعتبار الثاني قبيح، فلا ضير وهذا عندنا ظاهر، وأما عندهم فللتأمل فيه مجال لعدم قولهم باجتماع الوجوب والحرمة في شيء ما ولو باعتبارين
And they said: secondly: (If each of them were essential, then two contradictories would meet in such as 'I will surely lie tomorrow,' because its truth entails lying) tomorrow, which is the thing reported (and vice versa) i.e., its falsehood entails not lying tomorrow. So its truth is a necessary concomitant of lying which is essentially evil, and its falsehood is a necessary concomitant of its absence which is essentially good. (And the necessary concomitant has the ruling of the necessary) so its truth would be evil while being good, and its falsehood would be good while being evil. And it is not turned against them that it would entail that its truth is legally good and legally evil, which are two opposites, so where is the escape? Because they can say that it is permissible for one of them to be invalidated, since it is by the Lawgiver's making, unlike the Mu'tazila who say that they are for the essence of the act, so reflect on it. (And perhaps this is prevented, i.e., that the ruling of the necessary concomitant is exactly the ruling of the necessary essentially. Do you not see that what leads to evil is not essentially evil? How is it that the coming of a messenger causes the destruction of many people while there is great good? The Shaykh Abu Ali said in al-Isharat: evil enters into decree accidentally. For the divine decree attaches first and essentially to good, but it was dependent on the existence of a little evil, as it is the way of the wise to leave much good for the sake of a little evil, so He decreed evil and caused it. Then it is not hidden from one disciplined with the shar'i disciplines that correctness is to leave supporting the statement of Ibn Sina, for he is not among the men of this discourse. I say: this answer guides you to the commitment mentioned previously) that they are as they are essentially, they are as accidental. For the goodness of the necessary concomitant, even if it is not essentially entailing the ruling of the necessary, yet it entails it accidentally certainly. And similarly, its evil entails its evil accidentally. (So understand) for there is no covering over it. And it may be said in presenting the proof that the truth of 'I will surely lie tomorrow' is the very realization of its referent which is lying tomorrow, and that is essentially evil according to them, so truth is evil while being good. Then this answer does not apply at all. And in it is that we do not accept that truth is the same as the realization of the referent; rather, it is correspondence with the real referent. So the reality of the referent is necessary to it, not that it is it. Even if accepted, then 'I will surely lie tomorrow' has two considerations: one consideration that it is the realization of the referent of the report, and one consideration that its referent is not realized. We say: by the first consideration it is essentially good, and by the second consideration it is evil, so no harm. And this is apparent according to us. As for according to them, there is room for reflection because they do not accept the conjunction of obligation and prohibition in anything, even by two considerations.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazila argument: if good and evil are essential, then the statement 'I will surely lie tomorrow' leads to contradiction, because its truth entails lying (evil) and its falsehood entails not lying (good). Therefore, both truth and falsehood would be both good and evil. The Hanafi response is that the ruling of the necessary concomitant is not essentially the same as the ruling of the necessary; it can be accidentally so. The author cites the example of a messenger whose coming causes destruction but is good overall. He also quotes Abu Ali (Ibn Sina) that evil enters into divine decree accidentally. The Hanafi position is that we can consider two aspects: the truth in itself is good (correspondence), but it may entail evil accidentally. Since the Mu'tazila do not allow one thing to be both obligatory and forbidden even by two considerations, their objection fails.

Key Terms
الملزوم (al-malzum): That which is a necessary concomitant; the antecedent in a conditional relation.
اللازم (al-lazim): The necessary consequence that follows from something.
الذاتي (al-dhati): Essential, pertaining to the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why does the truth of 'I will surely lie tomorrow' not become essentially evil?
A) Because truth is always good, even if it entails evil.
B) Because truth is essentially good, and its entailment of evil is accidental.
C) Because lying is not evil in this context.
D) Because the statement is not a real statement.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between essential and accidental qualities resolves the liar paradox and preserves the theory of essential moral values in usul al-fiqh.

Refuting the Mu'tazila Argument on Determinism
Presenting and refuting the Mu'tazila argument on determinism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Mu'tazila argument that human action is necessary (ijtirari) and explain the Hanafi response affirming moral responsibility through the doctrine of kasb.

Core Idea
If human actions were necessary, reward and punishment would be meaningless — the Hanafi doctrine of kasb preserves moral accountability.

In this passage, we encounter a philosophical argument from the Mu'tazila that aims to deny moral categories by asserting that all human actions are compelled. This challenge to ethical evaluation has deep implications for usul al-fiqh, as it questions the very basis of divine commands. We will dissect the argument and see how the Hanafi school refutes it by distinguishing between God's creation and human acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the opening statement: 'Indeed, the action of the servant is necessary.' What conclusion do they draw from this?
They conclude that actions are not good or evil intellectually.
2
Trace the logical premises
Follow the reasoning: every possible action requires a preponderant cause to exist. Without such a cause, it would not occur. At the moment of existence, existence is preponderant, making the action necessary.
The argument hinges on the principle of sufficient reason.
3
Locate the hidden assumption
The Mu'tazila assume that the preponderant cause eliminates all contingency, thus negating choice. Identify this assumption in the text.
The phrase 'ترجح المرجوح محال' (the outweighing of the outweighed is impossible) shows their commitment to determinism.
4
Formulate the Hanafi counterargument
The Hanafi school rejects the equation of preponderant cause with necessity. They distinguish between God's creation of the action and the human's acquisition (kasb), which involves choice.
This preserves moral responsibility without denying divine omnipotence.
5
Assess the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Consider the stakes: if actions were necessary, divine commands and prohibitions would be unjust, and reward/punishment would be meaningless. The Hanafi view upholds the validity of shar'i obligation.
This debate directly impacts the theory of obligation (wujub) in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) قالوا (ثالثاً : إن فعل العبد اضطراري، فإن الفعل ممكن والممكن ما لم يترجح وجوده على عدمه لا يوجد) فحين الوجود يكون الوجود راجحاً والعدم مرجوحاً وترجح المرجوح محال، فما لم يجب لم يوجد) فالعدم حال ترجيح الوجود محال، فوجود الفعل واجب، فلا اختيار للعبد فيه أصلاً، فهو اضطراري (فلا يكون حسناً ولا قبيحاً) أصلاً (عقلاً إجماعاً) لأن الاضطراري لا يوصف بهما، وهذا التبيان غير متوقف على إبطال الأولوية الغير البالغة حد الوجوب، بخلاف ما في «المختصر حيث قال :
And they said thirdly: Indeed, the action of the servant is necessary. For the action is possible, and what is possible does not exist unless its existence outweighs its non-existence. So at the time of existence, existence is preponderant and non-existence is outweighed, and the outweighing of the outweighed is impossible. So unless it becomes necessary, it does not exist. So non-existence at the time of preponderance of existence is impossible. So the existence of the action is necessary, and there is no choice for the servant in it at all. So it is necessary. (Thus it is not good nor evil) at all (intellectually by consensus) because what is necessary cannot be described by them. And this explanation is not dependent on invalidating preference that has not reached the level of necessity, contrary to what is in al-Mukhtasar where he said:
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the third Mu'tazila argument: human actions are necessary (ijtirari) because every possible event requires a preponderant cause that makes its existence necessary; without necessity, it would not occur. Therefore, human choice is illusory, and actions cannot be morally evaluated. The author notes that this argument differs from a simpler version in 'al-Mukhtasar' that relies on invalidating the concept of preference (tarjih) without necessity. The Hanafi response (implied) is to reject the deterministic premise, as the Hanafi school affirms free will and moral responsibility.

Key Terms
الاضطراري (al-ijtirari): That which is necessary or compelled, with no choice.
الممكن (al-mumkin): The possible, which requires a preponderant cause to exist.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): Preponderance or outweighing, the cause that tips the balance for existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument in this passage, why can human actions not be described as good or evil?
A) Because good and evil are known only by revelation.
B) Because actions are necessary and not freely chosen.
C) Because actions are two types: good and evil.
D) Because humans have no free will in some actions.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument that actions are necessary is refuted by the Hanafi doctrine of kasb, which preserves human choice and moral accountability grounded in revelation.

Essential vs Accidental: Resolving the Liar Paradox in Moral Attributes
Hanafi response to the liar paradox objection against essential good/evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between essential (dhātī) and accidental (ʿaraḍī) attribution to resolve apparent contradictions in rational moral evaluation.

Core Idea
Good and evil can be accidental without being essential—rescuing reason from paradox.

Does an act that necessarily leads to evil become evil itself? The Mu'tazila argued that if good and evil are essential, then statements like 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' become logically impossible. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi/Ash'ari response: the key is distinguishing between what is essential and what is accidental. An act can be good in one respect and evil in another, and the ruling of the entailer is not identical to the ruling of the consequent in essence, only accidentally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's objection
The Mu'tazila claim: if good and evil were essential to actions, then 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' would be both good (if true) and evil (if it entails a lie), a contradiction. Understand their logical structure.
2
Trace the entailment chain
They argue: truth of the statement entails the lie, which is evil by essence; falsehood entails absence of lie, good by essence. Since the ruling of the entailer follows the ruling of the entailment, truth is both good and evil.
3
Distinguish essential vs accidental
The Hanafi response: the ruling of the entailer is not necessarily the same as the ruling of the entailment in essence; it can be accidental. The truth is good essentially (as correspondence), but its relation to the lie is accidental, so no contradiction arises.
Refer to the example of the messenger's coming causing both harm and good: the harm is accidental, not essential.
4
Apply the two considerations
The statement 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' can be considered in two ways: (1) as the act of truthful reporting (good essentially), (2) as referring to a future lie (evil accidentally). The Hanafi position allows these two aspects without contradiction.
The Mu'tazila reject combining obligation and prohibition in one act even by two considerations; this is why they find the solution problematic.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Good and evil are not essential to actions; they can be accidental. Rational evaluation remains possible without essentialism, preserving both reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ذاتياً لاجتمع النقيضان في مثل لأكذبن غداً، فإن صدقه يستلزم الكذب) في الغد الذي هو المحكي عنه (وبالعكس) أي كذبه يستلزم عدم الكذب في الغد فصدقه ملزوم الكذب القبيح بالذات وكذبه ملزوم عدمه الحسن بالذات (فافهم) فإنه لا سترة فيه، وقد يقال في تقرير الدليل أن صدق لأكذبن غداً هو نفس تحقق مصداقه الذي هو الكذب في الغد وهو قبيح بالذات عندهم فالصدق قبيح مع كونه حسناً، فحينئذ لا يتوجه هذا الجواب أصلاً، وفيه
