الإرادة الجزئية إن كانت مؤثرة في الفعل فهو مذهب المعتزلة، وقد نهي عنه، تخصيص جميع
Described thereby: So upon this, creating the capacity of the contingent is necessary, and you had prevented it. So it was said: That intention is from the states (ahwal), neither existent nor non-existent, and they are relational matters whose existences are by their appropriates. So its attribution is not creation, for it is the effusion of existence by essence as for substances and accidents, rather it is causing (ihdath), and causing is not like creation, rather it is easier, because it is for the suitability of matter to accept the act, so it is among the completers of the preparedness of the contingent, which is a type of possibility as established. So there is no harm that the servant's capacity creates this firm intention (qasd musammam). And the texts do not testify except that creation is for Him alone, i.e., the effusion of existence, for it becomes an independent essence unlike relational matters. Do you not see that the rational ones agreed that possibility is not caused? So it is not meant that on the estimation of the compound making chosen by most theologians, the made is the qualification of essence with existence, and existence is a state, so causing is not different from creation. And on the estimation of the simple making, in causing there is also the effusion of the very essence of the state, as in creation, because the making, even if compound, necessitates the reification of the made as an independent essence, unlike states for they have no independent essence; rather they are subsidiary matters, so ponder. And it was said: Rather it is existent, so then it is necessary to specify the firm intention from the general texts of creation with the act, because it is the least that achieves the benefit of creating capacity, since its benefit is that it effects something, and the least is that it effect this intention. And the act of God the Wise is not devoid of its embedded ends, so capacity must have some effect, because it is the least by which the goodness of obligation is directed, for obligation upon the incapable is rendered impossible by reason, and this is the least way of being capable. And this opinion is as if it is an intermediate between Jabr and Tafwid, and the truth is the intermediate between them, as narrated from the eminent Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq, may God be pleased with him and his noble ancestors. The author said: There is something in it. And it is argued that the benefit of creating capacity and the direction of the goodness of obligation necessitate that all acts of servants be specified, but you refused. So specifying the firm intention is a specification without a specifier, and this is insufficient. For their intent, may their secrets be sanctified, is that the benefit of creating capacity and the direction of obligation necessitate that it have some type of effect in voluntary acts, either in their means only, or in both, and the effect in the means is the lesser of them, so we specified it with that. As for voluntary acts, it is not valid because then the general would be entirely invalidated, and that is not permissible, as in His statement: 'And you do not will unless God wills, the Lord of the worlds' (81:29) and 'God created you and what you do' (37:96) and their likes, and the hadiths on the creation of actions. Then in 'al-Nusus' also God indicates that this specification is of ascribing will and action to us, as is not hidden from the fair-minded. So reflect well and do not heed the doubts of the deluders, for truth does not go beyond what I have said. The author said: And in my view, in the emergence of voluntary acts, there must be a universal perception that arouses a universal will, and a particular perception that arouses a particular will. So the servant is free according to the particular bodily perceptions, for the particular will attaches to the act and occurs thereby, and compelled according to the universal intellectual sciences from which the universal will is aroused. So in the arousal of the universal will he is compelled, and in the arousal of the particular will he is free, and this servant does not understand this. For if this particular will is effective in the act, that is the Mu'tazila school, and it is prohibited. Specifying all.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a theological debate on human capacity and intention within the Hanafi-Maturidi framework. The text begins by discussing the nature of intention (qasd musammam) as a 'state' (hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent, a category of relational matters. It argues that creating such an intention is not 'creation' (khalq) which is the effusion of existence, but rather 'causing' (ihdath), which is easier. The purpose is to complete the preparedness of the contingent to receive the act. The author then presents a view that intention is existent, which necessitates specifying it from general texts about creation, as the minimal effect of human capacity. This leads to a discussion of the intermediate position between Jabr (compulsion) and Tafwid (delegation), attributed to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq. The author (al-Musannif) comments with his characteristic 'Fīhi mā fīhi', critiquing the insufficiency of the specification argument. He then presents his own view: voluntary acts require two levels of perception and will—universal and particular. The human is compelled at the universal level (divine will) and free at the particular level (choice), thus reconciling divine omnipotence with human accountability. This avoids the extremes of both the Mu'tazila (who affirm full human power) and the Ash'aris (who deny human efficacy).

Key Terms
القصد المصمم (al-qasd al-musammam): Firm intention that is the minimal effect of human capacity, a type of state (hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent.
الإحداث (al-ihdath): Bringing into existence in a non-essential way, easier than creation (khalq), applied to temporal entities like intentions.
الأحوال (al-ahwal): States, mental or relational attributes that are not independently existent; used to describe intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimal effect of human capacity that justifies the goodness of obligation (taklif)?
A) Creating the entire act independently
B) Effecting the firm intention (qasd musammam) in the means of the act
C) Producing the universal will from intellectual sciences
D) Being able to choose between obedience and disobedience without any divine involvement

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Maturidi doctrine of qasd musammam as a state (hal) caused by the servant reconciles divine omnipotence with human accountability, providing the basis for valid taklif.

Ash'ari Arguments Against Rational Good and Evil: Refutation in Hanafi Usul
Refutation of Ash'ari proofs against rational good and evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ash'ari arguments against rational good and evil and evaluate the Hanafi-Maturidi responses using usul al-fiqh principles.

Core Idea
Obligation by choice is not compulsion; rational evil permits punishment in essence but wisdom may delay it.

This passage presents the fourth and fifth Ash'ari arguments against the concept of rational good and evil, along with the Hanafi-Maturidi responses. The Ash'aris argue that if good and evil were rationally known, God would be compelled to judge accordingly, and punishment would be permissible before revelation. The Hanafi response distinguishes between essential permissibility and actual occurrence, and between obligation by choice and compulsion. By studying this debate, you will understand a key usul al-fiqh dispute about the role of reason in moral judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari arguments
State the fourth and fifth arguments in your own words. What is the logical entailment they claim follows from rational good and evil?
The fourth argument concerns divine freedom; the fifth concerns punishment before prophecy.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the fourth argument
Explain how the response distinguishes obligation by choice (wujub bi al-ikhtiyar) from compulsion. Why is the argument from al-Tahrir insufficient?
Focus on the concept of ta'allaq (relation) being temporal.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the fifth argument
Explain the distinction between permissibility per se (jawaz bi al-dhat) and permissibility from wisdom (jawaz bi al-hikma). How does the Quran 17:15 fit into this?
Note the exception for gratitude/ingratitude.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between Hanafi and Ash'ari positions on punishment for evil before revelation? Give an example.
The consequence affects cases like clear ingratitude.

المقالة الثانية في الأحكام
وإن لم تكن مؤثرة فيه بل الفعل منه سبحانه فهو قولنا ،بعينه والإيراد المذكور لازم لا يندفع بالجواب الذي مر وشرح ذلك في الفطرة الإلهية وأنه لأجدى من تفاريق العصا) والذي وصل إليَّ من هذه الرسالة ليس فيها إلا العبارات الرائقة والكلمات الفصيحة، وحاصلها لا يزيد على إبطال قول المعتزلة بما ذكر ههنا من عدم صلوح الممكن للإيجاد، وقول الأشعرية بما ذكرها هنا أيضاً، ويلوح من ظاهرها أنه اختار مذهبنا ولم يقصد دفع الإيراد المشار إليه، فليس في تلك الرسالة إلا التحير والله أعلم بحال عباده والأشعرية قالوا: رابعاً: لو كان كذلك أي لو كان كل من الحسن والقبح عقلياً لم يكن الباري تعالى مختاراً في الحكم لأن الحكم على خلاف مقتضى الحسن والقبح حكم على خلاف المعقول (والحكم على خلاف المعقول قبيح وقد وجب تنزيهه عن القبائح، فوجب منه الحكم على مقتضاهما، فلا اختيار (والجواب أن موافقة حكمه للحكمة لا يوجب الاضطرار) فإنه إنما وجب هذا النحو من الحكم لأجل الحكمة بالاختيار، وقد عرفت أن الوجوب بالاختيار لا يوجب الاضطرار، وما أجاب به في «التحرير» من تسليم عدم الاختيار في الحكم»، لأنه خطاب الله تعالى، وخطابه صفة قديمة عندنا والصفات القديمة غير صادرة بالاختيار فغير ،واف لأن الخطاب وإن كان قديماً، لكن التعلق حادث، والحاكم جل مجده مختار فيه فتعود الشبهة كما كانت فتدبر (و) قالوا :(خامساً : لو كان كذلك لجاز العقاب قبل البعثة على مرتكب القبيح، وتارك الحسن، لأن الحسن استحقاق الثواب على الفعل والقبح، استحقاق العقاب، فلو أتى أحد بالفعل القبيح أو ترك الحسن قبل البعثة وعاقبه عليه كان عدلاً فيجوز (وهو) أي الجواز منتف بقوله تعالى : هو وما كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولا) [الإسراء : ١٥] فإن معناه ليس من شأننا ولا يجوز منا ذلك) فإن أمثال هذه العبارة يتبادر منها هذا وفي هذا التعليل دفع لما يتراءى وروده من أن الآية لا تدل إلا على عدم الوقوع وأين عدم الجواز (أقول) في الجواب أنه إن أراد بجواز العقاب الجواز الوقوعي فلا نسلم الملازمة، فإن القول بالقبح العقلي إنما يقتضي الجواز نظراً إلى ذات الفعل والجواز نظراً إلى ذات الفعل لا ينافي عدم الجواز نظراً إلى الحكمة، كيف يجوز نظراً إلى الحكمة (وحينئذ قد كان لهم العذر بنقصان العقل وخفاء المسلك الدال على القبح، والحكيم لا يعذب المعذور (ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿ لِئَلَّا يَكُونَ لِلنَّاسِ عَلَى اللَّهِ حُجَّةُ بَعْدَ الرُّسُلِ [النساء: ١٦٥] ) وإن أراد الجواز نظراً إلى نفس الفعل، وإن كان ممتنعاً نظراً إلى الواقع والحكمة، فبطلان اللازم ،ممنوع والآية الكريمة لا تدل إلا على عدم كونه شأن الباري الحكيم وهذا الجواب صحيح فيما عدا الشكر وكفران المنعم ونحوهما ، وأما فيهما فالمسلك واضح، ولا عذر أصلاً، والعقاب عليهما عدل غير منافٍ للحكمة، كما ذهب إليه معظم مشايخنا الكرام :(وأيضاً : الملازمة ممنوعة فإنه أي التعذيب (فرع الحكم ونحن لا نقول به) وهذا غير واف أصلاً، لأن حقيقة القبح ليس إلا جواز التعذيب، فكيف يكون مناط الحكم؟ (وإنما ينتهض على المعتزلة بل على معظم مشايخنا أيضاً، ولا ينفع الحمل على عدم الوقوع، فإن المؤاخذة على قبيح ظاهر قبحه واقع عند المعتزلة وجوباً، وعلى التدين بالشرك واقع عند مشايخنا الكرام
The Second Treatise on Rulings
And if it is not effective in it, but rather the act is from Him, exalted, then it is our very statement, and the mentioned objection is necessary and cannot be repelled by the answer that passed, and that is explained in the Divine Nature and that it is more beneficial than the fragments of a stick. And what reached me from this treatise does not contain except elegant expressions and eloquent words, and its sum does not exceed invalidating the statement of the Mu'tazila by what is mentioned here of the unsuitability of the contingent for creation, and the statement of the Ash'ariyya by what is mentioned here also. And it appears from its outward that he chose our school and did not intend to repel the mentioned objection. So there is nothing in that treatise but perplexity, and God knows best the condition of His servants. And the Ash'ariyya said: Fourthly: If it were so, i.e., if each of good and evil were rational, then the Exalted Creator would not be free in judgment, because a judgment contrary to the requirement of good and evil is a judgment contrary to reason, and a judgment contrary to reason is evil, and it is obligatory to exalt Him above evils. So judgment in accordance with them would be obligatory upon Him, and thus there is no choice. The answer: That His judgment's accordance with wisdom does not necessitate compulsion, for this type of judgment is only required due to wisdom by choice, and you know that obligation by choice does not necessitate compulsion. And what was answered in 'al-Tahrir' of conceding the lack of choice in judgment, because it is the speech of God, and His speech is an eternal attribute with us, and eternal attributes are not originated by choice, is insufficient, because speech, even if eternal, but its relation (ta'allaq) is temporal, and the Ruler, exalted be His majesty, is free in it, so the doubt returns as it was, so ponder. And they said: Fifthly: If it were so, then punishment before the mission (of prophets) would be permissible upon one who commits evil and abandons good, because good is deserving of reward for the act and evil is deserving of punishment. So if someone committed an evil act or abandoned good before the mission and He punished him for it, it would be just and thus permissible. And that permissibility is negated by His statement: 'We are not punishers until We send a messenger' (17:15). For its meaning is: It is not our way and it is not permissible from us that. For such expressions imply this. And in this explanation there is a repulsion of what may appear: that the verse only indicates the negation of occurrence, not the negation of permissibility. I say in answer: If he means by 'permissibility of punishment' permissibility in terms of occurrence, we do not concede the entailment. For the statement of rational evil only necessitates permissibility with respect to the essence of the act, and permissibility with respect to the essence of the act does not preclude the lack of permissibility with respect to wisdom. How can it be permissible with respect to wisdom? And then they would have an excuse due to deficiency of intellect and the obscurity of the path indicating evil, and the Wise does not punish the excused. For this reason, He said: 'Lest people have an argument against God after the messengers' (4:165). And if he means permissibility with respect to the act itself, even if it is impossible with respect to reality and wisdom, then the falsity of the consequent is denied, and the noble verse only indicates that it is not the way of the Wise Creator. And this answer is correct except in the matter of gratitude (shukr) and ingratitude to the Benefactor and the like. For in these the path is clear, and there is no excuse at all, and punishment upon them is just and not contrary to wisdom, as most of our noble scholars held. And also: The entailment is denied, for punishment is a branch of judgment, and we do not say it. This is completely insufficient, because the reality of evil is nothing but the permissibility of punishment, so how can it be the basis of judgment? And it only is effective against the Mu'tazila, but also against most of our scholars. And it does not help to revert to the negation of occurrence, for holding one accountable for a clearly evil act is actual according to the Mu'tazila necessarily, and for adherence to shirk is actual according to our noble scholars.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is the continuation of the theological discussion on rational good and evil, presenting the fourth and fifth Ash'ari arguments and their responses within the Hanafi-Maturidi tradition. The text begins with a closing remark on a previous treatise that attempted to refute both Mu'tazila and Ash'ari positions but ultimately only caused perplexity. Then it presents the Ash'ari's fourth argument: if good and evil were rational, God would not be free in judgment because He would be compelled to judge according to reason, which is evil. The response distinguishes between obligation by choice and compulsion: God's judgment follows wisdom, but since He chooses that wisdom, it does not constrain Him. The answer from 'al-Tahrir' that concedes lack of choice in judgment because God's speech is eternal is critiqued: the relation (ta'allaq) of speech is temporal and God is free in it. The fifth argument: if good and evil were rational, punishment before sending prophets would be permissible, but the Quran (17:15) negates that. The response differentiates between permissibility per se (based on the act's essence) and permissibility from wisdom: rational evil makes punishment possible in essence, but divine wisdom may prevent it due to excuses (ignorance). However, for ingratitude to the Benefactor, there is no excuse and punishment is just. The text also critiques a response that denies the entailment by calling punishment a branch of judgment, arguing that the reality of evil is precisely the permissibility of punishment.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح العقليان (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyan): Moral values of good and evil that are intrinsically knowable by reason independent of revelation.
الوجوب بالاختيار (al-wujub bi al-ikhtiyar): Necessity that arises from a free choice, not compulsion; used to argue that God's judging according to reason is by His own wisdom.
التعلق (al-ta'allaq): The relation of God's eternal speech to temporal objects; while speech is eternal, its relation to specific judgments is temporal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the fifth Ash'ari argument, why is punishment before sending a messenger not permissible despite rational evil?
A) Because rational evil does not exist for actions before revelation
B) Because the Quran absolutely prohibits any punishment before messengers
C) Because people have an excuse due to deficient intellect and unclear evidence, so the wise God does not punish them
D) Because punishment is a branch of judgment and judgment does not apply before revelation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Maturidi position affirms that good and evil are rationally known in essence, but divine wisdom may suspend punishment for those with excuses; for clear cases like ingratitude, rational evil alone justifies punishment.

Ash'ari's Fourth Argument: Rational Good/Evil and Divine Choice
Ash'ari argument and Hanafi response on divine choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ash'ari argument that rational good/evil negate divine choice and evaluate the Hanafi response using the distinction between wujūb bi al-ikhtiyār and wujūb bi al-iḍtirār.

Core Idea
Obligation by choice preserves divine freedom; rational good/evil do not compel God.

The Ash'ariyya argue that if good and evil are rationally known, God would be compelled to judge accordingly, negating His free choice. The Hanafi response distinguishes between compulsion and voluntary necessity. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari argument
Identify the core claim: if good/evil are rational, then judging against them would be irrational, which is impossible for God; thus He must judge according to them, losing free choice.
The argument rests on the premise that God must avoid what is rationally evil.
2
Identify the key premise
The Ash'ari assume that rational good/evil impose a binding necessity on God's actions.
This premise is contested by the Hanafi response.
3
State the Hanafi response
The Hanafi answer denies that conformity to wisdom entails compulsion; it introduces the concept of 'wujūb bi al-ikhtiyār' (obligation by choice).
God's judgment is according to wisdom because He freely chooses it.
4
Explain wujūb bi al-ikhtiyār
This concept distinguishes between necessity that arises from free will (e.g., a promise) and necessity from external force. God's judgments are necessary in the sense of being consistent with His wisdom, but that consistency is self-imposed.
This is a key Maturidi contribution to the debate.
5
Critique the al-Tahrir answer
The author notes that merely denying choice in divine speech (because speech is eternal) is insufficient, because the temporal connection (ta'alluq) of speech involves choice.
This shows the depth of the Hanafi engagement with the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأشعرية قالوا: رابعاً: لو كان كذلك أي لو كان كل من الحسن والقبح عقلياً لم يكن الباري تعالى مختاراً في الحكم لأن الحكم على خلاف مقتضى الحسن والقبح حكم على خلاف المعقول (والحكم على خلاف المعقول قبيح وقد وجب تنزيهه عن القبائح، فوجب منه الحكم على مقتضاهما، فلا اختيار (والجواب أن موافقة حكمه للحكمة لا يوجب الاضطرار) فإنه إنما وجب هذا النحو من الحكم لأجل الحكمة بالاختيار، وقد عرفت أن الوجوب بالاختيار لا يوجب الاضطرار، وما أجاب به في «التحرير» من تسليم عدم الاختيار في الحكم»، لأنه خطاب الله تعالى، وخطابه صفة قديمة عندنا والصفات القديمة غير صادرة بالاختيار فغير واف لأن الخطاب وإن كان قديماً، لكن التعلق حادث، والحاكم جل مجده مختار فيه فتعود الشبهة كما كانت فتدبر
And the Ash'ariyya said: Fourthly: If it were so, that is, if each of good and evil were rational, then the Exalted Creator would not be free in judgment, because judgment contrary to the requirement of good and evil is judgment contrary to reason, and judgment contrary to reason is evil, and He must be deemed free from evil, so judgment must be according to their requirement, thus no choice. The answer: That His judgment's conformity to wisdom does not necessitate compulsion. For this kind of judgment is necessitated for the sake of wisdom by choice, and you know that obligation by choice does not necessitate compulsion. And what was answered in al-Tahrir by conceding lack of choice in judgment because it is the speech of Allah, and His speech is an eternal attribute with us, and eternal attributes are not issued by choice—it is insufficient, because speech, though eternal, its connection is temporal, and the Judge, may His glory be exalted, is free in it, so the doubt returns as it was, so reflect.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ariyya's fourth argument against rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqli). They argue that if good and evil were rationally known, then God would be compelled to judge accordingly, negating His free choice. The Hanafi response, consistent with the Maturidi school, distinguishes between obligation by compulsion (idṭirār) and obligation by choice (wujūb bi al-ikhtiyār). God's judgment conforms to wisdom by His own choice, not by external compulsion. The author also critiques the answer from al-Tahrir that denies choice in divine speech because speech is eternal; he notes that the temporal connection (ta'alluq) of speech is a matter of divine choice, so the problem remains.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح العقليان (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-ʿaqliyyān): The rational determination that certain acts are intrinsically good or evil, known by reason independently of revelation.
الوجوب بالاختيار (al-wujūb bi al-ikhtiyār): Necessity that arises from a voluntary choice, not from external compulsion.
التعلق (al-taʿalluq): The temporal connection or relation of an eternal attribute to created things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the Ash'ariyya's fourth argument, why does God's judgment according to wisdom not negate His free choice?
A) Because God's judgment is eternal and thus beyond choice.
B) Because the obligation arises from God's own free choice, not from external compulsion.
C) Because God cannot act contrary to reason.
D) Because rational good and evil do not exist.

Key Takeaway
God's judgment conforms to wisdom by His own free choice; rational good/evil do not compel Him.

Ash'ariyya's Fifth Argument: Does Rational Evil Imply Pre-Prophethood Punishment?
Ash'ariyya argues against rational morality using Quranic verse; Hanafi responds with nuanced distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Ash'ari argument against rational evil by applying the Hanafi distinction between permissibility per se and permissibility per wisdom, and identify the exception for acts like ingratitude to the Benefactor.

Core Idea
Punishment before prophethood is not impossible; it is simply not God's wise practice—except for clear evils.

This passage presents the Ash'ariyya's fifth argument against rational good and evil: if acts are rationally evil, then punishment before the sending of prophets would be just and permissible, contradicting the Quranic verse that denies punishment without a messenger. The Hanafi response refines the concept of permissibility, distinguishing between what is possible in essence and what is consistent with divine wisdom. This lesson unpacks that distinction and explores its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari argument
State the logical implication they claim: rational evil → permissibility of punishment before prophethood.
2
Locate the Quranic verse
Recognize the verse 'We are not punishers until We send a messenger' (17:15) and its apparent contradiction.
The Ash'aris treat this as a categorical negation.
3
Analyze the Hanafi distinction
Separate permissibility with regard to the essence of the act from permissibility with regard to divine wisdom. The former may exist while the latter negates punishment due to excuses.
The text: 'permissibility with regard to the essence does not negate impermissibility with regard to wisdom.'
4
Examine the exception
For acts like kufr al-mun'im (ingratitude to the Benefactor), the evil is clear and no excuse exists, so punishment is just and not contrary to wisdom.
This restricts the scope of the verse.
5
Evaluate alternative interpretations
Note that some Hanafis specify the verse for worldly punishment only (by context) or interpret 'messenger' as the intellect.
These show the flexibility of the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا :خامساً : لو كان كذلك لجاز العقاب قبل البعثة على مرتكب القبيح، وتارك الحسن، لأن الحسن استحقاق الثواب على الفعل والقبح، استحقاق العقاب، فلو أتى أحد بالفعل القبيح أو ترك الحسن قبل البعثة وعاقبه عليه كان عدلاً فيجوز (وهو) أي الجواز منتف بقوله تعالى : هو وما كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولا) [الإسراء : ١٥] فإن معناه ليس من شأننا ولا يجوز منا ذلك) فإن أمثال هذه العبارة يتبادر منها هذا وفي هذا التعليل دفع لما يتراءى وروده من أن الآية لا تدل إلا على عدم الوقوع وأين عدم الجواز (أقول) في الجواب أنه إن أراد بجواز العقاب الجواز الوقوعي فلا نسلم الملازمة، فإن القول بالقبح العقلي إنما يقتضي الجواز نظراً إلى ذات الفعل والجواز نظراً إلى ذات الفعل لا ينافي عدم الجواز نظراً إلى الحكمة، كيف يجوز نظراً إلى الحكمة (وحينئذ قد كان لهم العذر بنقصان العقل وخفاء المسلك الدال على القبح، والحكيم لا يعذب المعذور (ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿ لِئَلَّا يَكُونَ لِلنَّاسِ عَلَى اللَّهِ حُجَّةُ بَعْدَ الرُّسُلِ [النساء: ١٦٥] ) وإن أراد الجواز نظراً إلى نفس الفعل، وإن كان ممتنعاً نظراً إلى الواقع والحكمة، فبطلان اللازم ممنوع والآية الكريمة لا تدل إلا على عدم كونه شأن الباري الحكيم وهذا الجواب صحيح فيما عدا الشكر وكفران المنعم ونحوهما ، وأما فيهما فالمسلك واضح، ولا عذر أصلاً، والعقاب عليهما عدل غير منافٍ للحكمة، كما ذهب إليه معظم مشايخنا الكرام :(وأيضاً : الملازمة ممنوعة فإنه أي التعذيب (فرع الحكم ونحن لا نقول به) وهذا غير واف أصلاً، لأن حقيقة القبح ليس إلا جواز التعذيب، فكيف يكون مناط الحكم؟ (وإنما ينتهض على المعتزلة بل على معظم مشايخنا أيضاً، ولا ينفع الحمل على عدم الوقوع، فإن المؤاخذة على قبيح ظاهر قبحه واقع عند المعتزلة وجوباً، وعلى التدين بالشرك واقع عند مشايخنا الكرام (فخصصوا) الآية بعذاب الدنيا بدلالة (السياق) وهو قوله عزّ من قائل: ﴿وَإِذَا أَرَدْنَا أَن تُهْلِكَ قَرْيَةٌ أَمَرْنَا مُتْرَفِيهَا فَفَسَقُوا فِيهَا فَحَقَّ عَلَيْهَا الْقَوْلُ فَدَمَرْنَهَا تَدْمِيرًا ﴾ [الإسراء : ١٦] ولعل هلاك القرى و تخريبها مسبب عن وقوع العباد المصطفين في الغم، ودعائهم به عليها، وهو مسبب عن فسقهم، ولهذا يتأخر هلاك القرى عن الفسق إلى زمان إرسال الرسل، وليس شأن كل قبيح تسبيبه إلى هلاك القرى حتى يقال : ما الفرق بين عذاب الدنيا وعذاب الآخرة؟ حتى جوزوا الثاني بذنوب قبل البعثة دون الأول وأولوا أيضاً الرسول (بالعقل، فإنه رسول باطن) في تنبيه القلب، فالمعنى إذا والله أعلم وليس شأننا التعذيب من غير إعطاء العقل الذي به تنبيه الإنسان إلى غير ذلك من التأويلات، وها هنا جواب آخر، وها هنا جواب آخر هو أنه ليس زمان لم يبعث فيه نبي أصلاً البعثة مطلقاً، في الواقع، بل لم يترك الإنسان سدى، فتقدير وجود زمان خال ووقع الأعمال القبيحة تقدير محال فبعد فرضه يلتزم صحة العقاب، فمعنى الآية الكريمة والله أعلم : وليس شأننا العقاب من دون البعثة، فإنها لازمة لوجود الإنسان من لدن آدم إلى القيامة ، ولم يخل زمان عنها فافهم
And they said: Fifthly: If it were so, then punishment before the sending of messengers would be permissible for the committer of the evil and the abandoner of the good, because good is entitlement to reward for the act, and evil is entitlement to punishment. So if someone did the evil act or left the good act before the sending of messengers and He punished him for it, it would be just, thus permissible. But that permissibility is negated by His exalted saying: 'And We are not punishers until We send a messenger' [al-Isra 17:15], for its meaning is that it is not Our way nor permissible for Us. For such expressions give this impression, and in this explication is a repelling of what might appear objectionable, that the verse only indicates non-occurrence, not impossibility. I say in the answer: If he intends by permissibility of punishment the permissibility of occurrence, we do not concede the implication, for the claim of rational evil only entails permissibility with regard to the essence of the act, and permissibility with regard to the essence of the act does not negate impermissibility with regard to wisdom. How could it be permissible with regard to wisdom? (And then they had the excuse of deficiency of intellect and hiddenness of the path indicating evil, and the Wise does not punish the excused. And for this He said exalted: 'So that people would not have an argument against Allah after the messengers' [al-Nisa 4:165].) And if he intends permissibility with regard to the act itself, even if it is impossible with regard to reality and wisdom, then the annulment of the necessary consequence is not conceded, and the noble verse only indicates its not being the way of the Wise Creator. This answer is correct except regarding gratitude and ingratitude to the Benefactor and the like, for in these the path is clear, and there is no excuse at all, and punishment on them is just and not contrary to wisdom, as the majority of our esteemed shaykhs held. And also: The implication is not conceded, for punishment is a branch of judgment, and we do not say with it (i.e., the rationalist view). This is not sufficient at all, for the essence of evil is nothing but the permissibility of punishment, so how can it be the basis of judgment? Rather it is valid against the Mu'tazila and also against most of our shaykhs, and the argument of non-occurrence does not help, for accountability for an evidently evil act is real according to the Mu'tazila by necessity, and accountability for committing shirk is real according to our esteemed shaykhs. So they specified the verse for worldly punishment by indication of the context, which is His saying, exalted: 'And when We intend to destroy a town, We command its affluent, then they commit transgression in it, so the word becomes justified against it, then We destroy it completely' [al-Isra 17:16]. And perhaps the destruction of towns is caused by the occurrence of chosen servants in grief and their supplication against them, which is caused by their transgression, and for this reason the destruction of towns is delayed after transgression to the time of sending messengers. And it is not the way of every evil to cause destruction of towns, so it cannot be said: What is the difference between worldly punishment and otherworldly punishment? That they allowed the second for sins before the sending of messengers but not the first; and they also interpreted 'messenger' as intellect, for it is an inner messenger in alerting the heart, so the meaning is, and Allah knows best, it is not Our way to punish without giving the intellect by which a person is alerted, and other such interpretations. And here is another answer, and here is another answer, which is that there is no time in which no prophet was sent at all, in reality; rather, man was not left neglected. So supposing the existence of an empty time and the occurrence of evil deeds is a supposition of impossibility. After hypothesizing it, one concedes the validity of punishment. So the meaning of the noble verse, and Allah knows best, is: It is not Our way to punish without the sending of messengers, for it is necessary for the existence of man from Adam until the Day of Resurrection, and no time has been empty of it. So understand.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ariyya's fifth argument against rational good and evil: if evil is rationally known, then punishment of evil acts before the sending of prophets would be permissible, which contradicts the Quranic verse that forbids punishment before prophetic mission. The Hanafi response distinguishes between two types of permissibility: permissibility taken from the essence of the act (which rational evil implies) and permissibility considered with respect to divine wisdom. The second type can negate the first, as God in His wisdom may withhold punishment for excuses such as deficiency of intellect. The exception is ingratitude to the Benefactor (kufr al-mun'im) and similar acts where the evil is clear and no excuse exists, punishment for which is just according to many Hanafi shaykhs. Another response argues that the verse refers specifically to worldly destruction, as indicated by context, not to otherworldly punishment. Additionally, some interpret 'messenger' as the intellect, while another view holds that no time is devoid of prophets, so the hypothesis of pre-prophetic evil acts is impossible. The overall conclusion is that the Ash'ari argument is not compelling.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق (al-istiḥqāq): Entitlement to reward or punishment based on the rational nature of the act.
كفران المنعم (kufrān al-munʿim): Ingratitude to the Benefactor (God), considered rationally evil beyond excuse.
تعذيب المعذور (taʿdhīb al-maʿdhūr): Punishing someone who has a legitimate excuse (like ignorance or lack of access to revelation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, for which acts does punishment before prophethood remain just and not contrary to wisdom?
A) All evil acts without exception.
B) Acts where the evil is unclear and the agent has an excuse.
C) Acts of ingratitude to the Benefactor and similar clearly evil acts.
D) No acts, because such punishment is always unjust.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position affirms rational good and evil but limits punishment before revelation to acts of extreme ingratitude, reconciling reason with Quranic statements about divine wisdom.

The Mu'tazila's Argument of Ifḥām al-Rusul and the Hanafi Critique
Mu'tazila argument and Hanafi critique of intuitive obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Mu'tazila's argument that religious obligation leads to silencing of messengers (ifḥām al-rusul) and explain the Hanafi critique of their claim that the obligation to reflect is intuitive.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's argument against religious obligation is itself vulnerable: the 'intuitive' obligation to reflect is not self-evident.

This passage presents a key usul debate between the Mu'tazila and the Hanafi tradition. The Mu'tazila argue that if all obligation comes from revelation, then before revelation, a person cannot be obligated to reflect on a miracle, creating a vicious circularity that 'silences' the messenger. The Hanafi commentator deconstructs this, showing the Mu'tazila's appeal to intuitive obligation is not tenable. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate arguments in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila's claim
State the core Mu'tazila argument: if judgment is only religious, then a person before revelation has no obligation to reflect on a miracle, so the messenger is silenced.
2
Locate the Mu'tazila's solution
The Mu'tazila claim that the obligation to reflect is an intuitive (fitri) proposition, thus not dependent on revelation.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi commentator argues that the obligation of reflection depends on theoretical premises that are themselves not intuitive, so the Mu'tazila's position is weak.
4
Follow the dialectical exchange
The text shows a back-and-forth where even if the Mu'tazila insist on intuitive obligation, the messenger can still circumvent the problem by appealing to harm avoidance.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Mu'tazila affirm rational obligation a priori; Hanafis see it as theoretical but not necessarily dependent on revelation, leaving room for a more nuanced account of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتزلة قالوا :أولاً : لو كان الحكم شرعياً لزم إفحام الرسل عند أمرهم المكلف (بالنظر في المعجزات ليعلم أنهم رسل (فيقول) هذا المكلف (لا أنظر) في معجزتك (ما لم يجب النظر عليَّ لأن للإنسان أن يكف عما ليس واجباً عليه (ولا يجب) عليَّ ما لم يصدر عن لسان نبي ولا نبوة إلا بالمعجزة ولا تعلم المعجزة إلا بالنظر، فحينئذ لا يجب النظر (ما لم أنظر فلزم إفحام الرسل، أي إسكاتهم، وهو محال، لأنه حينئذ يفوت الغرض من الرسالة، فإن قيل : يلزم عليهم هذا قالوا : ولا يلزم علينا لأن وجوب النظر عندنا من القضايا الفطرية القياس) فإنكارهم وجوب النظر من المكابرة، فلا إفحام (وفيه ما فيه لأن وجوب النظر موقوف على إفادته للعلم مطلقاً، وفي الإلهيات خاصة، وفيه خلاف الرياضيين، وعلى أن معرفة الله واجبة، وفيه خلاف الحشوية وأن المعرفة لا تتم إلا بالنظر، ونسب الإباء عنه إلى المتصوفة، وهو غير ثابت عنهم، بل هم مصرحون بخلافه، إلا عن الملاحدة المدعين للتصوف؛ وعلى أن مقدمة الواجب واجبة وسيجيء الخلاف فيه ولا تثبت هذه المقدمات إلا بنظر أدق، والموقوف على ما لا يثبت إلا بالنظر الدقيق كيف يكون فطرياً، كذا قيل، وفيه: أن هذه مؤاخذة لفظية، فإن لهم أن يقولوا إن وجوب النظر وإن كان نظرياً لكن لا يتوقف على الشرع، فيمكن أن يقول الرسول : إن هذا واجب عليك مع قطع النظر عن إخباري فامتثل، فلا يتمشى منه لا يجب النظر ما لم أنظر لأنه حينئذ له أن يقول : لا أنظر، فإنه غير واجب، فإذا قال الرسول : النظر واجب يقول المكلف هذا نظري لا يدرك إلا بالنظر، فليجز عدم صدقه وإني لا أضيع وقتي فيما لا أعلم وجوبه فحينئذ الإفحام لازم قطعاً، فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا، فإن قلت للرسول أن يقول : أدعي قضية إن كنت صادقاً فيها يضرك الإباء فاسمع، وليس للعاقل الإباء عن سماع مثلها فلا إفحام، قلت : هذا ينقلب على أصل الدليل، فإنه لو كان الوجوب بالشرع كان له أن يقول : إني أدعي قضية إن كنت صادقاً فيها لزم الإحكام بقولي فاسمع، فتدبر فإنه سانح عزيز :(والجواب : أنا لا نسلم أن الوجوب بالشرع (يتوقف على النظر
The Mu'tazila said: First: If judgment were religious (shar'i), then the silencing of the messengers would be necessary when they command the obligated person to reflect on miracles to know that they are messengers. This obligated person would say: I will not reflect on your miracle as long as reflection is not obligatory for me, because a person may refrain from what is not obligatory upon him, and it is not obligatory upon me unless it issues from a prophet's tongue, and prophethood is only known through a miracle, and a miracle is only known through reflection, so then reflection is not obligatory unless I reflect, thus the silencing of the messengers is necessary, i.e., silencing them, which is impossible because then the purpose of the message fails. If it is said: This applies to them, they say: But it does not apply to us because the obligation of reflection with us is from the intuitive innate propositions (al-qadaya al-fitriyya al-qiyasiyya). Their denial of the obligation of reflection is stubbornness, so no silencing. But there is much in this: Because the obligation of reflection depends on its leading to knowledge absolutely, and especially in theology; and there is disagreement among the mathematicians; and that knowledge of God is obligatory is disputed by the Hashwiyya; and that knowledge is only attained through reflection is denied by some claimed Sufis; and that the prerequisite of the obligatory is obligatory will be disputed later; and these premises are not established except through precise reflection, so how can something dependent on precise reflection be intuitive? So it is said. And among [the weaknesses] is that this is a verbal criticism, for they can say that the obligation of reflection, even if theoretical, does not depend on divine law, so the messenger could say: This is obligatory upon you regardless of my informing you, so obey. Then the [reply] 'I will not reflect unless I reflect' does not work because he can then say: I will not reflect, for it is not obligatory. So when the messenger says 'reflection is obligatory', the obligated person says: This is theoretical, only known through reflection; so it is possible that it is false, and I will not waste my time on what I do not know to be obligatory. Thus silencing is definitely necessary. So whatever your answer is, it is our answer. If you say the messenger can say: I claim a proposition; if you are truthful in it, refusal harms you, so listen; and a rational person does not refuse to listen to such, so no silencing. I say: This turns against the basis of the proof, for if obligation were by divine law, he could say: I claim a proposition; if you are truthful in it, then adherence to my statement becomes necessary, so listen. So reflect, for it is a precious insight. And the answer is: We do not concede that obligation by divine law depends on reflection.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's first argument against the Ash'ari (and generally non-rationalist) position that judgment (al-hukm) is purely religious (shar'i). They argue that if obligation only arises from religious command, then a person facing a prophet's challenge to reflect on a miracle would be caught in a circularity: he needs the prophet to make reflection obligatory, but the prophet's authority depends on the miracle, which requires reflection to know. Thus the messenger is 'silenced' (ifḥām), making the purpose of prophethood impossible. The Mu'tazila claim to avoid this by positing that the obligation to reflect is intuitive (fiṭrī), not derived from revelation. The Hanafi commentator then critiques this by noting that the obligation of reflection itself depends on many disputed premises (e.g., that reflection leads to knowledge, that knowledge of God is obligatory, etc.), which are not intuitive but require precise reasoning. He then engages in a dialectic showing that even if the Mu'tazila appeal to intuitive obligation, the messenger can still circumvent the problem by claiming a direct obligation without need for proof. The passage ends with an inconclusive exchange, advising reflection ('so reflect, for it is a precious insight'). The final answer asserts that the Mu'tazila's premise that religious obligation depends on reflection is not conceded. The commentary highlights that the Hanafi position here is not simply to side with the Mu'tazila but to show the complexity of the argument and the insufficiency of the Mu'tazila's claim of intuitive obligation.

Key Terms
إفحام الرسل (ifḥām al-rusul): The logical impossibility of messengers establishing their authority because the prerequisites for recognizing their miracle are not yet known to be obligatory.
القضايا الفطرية (al-qadāyā al-fiṭriyya): Self-evident, intuitive propositions that require no proof, such as the obligation to avoid harm.
مقدمة الواجب (muqaddimat al-wājib): The prerequisite or preliminary step required to fulfill an obligatory act (e.g., reflection to know God).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentator, what is a key weakness in the Mu'tazila's claim that the obligation to reflect (wujūb al-naẓar) is an intuitive (fitri) proposition?
A) It contradicts the Quranic verse about punishment before messengers.
B) It depends on theoretical premises that themselves require precise reflection.
C) The Mu'tazila do not believe in rational good and evil.
D) The messenger can simply command reflection without proof.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument that religious obligation leads to an impossible circularity is not decisive; the Hanafi critique shows that their appeal to intuitive obligation is itself problematic, leaving room for a different account of obligation.

The Original Ruling of Actions Before Revelation: Ibahah vs Hurmah
Explores the dispute over the original ruling of actions before revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the original ruling before revelation and identify the evidence and reasoning for their distinction between wealth and lives.

Core Idea
Hanafi: original permissibility for wealth, prohibition for lives, based on the fatra period and excuse of ignorance.

This lesson explores a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: what was the legal status of actions before divine revelation arrived? The Hanafi school, along with many Shafi'is, holds that the default is permissibility (ibahah) for non-essential acts, while others argue for original prohibition. The commentator navigates through the evidence, including Quranic verses and the concept of the fatra period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main positions
State the Hanafi view (original permissibility for non-essential acts, prohibition for lives) and the opposing view (absolute prohibition). Note the refinement by Sadr al-Islam on wealth vs. lives.
The dispute is specifically about the period of intermission (fatra) when the law was forgotten.
2
Understand the role of the fatra period
Analyze why the dispute is confined to the fatra: after a messenger, law exists; fatra is an exception. The Quranic verses prove no time without a warner, but the fatra represents oblivion.
The Hanafi view treats ignorance during fatra as an excuse, leading to practical permissibility.
3
Examine the Hanafi distinction between wealth and lives
Explain why lives are originally prohibited: killing, cutting limbs, etc., remain prohibited unless specified. Wealth is permissible by default.
This distinction is from Sadr al-Islam, reflecting a middle ground.
4
Consider the Mu'tazilah alternative
The Mu'tazilah divide acts into voluntary and necessary, and further by perceived moral qualities. They have three opinions: permissibility, prohibition, or suspense (tawaqquf).
Their approach is based on rational perception of good and evil.
5
Evaluate the objections and resolution
The commentator raises objections to the first two views (contradiction from lack of detailed cause) and to the suspense view. The final resolution: a general ruling from a universal proof is permissible and actionable until revelation.
The key is that lack of detailed cause does not preclude a general ruling from a comprehensive evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اتصاف الفعل بحكمين متضادين في نفس الأمر فإن فرض عدم العلم بالحكم في فعل يجوز أن يكون الوجوب مثلاً، والآن أثبتم الإباحة أو الحظر (ولا ينفع حينئذ الإجمال) والتفصيل اللذان ذكرا في الجواب (لأن اختلاف العلة لا يرفع التناقض وها هنا الإجمال في علة معرفة الحكم لا في محل الحكم (فتأمل) فإنه يمكن الجواب بأن هذا الحكم الإجمالي كالحكم الاجتهادي الخطأ فيجب العمل به إلى أن تطلع شمس الحقيقة بتحقق البعثة فلا يلزم جواز الاتصاف أصلاً كذا في «الحاشية». ويمكن توجيه الجواب المشهور بأنهم لا يقولون بعدم علم الحكم في خصوص فعل فعل، وتجويز أن يكون غير الإباحة والحظر حتى يلزم من إثباتهما ولو بالدليل الإجمالي اجتماع المتنافيين، بل المقصود عدم العلم الحاصل بدليل دليل مخصوص مخصوص بكل فعل فعمدوا إلى دليل إجمالي شامل لكل فعل فوجدوه حاكماً بالإباحة أو الحظر، فحينئذ لا تناقض فتدبر (الثالث : التوقف) في الحكم بشيء من الأحكام (لأن ثمة حكماً معيناً من الخمسة، ولا يدرى أيها واقع) فيتوقف (أقول : هذا يقتضي الوقف في الخصوصية) أي الحكم في مخصوص لمخصوص (ولا ينافي ذلك الحكم) إجمالاً في كل فعل فتدبر وهذا شيء عجاب، فإن المصنف أورد على الأولين بعدم كفاية الإجمال، والتفصيل في عدم المنافاة، وها هنا حكم بعدم المنافاة لأجل الإجمال والتفصيل، إلا أن يقال : المراد أن الوقف في الخصوص لا ينافي الحكم الإجمالي ولو على سبيل الخطأ في الاجتهاد والأصوب في التقرير أن يقال : إن عدم كفاية الإجمال والتفصيل هناك، لأن أهل المذهبين الأولين حكموا بالإباحة أو التحريم مطلقاً، والمفروض عدم معرفة الجهة المخصوصة لكل فعل فعل فاحتمل أن يكون في البعض جهة محسنة موجبة معلومة عند الله تعالى فيكون الحكم بحسبها الوجوب، ويرد الشرع بحسبها أن ورد كما ورد في صوم آخر رمضان المبارك، فيجتمع فيه الوجوب مع الحرمة أو الإباحة، ولا ينفع الإجمال والتفصيل، وبهذا ظهر اندفاع تقرير الجواب المذكور سابقاً، والمقصود من الإيراد هنا أن المفروض إنما هو عدم معرفة العلة المخصوصة المعينة في كل فعل فعل، ولا ينافي معرفة حكم مخصوص بفعل مطابقاً لما في نفس الأمر بالاستنباط على ضابطة كلية، لا أن يعلم حكم
The choice of most Hanafis and Shafi'is (or its origin is prohibition, as held by others, and Sadr al-Islam said) the origin (is permissibility in wealth and prohibition in lives) so killing a person, cutting off a limb, causing pain by beating, and actions regarding private parts remain prohibited, except what is specified by evidence like retaliation and marriage. (So before) this dispute occurred after revelation by scriptural evidences, meaning those evidences indicated that what no evidence permits is permitted by indication of another evidence, as with most Hanafis and Shafi'is (or prohibited) by indication of another evidence as with others, so this does not contradict the absence of hardship before the mission. And therein lies what is there, since from investigation it appears that the dispute is before the coming of revelation, and therefore they did not consider the removal of original permissibility as abrogation due to the absence of prohibition. Their statement: that it is the address of the Lawgiver, so ponder. Likewise in the Hashiyah. And let us convey in affirming the truth, so let us lay a premise: First, there never passed a time upon mankind without God sending a messenger with a religion, because the law of Adam, peace be upon him, remained until the coming of Noah, and his law until Abraham, and his law was general for all. So whoever had his law abrogated, another law took its place like the law of Moses and Jesus for the Children of Israel, and it remained for others, as it was until the arrival of our true law which remains until the Day of Judgment. And this is indicated by His saying: "And there is no nation except that a warner came to them" [Fatir 35:24] and His saying: "Does man think that he will be left aimless?" [Qiyamah 75:36]. When this is established, we say: Then no dispute can occur in any time of human existence at all, and the ruling of absolute permissibility cannot be given, nor absolute prohibition, for how can it be when in every time there is a law containing prohibition of some things, obligation of some, permissibility and other things? So then the dispute is only in the period of intermission (fatra) in which the law was obliterated by their negligence. Its gist: those who came after the obliteration of the law and ignorance of rulings: either this ignorance of theirs is an excuse, so all acts are treated as permissible—meaning they are not punished for doing nor for leaving, as with the permissible—and this is the view of most Hanafis and Shafi'is, and they call it original permissibility. This is what Imam Fakhr al-Islam meant by his saying: And we do not say by this origin i.e., that prohibition is an abrogator of original permissibility by the supposition that humans were not left aimless in any time; rather, this—the statement of original permissibility—is based on the period of intermission before our law, meaning there is no real permissibility, but rather in the sense of negation of hardship. Perhaps it is meant for acts other than disbelief and the like, for their prohibition in every law is clearly apparent. And if it is not an excuse, then it is necessary to say all things are prohibited due to mixing of lawful and unlawful due to ignorance of specification, so they are prohibited as a precaution, so the origin becomes prohibition as with others. Perhaps they meant other than the necessary things. And the claim of Sadr al-Islam that prohibition of lives is an established origin in every law never abrogated, so he ruled by it. As for other things, they are unknown, and this ignorance is an excuse, so he differentiated. Perhaps this is his interpretation of the statement of Hanafis and Shafi'is, and in the author's words there is also an indication of it. This is what is with this slave, and perhaps God will bring about after that something. (As for the Mu'tazilah, they divided voluntary acts—those which one can live without, like eating fruit for example—and necessary acts which are otherwise, which are obligatory or permissible according to them—into: what is perceived in it a ground of beautification, either a strong beauty whose omission causes ugliness and blame, or a weak one such that one is rewarded for the act and not punished for omission, or weaker than it such that one is safe from punishment for doing or leaving; or a ground of uglification, either strong ugliness such that one is punished for the act, or weak that does not cause hardship but rather leaving is better. So it divides into the five well-known categories of obligation, recommendation, permissibility, prohibition, and detestability, and into what is not like that—i.e., a ground of beautification or uglification is not perceived in it. And they have three opinions concerning it before revelation: permissibility to achieve the wisdom of creation, removing aimlessness—meaning if it were not permissible, the benefit of creation would be lost, which is the servant's benefit, so it would be aimlessness. And perhaps the implication may be denied—i.e., the implication that non-permissibility leads to loss of the benefit of creation, because it is possible that the benefit is trial by obligating avoidance and prohibition, lest there be disposition in the property of another, the Creator, without permission. And it has passed with what is in it. And it is not objected to them that how can rational permissibility and prohibition be said when it is assumed that there is no ruling for the intellect? So saying both with this assumption is combining contradictory things. That is because the assumption is that there is no detailed knowledge of the cause of the ruling concerning any particular act, and that does not preclude general knowledge of a cause encompassing the totality of acts. (I say:) It is objected to them that then the possibility of an act being characterized by two contradictory rulings in themselves would follow; for if lack of knowledge of the ruling in an act is assumed, it could be obligation, for example, and now you have established permissibility or prohibition. Neither general nor detailed knowledge, which were mentioned in the answer, helps then, because difference of cause does not remove contradiction, and here the generality is in the cause of knowing the ruling, not in the locus of the ruling. (So ponder,) for it can be answered that this general ruling is like a mistaken ijtihadic ruling; it must be acted upon until the sun of reality shines with the occurrence of the mission, so the possibility of characterization does not arise at all. Likewise in the Hashiyah. And the well-known answer can be directed that they do not say there is lack of knowledge of the ruling concerning each particular act and the possibility that it is other than permissibility and prohibition such that establishing them even by general proof would entail combining contradictories; rather, the intent is the lack of knowledge obtained by a specific evidence specified for each act, so they resorted to a general evidence encompassing every act and found it ruling with permissibility or prohibition; then there is no contradiction. So ponder. (Third: Suspense) regarding any of the rulings (because there is a specific ruling from among the five, and it is not known which one occurs) so they suspend. (I say:) This requires suspension in the specific—i.e., the ruling concerning a specific thing—and that does not contradict the ruling in general concerning every act. So ponder. This is something amazing, because the author objected to the first two by insufficiency of generality and specificity in non-contradiction, and here he ruled by non-contradiction due to generality and specificity. Except it may be said that the intent is that suspension in the specific does not contradict the general ruling, even if it is erroneous in ijtihad. The more correct in exposition is to say that the insufficiency of generality and specificity there is because the proponents of the first two schools ruled by permissibility or prohibition absolutely, and it is assumed that there is no knowledge of the specific ground for each particular act; so it is possible that in some acts there is a ground of beautification that necessitates obligation known to God, and the law would come accordingly if it came, as came in the fasting of the last day of blessed Ramadan; thus obligation would combine with prohibition or permissibility, and generality and specificity do not help. With this, the refutation of the previously mentioned answer becomes apparent. The purpose of the objection here is that what is assumed is only the lack of knowledge of the specific definite cause in each particular act; it does not preclude knowledge of a specific ruling for an act conforming to what is in reality by deduction upon a universal rule, not that the ruling is known.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh dispute concerning the original ruling of actions before revelation (qabl al-shar'). The commentator expands on the matn of Musallam al-Thubut. There are two main positions: 1) The majority of Hanafis and Shafi'is hold that the original ruling for things other than lives is permissibility (ibahah), while for lives it is prohibition (hurmah). Sadr al-Islam adds a nuance: permissibility in wealth, prohibition in selves. 2) Others hold that the original ruling is absolute prohibition. The commentator then addresses the period of intermission (fatra) when prophethood was absent. He argues that strictly speaking there is no time without a divine law, as per Quranic proofs. Thus the dispute actually centers on the fatra period. Those who consider ignorance during fatra as an excuse adopt original permissibility (ibahah asliyyah), which is really just negation of hardship. Those who see ignorance as no excuse adopt original prohibition as a precaution. The Mu'tazilah are then introduced: they divide acts into voluntary and necessary, and further by the perceived moral qualities (husn/qubh). They have three opinions on acts before revelation: permissibility, prohibition, or suspense. The commentator then presents objections to the first two views, arguing that establishing a general ruling without detailed cause can lead to contradiction. He gives and refutes answers. The third view of suspense is also critiqued: it is consistent with a general ruling but leads to suspension in specifics, which the commentator finds contradictory in the author's own argument. The final analysis clarifies that the problem is lack of knowledge of the specific cause; this does not preclude deducing a specific ruling by a universal rule, but prohibits knowing the actual ruling conclusively. The passage is a rigorous exercise in usul reasoning, illustrating the complexity of pre-revelation legal status.

Key Terms
الإباحة الأصلية (al-ibahah al-asliyyah): The original permissibility of actions before revelation, understood as negation of hardship rather than a positive legal ruling.
الحظر (al-hazr): Prohibition as the original state of things before revelation, based on precaution.
زمان الفترة (zaman al-fatra): The period of intermission between prophets when divine law was forgotten or inaccessible.
الأموال والأنفس (al-amwal wa al-anfus): Wealth and lives; the original ruling differs: ibahah for wealth, hurmah for lives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to most Hanafis and Shafi'is in the passage, original permissibility is understood as:
A) A real permissive ruling from Allah
B) A practical verdict due to ignorance
C) Absolute prohibition
D) Suspense

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds original permissibility as a practical verdict for actions before revelation, limited to the fatra period and excluding matters of lives, based on the excuse of ignorance.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Eternal Speech and Temporal Ruling: The Mu'tazilite Objection Answered
Responding to the Mu'tazilite argument that the definition is flawed because eternal speech cannot produce a temporal ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Mu'tazilite objection that the definition of hukm as divine speech fails because of abrogation, and state the Hanafi-Ash'ari response distinguishing between the eternal ruling and its temporal attachment (ta'alluq).

Core Idea
Abrogation removes the ta'alluq, not the eternal ruling itself — preserving the definition of hukm as divine speech.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazila against the definition of hukm as 'the speech of Allah' (khitab). If the speech is eternal, how can a ruling be temporal, since rulings can be abrogated? The answer lies in a critical distinction: the ruling itself (as an attribute) is eternal; what changes is only the attachment (ta'alluq) of that ruling to specific acts. This distinction defends the definition and reveals a profound insight into the nature of divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim: they argue that since the ruling can be abrogated (cease to exist), it must be temporal, whereas the speech of Allah is eternal. Thus the ruling cannot be equated with speech, making the definition invalid.
The objection hinges on the fact of abrogation as proof of temporality.
2
Understand the Hanafi-Ash'ari Response
The response does not deny abrogation but argues that what is abrogated is not the ruling itself as an eternal attribute, but rather the 'ta'alluq' – the attachment of that ruling to a specific act at a specific time.
The key is that the ruling (hukm) in the definition refers to the eternal attribute, not the temporal effect.
3
Analyze the Key Term: Ta'alluq
Ta'alluq is the relationship between the eternal ruling (as an attribute of Allah) and the act of the responsible individual. This relationship is temporal and can change through abrogation, while the attribute remains unchanged.
This is similar to the difference between the attribute of 'knowledge' and the known objects: knowledge is eternal, but what is known (creatures) is temporal.
4
Apply the Distinction
Re-evaluate the definition of hukm as 'the speech of Allah concerning the acts of the responsible individuals'. Since the speech (as eternal attribute) is the source of the ruling, and the ta'alluq is the temporal manifestation, the definition remains valid. Abrogation only changes the ta'alluq.
Thus the objection is resolved without altering the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني من المعتزلة : أن الخطاب عندكم) معشر أهل السنة (أي الكلام النفسي قديم والحكم حادث لثبوت عدمه بالنسخ وما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه فما لم عدمه لم يثبت قدمه والحكم قد ثبت عدمه فهو حادث ، فالحكم إذن مباين للخطاب، فلا يمتنع يصح تعريفه به والجواب أن حدوث الحكم غير مسلم بل الحادث هو التعلق) أي تعلق الحكم بالفعل تنجيزاً (فافهم) فإنه ظاهر جداً
The second investigation: from the Mu‘tazila: that the speech, according to you (Ahl al-Sunnah), i.e., the internal speech, is eternal, and the ruling is temporally originated because its non-existence is established by abrogation; whatever is eternal is impossible to be non-existent; and a thing whose non-existence is not established has not proven its eternity; and the ruling has proven its non-existence, so it is originated. Thus the ruling is distinct from speech, so it is not impossible that the definition by it is sound. The answer: that the origination of the ruling is not conceded; rather, the originated thing is the ta‘alluq, i.e., the attachment of the ruling to the act in a determinate manner. So understand, for it is very clear.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This is a dispute passage: the Mu'tazilites argue that if the definition of hukm is 'speech of Allah', but that speech (internal) is eternal, while the actual judicial ruling (hukm) can be abrogated and therefore temporal, then the ruling is distinct from speech and cannot be defined by it. The Hanafi-Ash'ari response is that the hukm itself (as the eternal attribute of Allah) is not temporal; what is temporal is the ta'alluq (relation/attachment) of that ruling to a particular act. Thus the definition remains valid.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي (al-kalam al-nafsi): المعنى القائم بالنفس القديم
التعلق (al-ta'alluq): ارتباط الحكم بالمكلف أو الفعل في وقت معين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do the Hanafi/Ash'ari respond to the Mu'tazilite claim that the ruling must be temporal since it can be abrogated?
A) They deny abrogation is possible
B) They argue that abrogation only removes the ta'alluq, not the eternal ruling itself
C) They agree that the ruling is temporal but still defined by eternal speech
D) They redefine speech as temporal

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Ash'ari distinction between the eternal ruling (as attribute) and its temporal attachment (ta'alluq) resolves the apparent conflict between the timeless nature of divine speech and the temporality of legal rulings subject to abrogation.

The Traveler's Intention in Ramadan: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi usul dispute on traveler's intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the usul principle underlying the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the traveler's intention in Ramadan, and evaluate the tarjih.

Core Idea
The traveler's license (rukhsah) transforms Ramadan into Sha'ban for him, so his intention for nafl does not fulfill the obligation according to Abu Hanifa.

In the previous lesson, we discussed the general rule that intending an act of worship different from the obligatory fast does not fulfill it. However, the traveler and sick person present an exception according to Imam Abu Hanifa. This lesson unpacks the internal Hanafi dispute on whether a traveler who intends a supererogatory fast on a day of Ramadan satisfies the obligation. The debate turns on how the license to break fast (rukhsah) interacts with the obligatory nature of the day.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
State the specific question: Does a traveler who intends a nafl fast on a day of Ramadan fulfill the Ramadan obligation? According to Abu Hanifa, no. According to the Sahibayn, yes.
2
Locate each position's usul basis
Abu Hanifa argues that the rukhsah makes Ramadan like Sha'ban for the traveler, so the day does not have the attribute of obligation for him. The Sahibayn argue that the day can only accommodate one fast, and that fast is the Ramadan obligation; intention does not change reality.
The usul principle: effect of a license on the general obligation.
3
Evaluate the tarjih
The text states that the view of the two companions is 'more correct' (الأشبه بالصواب). Note that there is a second narration from Abu Hanifa that aligns with the Sahibayn.
Tarjih mechanism: later Hanafi authorities favour the Sahibayn's view.
4
Apply to similar cases (sick person)
For the sick person, there is further ikhtilaf. Some equate him to the traveler (Karkhi, Sahib al-Hidayah), while Ibn al-Humam distinguishes based on the type of illness. Note the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا بنية المسافر) عند الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى لا يكفي لأداء الفرض إذا كان بنية نفل (للترخص) فرمضان في حقه كشعبان، فلا يتأدى بنية واجب آخر، ولا نفل في رواية، لذلك ولأنه لما رخص الفطر المصالح بدنه فالأولى أن يرخص لمصالح دينه بدلالة النص، ومن جملة مصالح دينه أن يفرغ أداء واجب آخر، وعلى هذا فيصح الفرض بنية النفل، فإن مصلحة دينه تقتضي أن يقع عن الفرض، وهذه رواية أخرى أفتى بها ورد الشيخ ابن الهمام هذا الوجه بأن الترخص يوجب أن يكون رمضان في حقه كشعبان، بل هو يوجب حل الفطر فحسب، وأما عدم اتساع الوقت صوماً آخر ،فبحاله كما يدل عليه الحديث المروي بعمومه وهذا كلام حق، وإن شئت فشيد أركانه بما ذكر سابقاً إن هذا اليوم كان لا يسع إلا صوماً واحداً، وقد جعله الشرع بصفة الفرضية، وليس صوم آخر حتى يجوز اتصافه بوصف، سواء كان حلالاً أو حراماً، والوجه الثاني أيضاً ضعيف، إذ لا شركة في المناط، فإن اعتبار مصالح البدن ممكن بتجويز الإفطار، فإن المحل صالح له بخلاف مصلحة أداء واجب آخر، فإنها غير ممكنة، إذا المحل ليس صالحاً لصوم آخر غير الفرض كما علمت وأما المريض فقد اضطربت الأقوال فيه، ففي كشف المنار أنه يقع عن الفرض، إذ لا ترخص إلا بحقيقة العجز وإذا صام بان أن لا عجز ولا يخفى ما فيه فإن الرخصة منوطة بظن زيادة المرض أو تحققها، وإذا صام وازداد مرضه فينبغي أن يجوز عن واجب آخر عنده قال الشيخ ابن الهمام وحقق في المريض تفصيل بين أن يضره فتتعلق الرخصة بخوف الزيادة، فهو كالمسافر، وإن كان المرض لفساد الهضم فبتحققها، فإن صام بنية النذر وقع عن الفرض، وفي الشق الثاني أيضاً خفاء، كما إذا صام صاحب المرض الثاني واستضر به وزاد مرضه ينبغي أن لا يقع عن فرض الوقت، وروى الإمام أبو الحسن الكرخي أن المريض والمسافر سيان، وهو مختار صاحب «الهداية»، هذا كله على رأيه، وأما عندهما فالصحيح والمريض والمقيم والمسافر سواسية في كفاية مطلق النية، ونية المباين لأداء صوم الوقت، وهو الأشبه بالصواب كما قررنا . ثم اعلم أنه نقل عن المصنف أن المريض الذي لا يضره الصوم والملك المرفه المسافر سواء في عدم المشقة، فتجويز الإفطار لأحدهما دون الآخر تحكم وليس كذلك، فإن الأحاديث الصحيحة قد دلت على عدم اعتبار المشقة في رخصة السفر وانعقد الإجماع حتى لم يرو خلاف فيه فأبقى السفر على إطلاقه بخلاف المرض، فإن الظاهر أن ترخيص الإفطار فيه للمضرة، كيف وقلما ينجو الإنسان عن المرض
Except for the intention of the traveler: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, it does not suffice for performing the obligation if his intention is for a supererogatory act, because of the license (for concessions). So Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban, so it is not performed by an intention for another obligation, nor for a supererogatory act in one narration. For that reason and because when he is permitted to break his fast for the interests of his body, it is more appropriate that he be permitted for the interests of his religion, by indication of the text. And among the interests of his religion is that he free his liability from the obligation. So based on this, the obligation is valid with an intention for supererogation, for the interest of his religion requires that it fall for the obligation. This is another narration which is the basis for fatwa. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam rejected this view: the license makes Ramadan like Sha'ban for him; rather, it only permits breaking the fast. As for the fact that the time cannot accommodate another fast, it remains as it is, as indicated by the hadith narrated generally, and this is correct. If you wish, strengthen its pillars by what was mentioned earlier: this day cannot accommodate more than one fast, and the Law has made it with the attribute of obligation, and there is no other fast for it to be described with a description, whether permissible or prohibited. The second view is also weak, for there is no commonality in the rationale, because consideration of bodily interests is possible by permitting breaking the fast, for the locus is suitable for that, unlike the interest of performing another obligation, for it is not possible since the locus is not suitable for another fast other than the obligatory one, as you know. As for the sick person, the statements have differed. In Kashf al-Manar, it is that it falls for the obligation, for there is no license except with actual incapacity; when he fasts, it becomes clear that there is no incapacity. And what is in it is not hidden: the license is conditional upon the fear of increase of illness or its actual occurrence. If he fasts and his illness increases, then it should be valid for another obligation, according to him. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam detailed for the sick person, distinguishing between: if fasting harms him, the license is attached to fear of increase, so he is like the traveler; if the illness is due to poor digestion, then it is attached to actual harm. If he fasts with the intention of a vow, it falls for the obligation. In the second case, there is also obscurity: if the person with the second illness fasts and is harmed and his illness increases, it should not fall for the obligation of the time. Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi narrated that the sick and the traveler are the same, and this is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah. All this is according to his opinion. As for the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the healthy, sick, resident, and traveler are all equal in the sufficiency of absolute intention and an intention for a contrary act to perform the fast of the time, and this is more correct as we have established. Then know that it is transmitted from the author that the sick person who is not harmed by fasting and the comfortable king are equal in the absence of hardship, so permitting breaking the fast for one and not the other is arbitrary. But it is not so, for authentic hadiths have indicated that hardship is not considered in the license of travel, and consensus has been reached, so travel is left in its absolute form, unlike illness, for the apparent is that the license to break fast in it is due to harm. How? Since man rarely escapes illness.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage addresses the exception to the previous rule: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, a traveler who intends a supererogatory fast on a day of Ramadan does not fulfill the obligation, because the license of travel (rukhsah) makes Ramadan like Sha'ban for him, meaning he has the option to break his fast and make it up later. Therefore, his intention for a nafl fast does not automatically become the obligatory fast. There is a second narration that allows it. The author discusses Ibn al-Humam's refutation. For the sick person, there are multiple views: some equate him to the traveler, while others distinguish based on the type of illness. Imam Abu Hanifa's two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that all persons are the same: absolute intention suffices. The author concludes with a critique of the claim that the sick and the traveler are similar, arguing that travel license is based on the act of traveling itself, not on hardship, while illness license is based on harm, so they are different.

Key Terms
الترخص (al-tarakhkhus): The license or concession given to travelers to break the fast
المسافر (al-musafir): A traveler who meets the conditions for qasr and rukhsa
المريض (al-marid): A sick person whose illness permits breaking the fast
الأشبه بالصواب (al-ashbah bi al-sawab): Closer to correctness; the preferred view in the Hanafi school

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view is considered 'more correct' (الأشبه بالصواب) regarding the traveler's intention?
A) The view that the traveler's nafl intention does not count as Ramadan
B) The view that the traveler's nafl intention counts as Ramadan
C) The view that the traveler must intend both nafl and Ramadan
D) The view that the traveler's fast is invalid regardless of intention

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's preferred view is that any intention for a fast on a day of Ramadan, even from a traveler, fulfills the obligation, because the day can only accommodate one fast.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
