Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Command Implies Prohibition of Opposite
Positions on whether amr entails nahi of its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a command entails prohibition of its opposite and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A command logically requires refraining from its opposite; the prohibition is inherent, not separate.

This lesson examines a core dispute in usul al-fiqh: does commanding an act imply that its opposite is prohibited? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on logical necessity, while the Mu'tazila and most Shafi'is disagree. Understanding this difference affects how we derive rulings from commands in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the claim: a command (amr) for an obligatory act entails prohibition of all opposites that would eliminate the commanded act. The prohibition is not a separate command but a logical consequence of the original obligation.
The opposite must be mufawwit (eliminating the act).
2
Identify the opponent's view
The Mu'tazila and most Shafi'is hold that a command does not intellectually entail prohibition of the opposite. They argue that commanding an act does not automatically make its opposite forbidden; the opposite may be permissible in itself.
They see the entailment as psychological or conventional, not logical.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi argument: refraining from the opposite is a necessary condition for performing the commanded act. If the opposite were permissible, the act could not be performed because two opposites cannot coexist. Therefore, the obligation of the act necessarily implies the obligation to refrain from its opposite, which is its prohibition.
This is based on the principle of al-malzum wa al-lazim (the implied and its necessary consequence).
4
Examine the response to the opponent's objection
The opponent objects that one can command without explicitly forbidding the opposite. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition is not separate but inherent in the same address; it is a logical entailment, not an independent command.
Text: 'الخطاب هنا واحد بالذات متعلق بالفعل وبالضد'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the opposite is performed, does one incur sin for violating an independent prohibition or for neglecting the command? According to the Hanafi view, the sin is for neglecting the command because the prohibition is derivative; for the opponent, it would be either no sin (if opposite is permissible) or sin for violating a separate prohibition.
Most Hanafis consider performing the opposite of a wajib as sinful due to neglecting the wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ وجوب الشيء وحرمة ضده] (وجوب الشيء يتضمن حرمة ضده المفوّت (وقيل) الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده وقيل الأمر بالشيء نفس النهي عن ضده، فمنهم من عمم في أمر الوجوب والندب، فجعلهما الضد تحريماً وتنزيهاً فالندب إلى الشيء نفس كراهة الضد (ومنهم من خصص) الحكم بأمر الوجوب فليس ضد المندوب مكروهاً (وقيل ليس) الأمر (نهيا) عن الضد (ولا متضمناً عقلاً، وعليه المعتزلة وعامة الشافعية ثم الخلاف في النهي كذلك) فالمختار أنه متضمن للأمر بالضد، وقيل نفس الأمر به سواء كان تحريماً أو تنزيهاً وقيل إذا كان تحريماً فقط، وقيل يقتضي كون الضد بمعنى سنة (إلا) أن الأمر ) بالشيء (نهي عن جميع الأضداد) لأن كل واحد منها مفوّت للواجب المأمور به (بخلاف النهي فإنه أمر بأحد أضداده) مخيراً، وقيل: ليس النهي أمراً بضد ولا متضمناً عقلاً كما في الأمر (وقيل) في النهي (لا) يتضمن الأمر بضد ولا نفسه بخلاف الأمر لنا أن الامتناع عن الضد من لوازم وجوب الفعل) وإلا فإن جاز الإتيان بالضد مع وجود الفعل ارتفع التضاد وإلا ارتفع الوجوب، وإذا كان من لوازم الوجوب فيتحقق وجوب الفعل بتحقق وجوب الامتناع عن الضد، فلزم حرمته بتبعية الوجوب وخطابه، وهو المراد من التضمن، كما أن جعل الملزوم وهو بعينه جعل اللازم، ولا يحتاج إلى جعل مستقل، كذلك لا يحتاج الامتناع عن الضد إلى موجب سوى موجب الفعل، والمصنف أفاد هذا المعنى بالتمثيل وقال : واللوازم مجعولة بجعل الملزوم لا بجعل جديد وإلا تكن كذلك لزم إمكان الانفكاك أي كما أن جعل الملزوم هو جعل اللازم كذلك إيجاب الملزوم هو بعينه إيجاب اللازم وبمثله يقال في النهي) يعني أن الاشتغال بالضد من لوازم كف الفعل، فإذا تحقق إيجاب الكف لا بد من تحقق إيجاب الاشتغال بالضد ولو تخييراً (وفيه شيء) لأن كون الاشتغال بالضد من لوازم الكف عن الفعل ممنوع، فإنه قد يوجد الكف ولا يخطر ضد بالبال، الاشتغال هذا الكف، وليس منه، وكذا ليس من لوازم عدم الفعل فإن العدم ربما يكون من عدم العلة لا بوجود المانع الذي هو الضد فلا يلزم الإيجاب (فالخطاب) هنا (واحد (بالذات متعلق بالفعل وبالضد والتفاوت بالأصالة والتبعية فبالأصالة للواجب وبالتبعية
Issue: The obligation of a thing and the prohibition of its opposite. (The obligation of a thing entails the prohibition of its opposite that renders it impossible (Some said) The command for a thing implies the dislike of its opposite. And some said the command for a thing is the same as the prohibition of its opposite. Some generalized in the case of obligatory and recommended commands, making the opposite forbidden or disliked; so the recommendation of a thing is the same as the dislike of its opposite. (Some specified the ruling to obligatory commands only, so the opposite of a recommended act is not disliked. (And some said) The command is not a prohibition of the opposite, nor does it entail it intellectually. Upon this are the Mu'tazila and most Shafi'is. Then the disagreement is similar in the case of prohibition.) The chosen view is that it entails the command for the opposite; some said it is the same as commanding it, whether the prohibition is for tahrim or tanzih; some said only if it is tahrim; some said it necessitates the opposite being sunna. (Except that) the command for a thing is a prohibition of all its opposites, because each one of them eliminates the commanded obligatory act. (Unlike the prohibition, for it is a command for one of its opposites) by choice. And some said: The prohibition is not a command for an opposite nor does it entail it intellectually as in the case of command. (And some said) regarding prohibition: it does not entail the command for an opposite nor is it the same, unlike the command. (Our proof) is that refraining from the opposite is a necessary consequence of the obligation of the act; otherwise, if performing the opposite were permissible along with the act, the opposition would be removed, or the obligation would be removed. And if it is a necessary consequence of obligation, then the obligation of the act is realized by the realization of the obligation to refrain from the opposite. Therefore its prohibition follows from the obligation and its address. That is what is meant by entailment. Just as making the implied (malzum) is exactly making the necessary (lazim) and does not require a separate making, likewise refraining from the opposite does not require a cause other than the cause of the act. The author conveyed this meaning by example and said: Necessary consequences are made by making the implied, not by a new making; otherwise separation would be possible. Just as making the implied is making the necessary, likewise obligating the implied is exactly obligating the necessary. And the same is said regarding prohibition, meaning that engaging in the opposite is a necessary consequence of refraining from the act. So if the obligation to refrain is realized, the obligation to engage in the opposite must be realized, even if by choice. (But there is something in it) because the claim that engaging in the opposite is a necessary consequence of refraining from the act is denied; for refraining can occur without any opposite coming to mind; engaging (in the opposite) is this refraining, and it is not from it. Likewise, it is not a necessary consequence of the non-existence of the act, for non-existence may be due to the absence of a cause, not due to the presence of a preventer which is the opposite; so obligation does not follow. (So the address) here is one in essence, related to the act and the opposite, and the difference is by originality and following: originally for the obligatory, and by following for the opposite.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on whether commanding a thing implies the prohibition of its opposite. The author presents multiple positions: (1) The command entails the prohibition of the opposite that eliminates it, (2) Some say it implies only dislike (karaha), (3) Some say it is identical to the prohibition, (4) Some say it is only for obligatory commands, (5) Some say it does not entail anything intellectually (Mu'tazila and most Shafi'is). The chosen view is that it does entail prohibition, because refraining from the opposite is a logical necessity of performing the commanded act. The author uses an analogy: just as making the implied (malzum) is the same as making the necessary (lazim), so the obligation of the act is the same as the obligation to refrain from its opposite. So the prohibition of the opposite is not a separate obligation. Similarly, for prohibition, the author holds that it entails a command to do one of the opposite acts by choice. However, a criticism is raised: refraining from an act does not necessarily imply engaging in an opposite; one can simply not do the act without doing anything. The author concludes that the address is one, related to both the act and the opposite, but with different modes: primary for the commanded act, and subsidiary for the opposite.

Key Terms
الضد المفوّت (al-didd al-mufawwit): The opposite that prevents the performance of the commanded act
الملزوم واللازم (al-malzum wa al-lazim): The implied (the act) and its necessary consequence (refraining from opposite)
التضمن (al-tadamman): Entailment; the inclusion of one ruling within another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in this passage, why does commanding a thing entail prohibition of its opposite?
A) Because the opposite is always haram.
B) Because refraining from the opposite is a necessary consequence of performing the act.
C) Because the command text explicitly mentions the prohibition.
D) Because the Prophet said so.

Key Takeaway
A command logically entails the prohibition of its opposite that eliminates it, because performing the commanded act requires refraining from that opposite.

Combining Wajib and Haram: The Case of Prayer in a Usurped House
Dispute over combining wajib and haram: salat in usurped house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the combination of wajib and haram in a single act, explain the concept of ta'addud al-jihah, and apply it to the case of salat in a usurped house.

Core Idea
An act can be both wajib and haram from different aspects; the combination is only impossible if aspects are unified.

This passage tackles a foundational usul al-fiqh question: can a single action be simultaneously commanded and prohibited? The Hanafi school, along with the majority (Shafi'i, Maliki), affirms this possibility through the concept of multiplicity of aspects (ta'addud al-jihah). The classic illustration is performing prayer in a usurped house: the act is wajib insofar as it is prayer, and haram insofar as it involves unlawful occupation. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the Hanafi reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Understand why combining wajib and haram in one act appears impossible if considered from one aspect: two opposites cannot coexist.
The initial claim in the text is that such taklif is impossible.
2
Recognize the concept of ta'addud al-jihah
The Hashiyah clarifies that if there is multiplicity of aspects, the locus of obligation and prohibition is different, so no genuine contradiction arises.
This is the key usul principle.
3
Apply to the case of salat in dar maghsubah
The act is one in person but multiple in aspect: as prayer it is wajib, as usurpation it is haram. The performer is both rewarded and punished accordingly.
This is the majority (Hanafi, Shafi'i, Maliki) position.
4
Compare with opposing views
Baqillani, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and others hold the combination invalid. Understand their proof and the Hanafi response.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the obligation is fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ترقى وقال : (بل تكليفه محال لأنه يلزم من هذا التكليف اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة في شيء واحد ذاتاً وجهة فيكون واجباً حراماً، وهو جمع بين الضدين في نفس الأمر . قال في الحاشية) : وفيه ما فيه فإنه إنما يتم إذا لم يكن تعدد جهة أصلاً، وأما إذا كان تعدد جهات متساوية فمحل الوجوب والحرمة مختلف فلا اجتماع للمتنافيين، نعم : لا يمكن الامتثال حينئذ، فالتكليف تكليف بالمحال لا تكليف محال فتدبر أو تتعدد الجهة حقيقة وحكماً، بحيث يمكن الافتراق من أحدهما كالصلاة في الدار المغصوبة، فعند الجمهور) من الحنفية والشافعية والمالكية (يصح) هذا النحو من التكليف، فالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة واجب حرام معاً، فالأتي بها يستحق ثواب الصلاة وعقاب الغصب (وقال القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني : (لا : (لا يصح ويسقط به أي بالفعل الذي شأنه هذا الطلب واستبعده الإمام الرازي صاحب المحصول فإن سقوط الطلب إما بالامتثال أو النسخ، وكلاهما منتف (وعند) الإمام (أحمد بن حنبل وأكثر المتكلمين) (والجبائي والروافض : (لا يصح هذا النحو من التكليف (ولا يسقط) به الواجب ( لنا) أنه لا مانع بتخيل اجتماع وصفين متضادين، وهو غير مانع إذ (عدم اتحاد المتعلقين) لهما (حقيقة) ثابت ها هنا فإن الكون في الحيز وإن كان كوناً واحداً بالشخص لكنه متعدد باعتبار أنه كون من حيث إنه صلاة) وعبادة الله تعالى (وكون من حيث إنه غصب وتعد على ملك الغير فبالجهة الأولى يكون واجباً وبالجهة الثانية حرام، فلا اتحاد في
Then he progressed and said: (Rather, his assignment [taklif] is impossible because from this assignment follows the combination of obligation and prohibition in one thing in essence and aspect, so it would be both obligatory and forbidden, which is a combination of two opposites in reality. He said in the Hashiyah: There is something in it, for it is only complete if there is no multiplicity of aspect at all. But if there is multiplicity of equal aspects, then the locus of obligation and prohibition is different, so there is no combination of opposites. Yes: in that case, compliance is not possible, so the assignment is an assignment of the impossible, not an impossible assignment. So reflect. Or the aspect is multiple both in reality and in judgment, such that separation from one of them is possible, like prayer in the usurped house. According to the majority) of Hanafis, Shafi'is, and Malikis (it is valid) this type of assignment. So prayer in usurped land is both obligatory and forbidden simultaneously; the one who performs it deserves the reward of prayer and the punishment of usurpation. (And Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: No, it is not valid, and it is dropped by it, i.e., by the act that is the subject of this demand. And Imam al-Razi, author of al-Mahsul, deemed this unlikely, for the dropping of a demand is either by compliance or abrogation, both of which are absent. (And according to) Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal and most theologians, and al-Jubba'i and the Rawafid: (it is not valid) this type of assignment (and does not drop) the obligation by it. (For us) there is no obstacle in imagining the combination of two opposing attributes, and it is not an obstacle because (the non-unity of the two objects) for them (in reality) is established here. For the being in the space, even if it is one being in person, it is multiple from the consideration that it is a being from the perspective of being prayer and worship of Allah, and a being from the perspective of being usurpation and transgression against the property of another. So from the first aspect it is obligatory, and from the second aspect it is forbidden. Therefore there is no unity in...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul_dispute) over the permissibility of combining wajib and haram in one individual act from a single aspect. The text initially states that such a combination is impossible, but then the Hashiyah clarifies that if there is multiplicity of aspects, it is possible, though compliance may be impossible. The key example is prayer in a usurped house (salat fi'l-dar al-maghsubah). The majority of Hanafis, Shafi'is, and Malikis hold that this is valid: the action is both wajib and haram from different aspects—wajib because of the command to pray, haram because of the prohibition of using others' property. The one performing it deserves reward for the prayer and punishment for the usurpation. Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and others (Imam Ahmad, most theologians, al-Jubba'i, the Rawafid) hold that it is invalid and does not fulfill the obligation. The Hanafi proof is that the two subject matters (prayer and usurpation) are not united in reality; the being in the space is one in person but multiple in aspect. The passage concludes that there is no unity, implying validity.

Key Terms
الصلاة في الدار المغصوبة (al-salatu fi al-dari al-maghsubah): Performing prayer in a place that has been unlawfully taken from its owner; a classic case of conflicting rulings.
تعدد الجهة (ta'addud al-jihah): Multiplicity of aspects or considerations, allowing the same action to be ruled differently from different perspectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the ruling for someone who prays in a usurped house?
A) The prayer is invalid and the person is not rewarded.
B) The prayer is valid, and the person deserves reward for prayer and punishment for usurpation.
C) The prayer is neither obligatory nor forbidden because of conflicting aspects.
D) The person must repeat the prayer in a lawful place to fulfill the obligation.

Key Takeaway
The concept of ta'addud al-jihah allows an act to be wajib and haram from different aspects, resolving apparent contradictions and enabling valid taklif.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Coexistence of Obligation and Prohibition: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
A usul dispute on the coexistence of obligation and prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of multiple legal aspects (jihāt) to distinguish when obligation and prohibition can coexist, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument using the example of prayer in usurped land.

Core Idea
Prayer in usurped land: valid yet sinful — obligation and prohibition can coexist when they arise from different legal aspects.

In this passage, we encounter one of the most famous usul disputes between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: can a single act be both obligatory and prohibited? At first glance, this seems contradictory. However, the Hanafi school, through its sophisticated theory of 'jihāt' (legal aspects), argues that if the obligation and prohibition attach to different dimensions of the act, coexistence is possible. The key example is performing salah in a usurped land. This lesson will walk you through the argument, the opposing view, and how the Hanafis defend their position using textual proofs and logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is whether wujub (obligation) and hurma (prohibition) can coexist in a single act. The Hanafis say yes if they arise from different jihāt; the Shafi'is say no.
The jiha is the legal aspect, e.g., the act of praying vs. the act of usurping.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis use an analogy: a master commands his slave to sew and forbids travel. If the slave sews while traveling, he is obedient in sewing and disobedient in travel. Similarly, the command to pray is absolute, while the prohibition of usurpation targets a different aspect.
The hadith and analogy show that two rulings can apply simultaneously when their causes (asbāb) are distinct.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'is argue that the prohibition of being in usurped land indicates that the commanded prayer is not this particular prayer, so the salah is invalid. They cite hadith forbidding fasting on Eid days and the principle that a vow to sin is void.
The objection is that the prohibition negates the obligation because the act is inherently sinful.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the prohibition on Eid fasting is a specific preventer (māniʿ) due to the sanctity of the day, not a general principle. The essence of fasting is not sinful; the sin is from a concomitant aspect (turning away from hospitality). Therefore, the vow is valid, and the coexistence principle holds.
The Hanafi response distinguishes between essential and accidental aspects, preserving the validity of the act while acknowledging sin.
5
Apply the principle to the classic example
Salah in usurped land: the obligation of salah comes from the absolute command to pray; the prohibition comes from the act of usurpation. Since these are different aspects, the salah is valid (obligation fulfilled) but the person is sinful (prohibition violated).
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the practical difference in ruling between the schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتعلقين أصلاً، فلا استحالة (قيل) في حواشي ميرزاجان» لا نسلم أن الكون في المغصوب من حيث إنه صلاة واجب حتى يجتمع الوجوب مع الحرمة وإنما يكون واجباً لو تناوله الأمر بالصلاة، وهو ممنوع، إذ النهي عن الكون في المكان المغصوب يدل على أن الكون المطلوب في الأمر بالصلاة غيره وهذا ظاهر كلام القائل، ويمكن أن يقرر معارضة بأنها لا تصح، لأن المطلوب غير هذه الصلاة والمخصص النهي عن الكون في المغصوب، ويلائم المعارضة قوله : (أقول) في الجواب (الدلالة) أي دلالة النهي عن الكون في المغصوب على أن الصلاة المطلوبة غير الصلاة فيه ممنوعة فإنها فرع التضاد بين النهي المذكور والأمر المذكور (وإذا جوزنا الاجتماع بينهما (نظراً إلى أن الأمر مطلق كما هو حقيقته مع تغاير الجهة والحمل على الحقيقة ضروري إذا لم يصرف صارف فأين الدلالة على عدم تناول الأمر لهذه الصلاة، وإن حرر الإيراد منعاً فتقرير الجواب أن الكون في المغصوب من حيث إنه صلاة واجب البتة، لأن الأمر الصلاتي طالب لمطلق الصلاة فإنه مطلق والتقييد لا بد له من صارف، وليس يتخيل إلا النهي عن الغصب ولا يصلح مقيداً إلا إذا دل على الفساد والدلالة فرع التضاد، وهو باطل لتعدد الجهة فافهم فصار) ما نحن فيه (كما إذا أمر عبده بالخياطة ونهى عن السفر فخاط وسافر فإنه مطيع) في الخياطة (وعاص) في السفر (قطعاً) كذا هذا والنقض بصوم) يوم (النحر) بأنه إذا نذر صوم يوم النحر يجب أن يصح، إذ الحرمة بجهة كونه في يوم النحر، والوجوب من جهة كونه صوماً منذوراً، فصار مثل الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة (مدفوع بأن التخلف) أي تخلف حكم الصلاة في المكان المغصوب (ممنوع) ها هنا (فعندنا يخرج عن العهدة بالصوم فيه فيصح الصوم من جهة كونه صوماً منذوراً الله ، ويأثم من جهة كونه في يوم النحر وإعراضاً عن ضيافة الله تعالى فإن قلت ينبغي أن لا يصح هذا النذر فإنه معصية، والنذر بالمعصية باطل .
أما الأول : فلما روى الشيخان عن أبي سعيد الخدري : لا يصح الصيام في يومين يوم الأضحى والفطر، والطبراني عن ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وسلم أرسل أيام منى صائحاً يصيح أن لا تصوموا هذه الأيام فإنها أيام أكل وشرب. وأما الثاني : فلما روى أصحاب السنن عن أم المؤمنين عائشة الصديقة رضي الله عنها : لا نذر في معصية وكفارته كفارة يمين وإذا لم ينعقد لم يتحقق الوجوب، قلت: لا نذر ها هنا بالمعصية، فإن الناذر ، إنما ينذر الصوم وذاته ليست ،معصية، وإنما المعصية في أمر مقارن له وهو الإعراض والنهي يقرر المشروعية وألفاظ أبي سعيد رضي الله عنه مختلفة، ففي بعضها نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ولو سلم عدم الصحة كما هو رأي الشافعية (فهو لمانع) عندهم، والتخلف لمانع لا يضر مقصودنا، وهو جواز اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة لأجل جهتين (وهو) الفساد أي المانع النهي الدال على فساد الصوم فيه فإن النهي عندهم يوجب وعدم مشروعية الذات بخلاف النهي عن الغصب فإنه لا يدل على فساد الصلاة إذ لم يرد النهي عنها بخصوصها (والجواب بتخصيص الدعوى أي دعوى الاجتماع (بما إذا كان بينهما عموم من وجه وها هنا ليس كذلك، فإن الصوم لا ينفك عن صوم النحر (لا يدفع النقض عن عموم الدليل) فإن مقدمات الدليل جارية فيه، إذ لا تضاد عند اختلاف الجهتين، فإن قلت يرد النقض على عموم الدليل بما إذا كان بين الجهتين تساو، فإن اعتذر بأن إيجاب ما لا يخلو عن الحرام لا فائدة فيه ولا يليق بشأن الحكيم يعتذر به فيما إذا كان اللزوم من جانب، فإن إيجاب شيء مع لزوم الحرمة لا فائدة فيه مع أن إيفاء هذا النذر والمنهي عنه متلازمان، قلت: لا نقض بمتساوي الجهتين فإنه يلزم فيه الإتيان بكل، لأنه واجب أو لازم واجب، والاجتناب عن كل لأنه حرام أو لازم ،حرام، ففيه تكليف بالمستحيل بخلاف ما إذا كان اللزوم من جانب، فإنه يجوز أن يكون جهة الوجوب أمراً عاماً يتحقق امتثاله في غير الخاص فلا تكليف بالمحال؛ نعم : لو كان جهة الحرمة عامة وجهة الوجوب جهة خاصة لزم الاستحالة، لكنا لا نقول بجوازه، ونحن إنما نقول بوجوب صوم النحر لأنه منذور من غير لحاظ إلى خصوص مادة، وحرمته من حيث اشتماله على الأعراض عن ضيافة الله فلا تساوي، وإن اعتبر التساوي على هذا الوجه فالكون الصلاتي في هذا المكان ملازم للغصب وبالعكس، فتأمل وأنصف لعل الحق لا يتجاوز عما ذكرنا (إلا أن يقال : العام المطلق لا حقيقة له في التحصيل إلا حقيقة الخاص لاتحاد الجعل والوجود، فإذا كان جهتا الوجوب والحرمة أعم وأخص كان تحصلهما واحداً (فيلزم اجتماع الحسن والقبح في الحقيقة المتحصلة وفي العموم من وجه حقيقتان) اجتماعهما اتفاقي فلا يلزم من كونهما منشأ الوجوب والحرمة اجتماعهما في ذات (فتأمل) فإنه غير واف، لأن ما ذكر إنما يتم فيما إذا كان العام ذاتياً للخاص وأما إذا كان عرضياً فلا، لأن تحصل الخاص غير تحصل العام العرضي كذا في الحاشية»، ثم ها هنا وجه آخر لفساد هذا ، التوجيه هو أن اجتماع الحسن والقبح في ذات محصلة إنما يستحيل إذا كانت هي المعروضة لهما بالذات، وهو ممنوع، بل المعروض بالذات الجهة العامة والخاصة فلا استحالة، قال في الحاشية» : إذا كان اللزوم ولو من جانب لزم استحالة التكليف بالمحال، وإن لم يلزم استحالة اجتماع المتنافيين فإن الحرمة تقتضي الاجتناب دائماً، والوجوب الإتيان بالفعل، والدائمة والمطلقة متناقضان وهذا غير واف، فإن الجهة العامة إذا كانت جهة وجوب كما فيما نحن فيه لا يلزم فإن الوجوب إنما يقتضي الإتيان في مادة من المواد والحرمة تقتضي الاجتناب عن الأخص ، نعم، لو كان جهة العموم جهة الحرمة لكان له وجه على أنَّا نقول : لا يلزم هذا إلا إذا ألزمنا الأداء من جهة الحرمة، وهو ممنوع ، بل الوجوب إنما هو ليأتي بالقضاء في وقت لا تكون هذه الجهة المحرمة، وإنما يصح معها لأنه أدى كما اشتغلت الذمة ناقصاً، لكن لارتكاب المنهي عنه ها هنا يكون ،آثماً وبهذا يندفع أن إيجاب أمر يلازم القبح لا يليق بجناب الحكيم، حدث العبد، كإيجاب هذا

مما يلزم
... as a matter of principle, there is no impossibility. (It was said) in the glosses of Mirzajan: We do not accept that being in the usurped place, insofar as it is prayer, is obligatory such that obligation and prohibition can coexist. Rather, it would only be obligatory if the command to prayer included it, which is prevented, because the prohibition against being in the usurped place indicates that the being required by the command to prayer is something else. This is apparent from the speaker's words. It is possible to formulate an objection that the prayer is invalid because the required prayer is different, and the specificator is the prohibition against being in the usurped place. The objection is supported by his saying: (I say) in the answer: (The indication) i.e., the indication of the prohibition against being in the usurped place that the required prayer is other than the prayer in it is denied, because it is a branch of the contradiction between the mentioned prohibition and the mentioned command. (And if we permit coexistence between them, considering that the command is absolute as per its true nature, with different aspects, and taking the literal meaning is necessary if there is no diverting factor, then where is the indication that the command does not encompass this prayer? And if the objection is refined as a denial, then the answer is that being in the usurped place, insofar as it is prayer, is absolutely obligatory, because the command to prayer demands absolute prayer; it is absolute, and restriction necessarily requires a diverting factor, which can only be the prohibition against usurpation, but it is not valid as a restrictor unless it indicates invalidity, and indication is a branch of contradiction, which is invalid because of multiple aspects. So understand: so our case becomes like when a master commands his slave to sew and forbids him to travel; then he sews and travels: he is obedient in sewing and disobedient in travel, definitely.) This is it. And the objection by the fast of the day of Nahr (sacrifice) — that if one vows to fast the day of Nahr, it should be valid, since the prohibition is due to its being the day of Nahr and the obligation is due to its being a vowed fast, so it is like prayer in usurped land — (is repelled by the fact that the failure) i.e., the failure of the ruling of prayer in the usurped place) is denied here. (According to us, one fulfills the obligation by fasting on it; so the fast is valid insofar as it is a vowed fast, and one sins insofar as it is on the day of Nahr and a turning away from God's hospitality.) If you say: This vow should not be valid because it is a sin, and a vow involving sin is void. First: Because the two Shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim) narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: Fasting on two days is not valid: the day of Adha and the day of Fitr. And al-Tabarani narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of God sent a caller during the days of Mina proclaiming: Do not fast these days, for they are days of eating and drinking. Second: Because the authors of the Sunan narrated from Umm al-Mu'minin Aisha: No vow in sin, and its expiation is the expiation of an oath. And if the vow is not contracted, obligation is not realized. I say: This is not a vow in sin, for the vower only vows fasting, and its essence is not a sin; the sin is in a concomitant matter, namely turning away. The prohibition establishes legality. The words of Abu Sa'id vary; in some versions the Prophet forbade it. Even if we accept invalidity as the Shafi'is hold (it is due to a preventer), and failure due to a preventer does not harm our purpose, which is the permissibility of coexistence of obligation and prohibition for two aspects. (And that) is the invalidity, i.e., the preventer is the prohibition indicating the invalidity of the fast therein, because according to them the prohibition necessitates the lack of legality of the essence itself, unlike the prohibition against usurpation, which does not indicate the invalidity of prayer, as the prohibition is not specifically addressed to it. (And the answer is by restricting the claim) i.e., the claim of coexistence (to the case where there is general-specific relationship between them, and this is not the case here, for fasting cannot be separated from the day of Nahr. This does not repudiate the objection to the generality of the proof, for the premises of the proof apply here, since there is no contradiction when the aspects differ. If you say: The objection applies to the generality of the proof when the two aspects are equal. If one excuses by saying that imposing something not free from the forbidden is useless and unbefitting of the Wise, that excuse applies when the implication is from one side. For imposing something with concomitant prohibition has no benefit, and fulfilling this vow and the forbidden are inseparable. I say: There is no objection when the two aspects are equal, because then one must do each (since it is obligatory or necessarily obligatory) and avoid each (since it is forbidden or necessarily forbidden), resulting in an impossible obligation. Unlike when the implication is from one side, because the aspect of obligation can be a general matter whose compliance can be realized in something other than the specific, so there is no impossible obligation. Yes, if the aspect of prohibition were general and the aspect of obligation specific, impossibility would follow, but we do not say it is permissible. We only say that the fast of Nahr is obligatory because it is vowed without considering the specific essence, and its prohibition is due to its including turning away from God's hospitality, so they are not equal. If equality is considered in this way, then the spatial being in prayer in this place is inseparable from usurpation and vice versa. Reflect and be just; perhaps the truth does not go beyond what we have mentioned. (Unless it is said: The absolute general has no reality in actualization except the reality of the specific due to the unity of the ruling and existence. So if the two aspects of obligation and prohibition are more general and more specific, their actualization is one, leading to the coexistence of good and evil in the actualized reality. In general-specific from one aspect, there are two realities; their coexistence is incidental, so it does not follow that they cause obligation and prohibition to coexist in one essence. (Reflect) for it is insufficient, because what was mentioned only holds when the general is essential to the specific, not when it is accidental, because the actualization of the specific is different from the actualization of the accidental general, according to the gloss. Then there is another aspect of the corruption of this direction: that the coexistence of good and evil in an actualized essence is impossible only if it is the locus of both in essence, which is denied. Rather, the essential locus is the general and specific aspects, so there is no impossibility. He said in the gloss: If the implication is even from one side, an impossible obligation arises, even if the impossibility of coexistence of opposites does not arise. The prohibition requires constant avoidance, and the obligation requires performing the act; constant and absolute are contradictory. This is insufficient, because when the general aspect is the aspect of obligation, as in our case, it does not follow. The obligation only requires performance in some matter, and the prohibition requires avoidance of the specific. Yes, if the general aspect were the aspect of prohibition, it would have some basis. But we say: This does not follow unless we require performance due to the aspect of prohibition, which is denied. Rather, the obligation is only to perform the make-up in a time when this prohibited aspect is not present, and it is only valid with it because one has performed as the obligation was initially deficient, but due to committing the prohibited act, one becomes sinful. And by this it is refuted that imposing something that accompanies evil is unbefitting of the Wise. The slave's action, like imposing this, is an incitement to sin. Similarly, because imposing something by the servant's making of the fast upon the existence of the vow from the servant is befitting of the Wise, and it is not incitement to sin, for it is an obligation upon the occurrence of an act that was not previously necessary. So reflect. Then here the two aspects of obligation and prohibition are not one and the same; rather, there is a general-specific relationship from one aspect. For the aspect of obligation (the vowed nature) and the aspect of prohibition (the day's sanctity) are such that the obligation is general (applies to any day) and the prohibition is specific (to this particular day).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute focusing on the principle of whether obligation (wujub) and prohibition (hurma) can coexist in a single act. The Hanafi position, defended here, is that they can, provided the two rulings arise from different legal aspects (jihāt). The author begins with the classic example of performing salah in a usurped land. The opponent objects: the prohibition against usurpation indicates that the commanded prayer is not this particular prayer, so the act is invalid. The Hanafi response uses the example of a master commanding his slave to sew and forbidding him to travel. The slave sews while traveling: he is obedient in sewing (fulfilling the command) and disobedient in traveling (violating the prohibition). Both apply because they involve different aspects. Similarly, salah is commanded absolutely; the prohibition is on the act of usurpation, not on the prayer as such. The text then addresses a counterexample: fasting on the day of Nahr (sacrifice). If one vows to fast on that day, the opponent argues the vow is invalid because the act is sinful. The Hanafi scholar replies that the fast is valid as a vowed act (obligation from the vow) but sinful due to turning away from God's hospitality on that day. The hadith evidence from Abu Sa'id and Aisha is examined: the prohibition of fasting on Eid days is a specific preventer (māni'), not a universal proof against coexistence. The discussion deepens into the logic of general and specific aspects. The author distinguishes between cases where the general and specific are essentially linked (causing impossibility) versus accidental (allowing coexistence). He concludes that in the prayer case, the two aspects are not essentially tied, so coexistence is possible. The Hanafi position is thus maintained: salah in a usurped land is valid (obligation met) but sinful (prohibition violated).

Key Terms
عام مطلق (ʿāmm muṭlaq): A general absolute term, unrestricted in scope, as in the command to prayer (ʿāmm is not limited to any specific instance).
خاص (khāṣṣ): A specific term or instance, such as the specific prohibition of being in a usurped place.
جهة (jiha): Legal aspect or dimension from which a ruling arises, e.g., the aspect of prayer vs. the aspect of usurpation.
وجوب (wujūb): The legal quality of being obligatory, arising from a command (amr).
حرمة (ḥurma): The legal quality of being prohibited, arising from a prohibition (nahy).
اجتماع (ijtimāʿ): Coexistence of two rulings (e.g., wujūb and ḥurma) in one act or subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position discussed in this passage, what is the ruling on performing salah in a usurped land?
A) The salah is invalid and sinful.
B) The salah is valid but sinful.
C) The salah is valid and not sinful.
D) The salah is invalid but not sinful.

Key Takeaway
Prayer in a usurped land is valid but sinful because the obligation and prohibition attach to different legal aspects, allowing their coexistence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
15 min
Commanding the Impossible: Hanafi vs. Ash'ari
Refuting Ash'ari objections to the Hanafi position on taklif al-muhal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five Ash'ari objections to the Hanafi position on taklif al-muhal, state each Hanafi rebuttal, and apply the distinction between conceptualizing the impossible absolutely and as occurring.

Core Idea
Commanding the impossible is invalid because it requires conceptualizing the impossible as occurring, which is inherently impossible.

In this passage, the author methodically refutes five Ash'ari objections to the Hanafi principle that commanding the impossible (taklif al-muhal) is invalid. The debate hinges on the nature of command: is conceptualization of the commanded act's occurrence necessary? The Hanafi school, following rational necessity, affirms this requirement. The Ash'ari objections challenge this point, and the text provides detailed responses, culminating in a distinction between conceptualizing the impossible absolutely and conceptualizing it as an occurrence. Understanding this debate sharpens one's grasp of how commands function in Islamic law and the limits of legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanafi premise
Identify the core Hanafi argument: a command is a request for occurrence; requesting occurrence requires conceptualizing that occurrence; the impossible cannot be conceptualized as occurring because it has no essence to be described with existence.
This is the rational proof (dalil aqli) at the heart of the dispute.
2
Examine each of the five Ash'ari objections
For each objection (numbered 1-5), state the objection clearly and then analyze the Hanafi rebuttal. Pay attention to the specific concepts: conceptualization by aspect, the prayer example, the conjunction of opposites, etc.
The objections escalate from denying the necessity of conceptualization to challenging the possibility of conceptualizing the impossible.
3
Distinguish between two types of conceptualization
The key Hanafi response is the distinction between conceptualizing the impossible 'absolutely' (tasawwur mutlaq) and conceptualizing it 'as an occurrence' (tasawwur iqa'). The former is possible (e.g., thinking of a square circle as a concept), but the latter is not because it requires the impossible to be realized externally.
This distinction is used to rebut objections 3, 4, and 5.
4
Analyze the foreknowledge argument
The Ash'aris argue that since Allah knows some acts will not occur, those acts are impossible and thus commanded acts would be impossible. The Hanafi response: knowledge is dependent on the known, not causal; impossibility is inherent in the act itself, not due to knowledge.
This argument is separate from the five objections but reinforces the Hanafi stance.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: a ruler cannot oblige someone to do something intrinsically impossible, such as flying without means. This illustrates the real-world impact of the theoretical debate.
This connects the usul principle to fiqh application.

وقوعه كما طلب، وإلا لما طلب ذلك الشيء بل شيء آخر، وهذا ضروري ثم إن ما ذكره غير واف، فإنا لا نسلم أن علم الشيء بالوجه ليس علماً له أصلاً، كيف والعلم ما به يتميز الشيء عن أغياره والتميز حاصل نعم : هو علم ضعيف حيث لا تدرك الحقيقة ولا تتميز عند الذهن حق التميز، لكن اشتراط الاستدعاء بهذا النحو من الإدراك ممنوع نعم : أصحاب الصورة يقولون : إن العلم بالوجه علم به حقيقة دون صاحبه، لأن الحاصل بالذات صورته، لكنا معشر أهل الحق لا نساعدهم على الصورة، بل العلم عندنا حالة إنجلائية أخرى، ولو تنزلنا قلنا على رأي أصحاب الصورة إن ذا الوجه في علم الشيء بالوجه وإن كان معلوماً بالعرض، لكن هذا العلم العرضي لم لا يكفي للتكليف كيف وقد خرج بهذا العلم عن كونه مجهولاً مطلقاً، ملتفت إليه بالذات والالتفات الذاتي كاف ،البتة :هذا فالصواب أن يجاب بأنه لا بد ها من التصور كما طلب أي ،واقعاً وهذا النحو من التصور بالوجه كان أو بالكنه لا يتصور في المحال، إذ لا حقيقة له يصح اتصافها بالوقوع والوجوه عنوانات فرضية من غير معنون أصلاً (و) قال (ثالثاً) سلمنا ذلك، لكن لا نسلم استحالة تصور المحال واقعاً، بل نقول (إن تصور العقل ماهية المحال متصفة بالوجود في الواقع (سواء اتصفت في الواقع وصدق العلم أم لا) وكذب ليس بمحال بعد كيف وتصور الكواذب لا يستحيل (أقول) في الجواب : إن أراد عدم استحالة التصور مع الغفلة عنها فلا يضر ولا كلام) لنا (مع الغفلة عن الاستحالة، بل المقصود أن المحال من حيث إنه معلوم الاستحالة لا يتصور وجوده إيقاعاً في الخارج) فإنه يرجع إلى تصوره موجوداً أو غير موجود ( فإن الكلام في الطلب الحقيقي) وهو لا يكون إلا بتصور الإيقاع، وأشار إلى هذا الدفع في الدليل بقيد الحيثية في المحال (و) قال (رابعاً إن في الأمر بالصلاة لم يتصورها الآمر متصفة بالوجود في الواقع وإلا انقلب علمه جهلاً (إذ لم توجد الصلاة ( بعد) تعالى الله عن ذلك علواً كبيراً، وحينئذ فقد صح الطلب من غير تصور وقوعه إيقاعاً في الخارج فانتقض مقدمة من دليلكم (أقول) في الجواب لا نسلم عدم تصورها إيقاعاً، بل (تصورها) الآمر على ما ستقع، لأن ماهيتها لا تنافي ثبوتها فلا استحالة في تصورها كذلك، وإن حرر النقض بالعاصي فلا يتوجه هذا الجواب إذ لم يتصور صلاته على ما ستقع، لأنه لا يقع منه شيء، بل الأولى أن يقول يتصور حقيقتها ويصفها بالإيقاع ثم يطلبها ولا يلزم من هذا وقوعها فإن العلم التصوري لا يقتضي وقوع معلومه، وهذا لا يتصور في المحال، إذ ليس حقيقة تتمثل وتوصف بالإيقاع، فإنه لا يصلح الاتصاف به، وأشار إلى هذا الدفع في الاستدلال بمفهوم قوله : وتصور وقوع المحال من حيث هو محال باطل، أي وتصور وقوع الممكن بما هو ممكن صحيح (و) قال خامساً : إن قولنا اجتماع النقيضين محال) قضية موجبة يستلزم تصور المحال الموضوع (مثبتاً ) فأمكن تصور المحال فانتقض قوله : وتصور وقوع المحال الخ (أقول) في الجواب الحكم فيه على الطبيعة باعتبار الفرد كما حققنا في السلم وتقريره أن المحال لا يتصور فلا يحكم عليه لا إيجاباً ولا سلباً، وأما أمثال هذه القضية فالعنوان فيها ممكن عام ليس محالاً فلا يحكم عليه بالاستحالة، لكن يصح الحكم عليه باعتبار موارد تحققه فإن الانتفاء ثابت للعنوان، بمعنى أن موارد تحققه منتفية، وقد استوفينا الكلام المتعلق بهذا في شرحه فاطلبه هناك، ولولا كون الفن غريباً لأشبعنا الكلام فيه، وإن شئت أن يظهر لك حقيقة الحال في أمثال هذه القضية فاطلب من حواشينا المتعلقة بالحواشي الزاهدية على شرح المواقف، لكن اعلم ها هنا أن هذا غير واف فيما هو بصدده، فإن له أن يقول : لما كفى تصور العنوان للحكم باعتبار موارد تحققه فليكف في طلب موارد تحققه تصور العنوان، وإن شئت قل تصور المكلف العنوان وكلف بإيقاعه في ضمن موارد التحقق فالصواب في الجواب ما أشار إليه بقوله : على أنه فرق بين (تصوره أي المحال (إيقاعاً وبين تصوره مطلقاً فالأول محال لازم على تقدير التكليف به، لأنه طلب الإيقاع ولا بد من تصور المطلوب كما طلب بخلاف الثاني فإنه ليس مستحيلاً، وهو اللازم في القضية المنقوض بها، إذ لا بد للحكم من تصور العنوان لا تصور إيقاعه ( فتدبر) وأشار إلى دفع هذا النقض بزيادة قيد في الخارج الأشعرية (قالوا :أولاً : لو لم يصح التكليف بالمحال (لما وقع وقد وقع، لأن العاصي مأمور والفعل منه محال كيف لا وقد علم تعالى أنه لا يقع منه الفعل، فالفعل منه خلاف العلم وخلاف علمه تعالى ممتنع فالفعل منه ممتنع وكذلك من علم الله تعالى (بموته ومن نسخ عنه قبل تمكنه إذ المعلوم عدمه وخلاف المعلوم محال (والجواب أنه) لا يلزم منه الامتناع بالذات و لا يمتنع تصور الوقوع منه بل يفيد أن الواقع عدم الوقوع) ويجوز أن يكون الوقوع ممكناً غير واقع والعلم لا يحيل شيئاً ولا يعطي الإمكان (فإن العلم) بإمكان المعلوم أو امتناعه (تابع) للمعلوم وليس سبباً له فإنه إن كان ممكناً في ذاته تعلق العلم به ممكناً، وإن كان ممتنعاً تعلق به ممتنعاً كيف لا والإمكان لا يكون بالغير، لأن الكلام في الامتناع بالذات (وما قيل إنه يلزوم من جواز الفعل) مع تعلق العلم بالعدم جواز الجهل) فإن الجائز إذ قد أمكن وقوعه، فلو فرض وقوعه كان العلم مخالفاً له، وهو الجهل، فجواز الفعل باطل ولزم امتناعه فممنوع) لزومه (فإن العلم حاكِ عن الواقع المحقق) لا عن الواقع الفرضي وجواز الوجود إنما يوجب جواز الفرض دون الوقوع المحقق بل نقول : إمكان وقوع خلافه إنما يوجب إمكان تعلق العلم به من الأزل فلا إمكان للجهل (وأيضاً يستدعي) استدلال الأشعرية أن يكون كل تكليف تكليفاً بالمحال لوجوب تعلق العلم بأحد النقيضين من الفعل وعدمه وخلاف العلم محال فهو إما واجب إن تعلق العلم بالفعل أو ممتنع) إن تعلق بالعدم
And now we detail what was said: First: The conceptualization of the existence of the impossible is not necessary for command and obligation. I say in response: That denial is stubborn, for there is no meaning of command except seeking its occurrence, and seeking a thing is not except after conceptualizing it necessarily. And he said: Second: We accept that, but we say: Conceptualization in some aspect is sufficient for command and it is not impossible. I say in response: Knowing a thing by aspect is knowledge of the aspect truly and essentially, for there is no true knowledge except by the essence. So the sought thing is the aspect because the sought is what is requested, and the request only pertains to what is known, and it has been assumed that it is other than it; how could it not be other? The impossible is the possessor of the aspect, not the aspect. And he indicated the answer to these two objections in the proof by his saying: 'Command depends on conceptualization of its occurrence as commanded, otherwise that thing would not be commanded but something else, and this is necessary.' Then what he mentioned is insufficient, for we do not accept that knowing a thing by aspect is not knowledge of it at all. How so, while knowledge is that by which a thing is distinguished from others, and distinction is achieved? Yes, it is weak knowledge where the essence is not perceived and not fully distinguished in the mind, but requiring this level of perception for request is rejected. Yes, the proponents of the form say that knowledge by aspect is knowledge of it truly without its owner, because the essentially obtained is its form. But we, the people of truth, do not support them regarding the form; rather, knowledge in our view is another illuminating state. Even if we descend, we say according to the opinion of the proponents of the form: The possessor of the aspect in knowledge of a thing by aspect, although it is known accidentally, this accidental knowledge does not suffice for obligation? How so, when by this knowledge it has left being absolutely unknown, and is inherently attended to, and inherent attention is sufficient absolutely. So the correct answer is that conceptualization as commanded is necessary, i.e., actually, and this kind of conceptualization, whether by aspect or by essence, is not conceivable for the impossible, for it has no reality that can be described with occurrence, and aspects are hypothetical headings without anything headed. And he said: Third: We accept that, but we do not accept the impossibility of conceptualizing the impossible as occurring; rather, we say: The mind's conceptualization of the essence of the impossible described with existence in reality (whether it is described in reality and the knowledge is true or not) and false is not impossible; how so, since conceptualization of falsehoods is not impossible. I say in response: If he intends no impossibility of conceptualization while being heedless of it, then it does not harm, and we have no argument with heedlessness of impossibility. Rather, the point is that the impossible, insofar as its impossibility is known, cannot be conceptualized as existing as an occurrence in the external world, for that amounts to conceptualizing it as existent or non-existent. For the discussion is about the true command, which only exists with conceptualization of occurrence. And he indicated this rebuttal in the proof by the qualification of 'insofar as' in the impossible. And he said: Fourth: In the command to pray, the commander did not conceptualize it described with existence in reality, otherwise his knowledge would turn into ignorance since the prayer did not exist yet; exalted is Allah above that greatly. Thus, the command was valid without conceptualization of its occurrence as an occurrence in the external world, so a premise of your proof is refuted. I say in response: We do not accept that he did not conceptualize it as occurring; rather, he conceptualized it as it will occur, because its essence does not contradict its existence, so there is no impossibility in conceptualizing it thus. And if the objection is refined to the disobedient person, then this answer does not apply, for he did not conceptualize his prayer as it will occur because nothing will occur from him. Rather, the better answer is that he conceptualizes its essence and describes it with occurrence, then commands it, and this occurrence does not necessarily follow, for conceptual knowledge does not necessitate the occurrence of what is known. This is not conceivable for the impossible, for it has no essence that can be represented and described with occurrence, as it is not fit for such description. And he indicated this rebuttal in the argument by the meaning of his statement: 'Conceptualization of the occurrence of the impossible insofar as it is impossible is invalid,' i.e., conceptualization of the occurrence of the possible insofar as it is possible is valid. And he said: Fifth: Our statement that the conjunction of two opposites is impossible is an affirmative proposition that necessitates conceptualizing the impossible subject as positively affirmed, so the conceptualization of the impossible is possible, thus refuting his statement: 'Conceptualization of the occurrence of the impossible...' I say in response: The judgment there is about the nature by consideration of the instance, as we have verified in al-Sullam. The explanation is that the impossible cannot be conceptualized, so no judgment, positive or negative, is made about it. As for such propositions, the heading there is a possible universal, not impossible, so no judgment of impossibility is made about it. Rather, judgment about it is valid by consideration of its instances of realization, for non-existence is established for the heading, meaning that its instances of realization are non-existent. And we have already covered the discussion related to this in its commentary, so look for it there. Were it not that the art is strange, we would have elaborated. And if you wish to see the truth of the matter in such propositions, look at our glosses related to the Zāhidiyya glosses on Sharh al-Mawāqif. But know here that this is insufficient for what he is dealing with, for he can say: Since conceptualization of the heading suffices for judgment by consideration of its instances of realization, then let it suffice in seeking its instances of realization to conceptualize the heading. Or if you wish, say: The obligated person conceptualizes the heading and is obliged to bring it about within instances of realization. So the correct answer is what he indicated by his saying: 'There is a difference between conceptualizing it (i.e., the impossible) as an occurrence and conceptualizing it absolutely. The first is impossible necessarily under the assumption of obligation to it, because it is a request for occurrence and it is necessary to conceptualize the requested as requested, unlike the second, which is not impossible. That is what is necessary in the proposition used as objection, for judgment requires conceptualization of the heading, not conceptualization of its occurrence. So reflect.' And he indicated the rebuttal of this objection by adding the qualification 'in the external world.' The Ash'aris said: First: If commanding the impossible were invalid, it would not have occurred, but it has occurred, because the disobedient person is commanded and the act from him is impossible. How so, when Allah knows that the act will not occur from him? So the act from him contradicts knowledge, and contradiction of His knowledge is impossible, so the act from him is impossible. Similarly, regarding the one whom Allah knows will die and one whose command is abrogated before he is able, since what is known is non-existence, and contradicting the known is impossible. The answer is that this does not entail impossibility in itself, and it is not impossible to conceptualize its occurrence from him; rather, it means that what occurs is non-occurrence. It is possible that the occurrence is possible but does not occur, and knowledge does not make anything impossible nor give possibility. For knowledge of the possibility or impossibility of what is known is dependent on the known and is not a cause for it. For if it is possible in itself, knowledge attaches to it as possible; if impossible, it attaches to it as impossible. How so, while possibility is not due to another? Because the discussion is about impossibility in itself. And what is said that from the permissibility of the act with knowledge attaching to non-existence, the permissibility of ignorance follows? For what is permissible, since its occurrence is possible, if its occurrence were assumed, knowledge would contradict it, which is ignorance. So the permissibility of the act is false and its impossibility follows. This is rejected: this consequence does not follow, for knowledge describes the actual reality, not the hypothetical reality. The possibility of existence only necessitates the possibility of assumption, not actual occurrence. Rather, we say: The possibility of the occurrence of its opposite only necessitates the possibility of knowledge attaching to it from eternity, so there is no possibility of ignorance. Also, the Ash'ari argument would require that every obligation be an obligation of the impossible, due to the necessity of knowledge attaching to one of the two opposites of the act or its non-existence, and contradicting knowledge is impossible. So it is either obligatory if knowledge attaches to the act, or impossible if knowledge attaches to non-existence.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a detailed presentation of five objections to the Hanafi ruling that commanding the impossible (taklif al-muhal) is invalid, along with their rebuttals. The debate is between the Hanafi/Maturidi position and the Ash'ari position. The first objection claims that conceptualization of the impossible is not necessary for command; the response asserts that command necessarily requires conceptualization of the requested action. The second objection argues that conceptualization in some aspect suffices; the response differentiates between knowing a thing by its aspect (which is not true knowledge of the thing itself) and knowing its essence, concluding that the impossible has no essence to conceptualize. The third objection claims that conceptualizing the impossible as occurring is possible; the response clarifies that while one can think of the impossible abstractly, one cannot conceptualize it as actually occurring in the external world. The fourth objection uses the example of commanding prayer before it exists to show that command does not require conceptualization of actual occurrence; the response explains that the commander conceptualizes the essence and its possibility, not its actual occurrence, and that this is different for the impossible. The fifth objection claims that propositions like 'the conjunction of opposites is impossible' involve conceptualizing the impossible; the response explains that such judgments are about a possible universal heading, not the impossible itself. Finally, the Ash'ari argument from divine foreknowledge is refuted by stating that knowledge is dependent on the known, not causal, and that possibility is intrinsic, not determined by knowledge. The correct Hanafi view is that the impossible cannot be commanded because it cannot be conceptualized as occurring, and the distinction between conceptualizing the impossible absolutely versus as an occurrence is key.

Key Terms
المحال (al-muhal): That which is inherently impossible in itself, such as the conjunction of opposites, due to its essence, not external factors.
التكليف (al-taklif): Legal obligation to perform an action, which requires the action to be conceptually and actually possible for the obligated person.
التصور (al-tasawwur): Conceptualization or mental representation of a thing as it is intended to be realized.
الإيقاع (al-iqa'): Actual occurrence or external realization of an act.
الوجه (al-wajh): Aspect or attribute; knowledge by aspect is indirect knowledge of the thing through one of its attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, why is commanding the impossible invalid?
A) Because it would contradict Allah's foreknowledge.
B) Because the act cannot be conceptualized as occurring in the external world.
C) Because it is a waste of effort.
D) Because it would require infinite time.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that commanding the impossible is invalid because a valid command requires the conceptualization of the commanded act as occurring, which is impossible for inherently impossible acts.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Refuting the Ash'ari Argument from Divine Knowledge for Taklif al-Muhal
Refutation of the first Ash'ari argument for taklif al-muhal from divine foreknowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ash'ari argument for taklif al-muhal from divine knowledge and evaluate the Hanafi response based on the principle that knowledge is subordinate to the known.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge describes reality; it does not cause impossibility. Acts remain possible even if not actualized.

This passage presents the first Ash'ari argument for the validity of obligating the impossible (taklif al-muhal) based on God's foreknowledge, and the Hanafi refutation. It clarifies a fundamental usul point: knowledge is subordinate to the known, not its cause. Understanding this distinction is critical for correct usul reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari argument
Trace how the Ash'aris claim that God's knowledge of non-occurrence makes the act impossible. The disobedient person is commanded, but God knows the act will not occur, so the act contradicts knowledge and is impossible.
The argument uses the impossibility of contradiction to establish essential impossibility.
2
Understand the Hanafi counter
The Hanafi response states that knowledge is subordinate (tabi') to the known. It does not cause possibility or impossibility; it merely reflects the actual state of the known object. An act can be possible in itself (mumkin bi al-dhat) even if it never occurs.
The core principle: al-'ilm tabi' li al-ma'lum.
3
Analyze the rebuttal regarding hypothetical ignorance
The Ash'ari may retort: if the act is possible, then its occurrence would contradict God's knowledge, making God ignorant. The Hanafi response: knowledge reports about actual reality, not hypothetical scenarios. The possibility of occurrence only allows supposition, not actuality that would contradict knowledge.
Knowledge is about the actual (al-waqi' al-muhaqqaq), not the hypothetical.
4
See the reduction to absurdity
The Hanafi critic shows that the Ash'ari argument implies that every obligation is an obligation of the impossible, because for any command, God knows either that it will be performed or not, and the opposite of His knowledge is impossible. This is self-defeating.
This reductio ad absurdum exposes the unsoundness of the Ash'ari premise.
5
Connect to al-Ash'ari's own view on power and kasb
The passage explains that al-Ash'ari held that power is concomitant with the act and that servants' actions are created by God. This forces him into accepting taklif al-muhal, because at the time of obligation the act is not within the servant's power. The Hanafi alternative: power is required at the time of action, not obligation, and obligation concerns acquisition (kasb), not creation (ijad).
The Hanafi position avoids taklif al-muhal by properly locating the role of human power.

وكذلك من علم الله تعالى (بموته ومن نسخ عنه قبل تمكنه إذ المعلوم عدمه وخلاف المعلوم محال (والجواب أنه) لا يلزم منه الامتناع بالذات و لا يمتنع تصور الوقوع منه بل يفيد أن الواقع عدم الوقوع) ويجوز أن يكون العلم بالفعل أو ممتنع) إن تعلق بالعدم ولا شيء منهما بمقدور فاستحالا من المكلف، ولزم كون كل تكليف تكليفاً بالمحال واعلم أن الأشعري ذهب إلى أن القدرة مع الفعل وأن أفعال العباد مخلوقة الله تعالى، فألزموا عليه تكليف المحال أما من الأول فلأنه لما لم تكن القدرة حال التكليف الذي هو قبل الفعل صار الفعل غير مقدور ومستحيلاً بالنسبة إلى المكلف، وأما من الثاني فلأن أفعال العباد لما كانت مخلوقة الله تعالى لم تكن مقدورة للعبد فاستحالت منه (بل) الأشعرية (التزموا) التكليف بالمحال (والحق أنه ليس بلازم) والالتزام من غير لزوم ( أما ) عدم اللزوم (من الأول فلأن القدرة إنما تجب في زمان الإيقاع) أي إيقاع الفعل (حتى يتحقق الامتثال لا زمان التكليف فلم يكن التكليف بما هو غير مقدور حال الإيقاع ( وأما ) عدم اللزوم (من) الثاني فلأن التكليف عنده أي الأشعري (لا يتعلق إلا بالكسب كما هو عندنا أيضاً، وهو فعل مقدور للعبد (لا) بالإيجاد) الذي هو غير مقدور له ( وفيه كلام) عظيم (في) علم (الكلام ) يطول الكلام بذكره، لكن ينبغي أن ينبه بأن الأشعري لا مخلص له عن القول بالتكليف بغير المقدور فإن الكسب عنده أيضاً من الله تعالى، وللعبد قدرة متوهمة فقط لا دخل لها في شيء من الأفعال فتأمل وأنصف
And they said first: If the obligation of the impossible were not valid, it would not have occurred, yet it has occurred, because the disobedient one is commanded, and his action is impossible. How could it not be, when God Almighty knows that the action will not occur from him? So the action from him is contrary to knowledge, and contrary to His knowledge is impossible, so the action from him is impossible. Similarly, someone of whom God knows that he will die or that the obligation is abrogated before he is able—since what is known is non-existence, and contrary to what is known is impossible. (And the answer is that) it does not necessitate impossibility in essence, nor does it prevent conceptualizing the occurrence from him; rather it indicates that what actually happens is non-occurrence. And it is possible that the occurrence is possible but not actual, and knowledge does not make anything impossible nor grant possibility. (For knowledge) of the possibility or impossibility of the known (is subordinate) to the known, not a cause of it. For if it is possible in itself, knowledge attaches to it as possible; if it is impossible, knowledge attaches to it as impossible. How could it not be, since possibility is not from another? Because the discussion is about essential impossibility. (And what is said that the permissibility of the act) with knowledge attached to non-existence necessitates the permissibility of ignorance—since the permissible, if its occurrence were possible, then if it were supposed to occur, knowledge would contradict it, which is ignorance. So the permissibility of the act is false, and its impossibility follows. But this necessity is denied. (For knowledge reports about actual reality,) not about hypothetical reality, and the possibility of existence only necessitates the possibility of supposition, not actual occurrence. Rather we say: the possibility of its contrary only necessitates the possibility of knowledge attaching to it from eternity, so there is no possibility of ignorance. (And also the reasoning of the Ash'aris) implies that every obligation is an obligation of the impossible, because knowledge necessarily attaches to one of the two contraries (act or omission), and what contradicts knowledge is impossible. So it is either obligatory if knowledge attaches to the act, or impossible if it attaches to non-existence. Neither of them is within the power of the obligated person, so both are impossible from him, and it follows that every obligation is an obligation of the impossible. Know that al-Ash'ari held that power is concomitant with the act and that the actions of servants are created by God Almighty. Thus they were compelled to [accept] the obligation of the impossible. In the first way: because when power is not present at the time of obligation—which is before the act—the act becomes beyond power and impossible relative to the obligated person. In the second way: because since the actions of servants are created by God Almighty, they are not within the power of the servant, so they are impossible from him. (Rather) the Ash'aris (accepted) the obligation of the impossible. (The truth is that it is not necessary,) and acceptance without necessity. (As for) the lack of necessity (from the first) it is because power is required only at the time of actualization—i.e., enacting the act—so that compliance can be realized, not at the time of obligation. So the obligation was not of something that is beyond power at the time of actualization. (As for) the lack of necessity (from the second) it is because obligation according to him—i.e., al-Ash'ari—(is only connected to acquisition (kasb), as it is with us as well, and it is an act within the servant's power, (not) to creation (ijad) which is not within his power. (There is great discussion) in the science of (kalam) that would take long to mention, but it should be noted that al-Ash'ari has no escape from saying that obligation is of what is not within power, because acquisition according to him is also from God, and the servant has only an imagined power that has no involvement in any actions. So ponder and be fair.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first Ash'ari argument for the validity of obligating the impossible (taklif al-muhal). The argument claims that since God knows a sinner will not obey, the act is impossible because it contradicts knowledge. The Hanafi response is that divine knowledge does not cause impossibility; it is subordinate to the actual state of affairs. If an act is possible in itself, knowledge of its non-occurrence does not make it impossible; it simply indicates that non-occurrence will happen. The response also addresses a rebuttal involving hypothetical ignorance and shows that the Ash'ari position leads to every obligation being an obligation of the impossible, which is absurd. The passage then explains that al-Ash'ari's own views on power and creation lead to this problem, but the correct Hanafi view is that power is required at the time of action, not obligation, and that obligation concerns acquisition (kasb), not creation.

Key Terms
تكليف المحال (taklif al-muhal): The obligation of the impossible; the idea that God can command what is inherently impossible for the servant.
القدرة (al-qudra): Power or ability; the capacity to perform an act.
الكسب (al-kasb): Acquisition; the servant's role in actions, as distinct from creation (ijad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why does God's knowledge that a sinner will not obey not make the act of obedience impossible for him?
A) Because God's knowledge is not infallible.
B) Because knowledge is subordinate to the known and does not cause impossibility.
C) Because the sinner always has free will regardless of knowledge.
D) Because the obligation was already abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is descriptive, not causative; an act remains possible in itself even if God knows it will not occur.

Refuting the Ash'ari Argument on Taklif al-Muhal: The Case of Abu Jahl
Response to the second Ash'ari argument about Abu Jahl's impossible obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Ash'ari argument of impossible obligation and reconstruct the Hanafi response using the distinction between summary and detailed belief, and the principle that knowledge does not negate possibility.

Core Idea
God's command to believe does not require affirming impossible contradictions; summary belief in the message avoids the paradox.

This passage tackles a sophisticated theological objection: that God's command to Abu Jahl to believe included believing a report about his own disbelief, creating an impossible paradox. The Hanafi response masterfully distinguishes the scope of obligatory belief and introduces the concept of summary (ijmali) versus detailed (tafsili) belief, preserving the rationality of divine command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari paradox
Locate the argument that Abu Jahl was commanded to believe everything the Prophet brought, which includes the report that he will not believe. This would require him to affirm his own disbelief, a logical contradiction.
This is the core of the taklif al-muhal (impossible obligation) objection.
2
State the initial Hanafi response
Note the response: Abu Jahl is only obligated to believe in the essential rulings of sharia (God, resurrection, etc.), not in reports about his own state. Therefore, no paradox arises.
3
Analyze the counter-objection and reply
The Ash'ari might retort that believing sharia reports is also impossible if God reports Abu Jahl won't believe. The Hanafi reply: knowledge or news does not turn a possible act into an impossible one; it only indicates what will occur.
This refutes the idea that divine foreknowledge creates impossibility.
4
Examine the refinement using summary belief
The passage introduces a more precise answer: the obligation is to believe in summary (ijmali), not detail (tafsili). Summary belief does not entail affirming specific details, so believing 'everything the Prophet said' in summary does not require believing the paradox in detail.
This is a key Hanafi contribution to resolving such theological puzzles.
5
Recognize the rejection of knowledge abrogating obligation
The author rejects the view that if Abu Jahl knew he would not believe, the obligation would fall. The obligation remains because man is not left without purpose; divine knowledge does not remove capability.
This upholds the perpetual nature of taklif.

And they said, secondly: God obligated Abu Jahl to believe, which is affirming the truth of what the Prophet brought in its entirety. And from it—i.e., part of what he brought—is that he (Abu Jahl) will not affirm him. So He obligated him to affirm him in that he will not affirm him, which is impossible. How could it not be? And it—i.e., affirming non-affirmation—only occurs by the negation of affirmation, for if affirmation existed, he would know affirmation and affirm it, so how can he affirm its non-existence? Therefore, affirmation is necessary for the non-existence of affirmation, and what is necessary for a contradiction is impossible in essence. So Abu Jahl was obligated with the essentially impossible. The answer: There is no obligation on Abu Jahl except to affirm the rulings of the sharia—that God, the reports of resurrection, judgment, Paradise, Hell, etc. And his non-affirmation is a report from God to him (the Prophet). Abu Jahl is not obligated to affirm this report, so he was not obligated to affirm non-affirmation, so there is no impossibility. Thus they said. If you say: affirming the sharia reports is also impossible from him because it contradicts His report, and contradiction is impossible. He said: (Knowledge or news does not remove the possible from possibility.) For they only necessitate that their object be actual, not that it be necessary. With this much the answer is complete. And some added: if Abu Jahl knew that he will not believe, the obligation would fall, because there would be no benefit then. The author did not approve this and said: (What is said: if he knew that he will not affirm him, the obligation would fall from him is denied—i.e., false—for man is not left in vain at all, so obligation never falls from him. It is said in the gloss: And how would it fall when God's knowledge—if it does not prevent capability—so His news of it and the obligated person's knowledge of it should be more likely not to prevent. Contemplate. And in it, the one who said obligation falls was not saying that power is negated, but rather that the benefit is trial or compliance, and it does not remain after the obligated person knows that it will not occur. The author will refer to this in the discussions of the fourth chapter. But the truth is what he mentioned here. It was said in the answer: He is obligated to affirm everything in summary, so he is also obligated to affirm non-affirmation in summary. Affirmative non-affirmation only necessitates non-affirmation if it is detailed, not if it is summary. So summary affirmation is not necessary for non-affirmation, so there is no impossibility. (I say: affirmation of everything from...)
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage addresses the second Ash'ari argument for taklif al-muhal: that God obligated Abu Jahl to believe, which includes believing that the Prophet said Abu Jahl would not believe. This creates a paradox—Abu Jahl would have to believe his own disbelief. The Hanafi response is that Abu Jahl was only obligated to believe in the essential teachings of Islam, not in reports about his own state. Furthermore, even if he were obligated to believe the report, knowledge or news does not turn a possible act into an impossible one; it only indicates what will occur. The passage also discusses and rejects the idea that knowledge of failure abrogates obligation, and it mentions a more refined answer: obligation is in summary form, not detailed, so the paradox is avoided. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the discussion of summary belief.

Key Terms
التصديق (al-tasdiq): Affirmation or belief in the truth of something; here, belief in the Prophet's message.
الإجمالي (al-ijmali): Summary or general belief, as opposed to detailed (tafsili) belief.
التفصيلي (al-tafsili): Detailed belief, specifying each item of faith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi response resolve the apparent paradox of Abu Jahl being commanded to believe that he will not believe?
A) The command was abrogated when Abu Jahl refused.
B) Abu Jahl was only commanded to believe in the general truths of Islam, not in specific reports about himself.
C) The obligation was only for a moment and then lifted.
D) Abu Jahl's disbelief was not truly known by God.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves the taklif al-muhal objection by limiting obligatory belief to general sharia truths and employing the distinction between summary and detailed belief, ensuring that divine commands remain possible.

15 min
Are Disbelievers Obligated by Subsidiary Rulings?
Intra-madhhab usul dispute with proofs and counter-proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi usul methods to evaluate the dispute over taklif of kuffar bi al-furu', contrasting the Bukhari and Iraqi positions with their proofs.

Core Idea
Disbelievers are obligated only by i'tiqad, not furu', because taklif bi al-furu' is purposeless without faith.

This passage presents a crucial intra-madhhab dispute in Hanafi usul: are disbelievers addressed by the subsidiary branches of Sharia? While all agree they must believe, later Hanafi masters divided on whether they also bear responsibility for acts like prayer and fasting. The debate rests on the purpose of taklif, logical possibilities of compliance, and interpretation of Quranic verses. This lesson guides you through the arguments and counterarguments, sharpening your ability to critically examine legal derivations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
Locate the contrast between Bukhariyun (scholars from Bukhara) and Iraqiyun. Note that both agree on obligation of belief (i'tiqad) but differ regarding furu'.
The Bukhariyun hold that kuffar are only obligated by belief.
2
Analyze the Purposive Argument and Analogy
Understand why the Bukhariyun argue that taklif bi al-furu' is futile for a disbeliever, using the analogy of a patient beyond cure.
The purpose of furu' is ethical refinement and drawing near to Allah, impossible for a kafir.
3
Trace the Inductive Derivation from Furu' Fiqhiyyah
Examine examples like the vow of fasting case from Imam Muhammad and four cases from Shaykh Siraj al-Din that support the Bukhari position.
These cases show that apostasy nullifies obligations, implying kuffar were not bound by them.
4
Examine the Logical Objections and Responses
Work through the three objections raised against the negator's position: validity of acts, possibility of compliance, and necessity of qada'. Study the Hanafi rebuttals.
Key rebuttal: Islam erases what came before, so no qada' is required.
5
Critique the Quranic Evidence
Evaluate the three verses cited by the affirmers (Al-Muddaththir 38-43, Al-Baqarah 21) and the Hanafi counter-interpretations.
The verses are dismissed as Makki, not legislative, or as alluding to disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
