Mistake in Talaq: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling that talaq uttered by mistake takes effect, and the role of zann in legal judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi use of mazinnah in the talaq dispute and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A mistaken adult's talaq takes effect because their discernment is a strong presumption (mazinnah) of intent, unlike a sleeper.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute: does a mistaken person's talaq take effect? The Hanafi school says yes, relying on the legal presumption of mazinnah. The Shafi'i school says no, emphasizing intent. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi usul balances hidden internal states with observable presumptions for judicial rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
First, state that the Hanafi school rules that a mistaken person's talaq takes effect. Note that this applies to all irrevocable constitutive acts.
The passage says 'and every constitutive act that does not admit of revocation'.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i school argues that talaq requires intention, and since the mistaken person lacks it, the utterance should be void, analogous to a sleeping person.
The objector is Imam al-Shafi'i (رحمه الله).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response acknowledges the role of intent but argues that whether one is heedless of the meaning is hidden. So the law uses the apparent sign (mazinnah): the person's status as a mature, sane adult who is awake, which strongly indicates intent.
The legal presumption is called mazinnah, specifically tamyīz al-bulūgh (discernment of maturity).
4
Distinguish judicial vs. internal realms
Note that the Hanafi ruling applies only in external judicial matters (qadā'). In the sight of Allah, the person's actual intent matters. This is a key limitation.
The text says: 'then it should be understood that this only applies if the claimant claims its occurrence in a judicial setting'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقع طلاقه عندنا وكل إنشاء لا يحتمل الفسخ (خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله (لأن اعتبار الكلام) إنما يكون بالقصد ولم يوجد في الخاطىء فلا اعتبار لكلامه (كما في النائم فلا يقع نعم اعتبار الكلام بالقصد، لكن الغفلة عن معنى اللفظ أمر خفي فلا تناط الأحكام على قصد المعنى (فأقيم تمييزاً لبلوغ مقامه أي القصد لأنه مظنة القصد، وإذا كانت المظنة موجودة لا ينتفي الحكم، وإن كانت الحكمة منتفية (بخلاف النوم فإن تمييز البلوغ منتف فيه، ثم يخفى أن هذا إنما يتم لو كان المدعي وقوعه قضاء، لأن القصد أمر مخفي فلا بد من اعتبار المظنة، وأما الخبير العليم فيعلم القصد، وليس هنا دليل على اعتبار المظنة وإلغاء الحكمة، ما يشيد أركان هذا
And his divorce falls (takes effect) according to us, and every constitutive act that does not admit of revocation, contrary to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him). Because the consideration of speech is only based on intent, and since intent is not present in the mistaken person, his speech should not be considered, just as in sleep it does not take effect. Yes, speech is considered based on intent, but heedlessness of the meaning of the utterance is a subtle matter, so rulings are not dependent on the intention of the meaning. Therefore, discernment (tamyīz) of reaching its status (i.e., the status of intent) was established because it is a probable indicator (mazinnah) of intent. And when the mazinnah is present, the ruling is not negated even if the wisdom (hikmah) is absent, unlike sleep where the discernment of maturity is absent. Then it should be understood that this only applies if the claimant claims its occurrence in a judicial setting (qadā'), because intent is hidden, so consideration of the mazinnah is necessary. As for the All-Knowing Expert, He knows intent. And there is no evidence here for considering the mazinnah and discarding the wisdom? This strengthens the foundation of this.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a exposition of the Hanafi position on the effect of mistake in talaq and similar irrevocable unilateral acts, contrasted with the Shafi'i view. The Shafi'i opinion holds that since talaq requires intention, and a mistaken person lacks it, the utterance is void, analogous to a sleeping person. The Hanafi response acknowledges that speech is generally considered by intent, but notes that whether a person is heedless of the meaning is difficult to ascertain externally. So the law, for practical judicial purposes, considers the manifest fact that the person is a mature, sane adult, which is a strong presumption (mazinnah) of intent. This presumption suffices to give legal effect unless the ignorance of meaning is proven. Unlike sleep, where the capacity for discernment (tamyīz) does not exist at all, a mistaken person is otherwise capable. This rule, however, applies only in the external court (qadā'); internally, the person's actual intent matters to Allah. Thus, the Hanafis do not simply disregard intention but rely on a legal presumption based on appearance.

Key Terms
المظنة (al-mazinnah): A probable indicator used as a basis for legal rulings when the actual cause is hidden.
تمييز البلوغ (tamyīz al-bulūgh): The capacity for discernment that comes with maturity, distinguishing an adult from a child or sleeper.
القصد (al-qasd): Intent or purpose in performing an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that a mistaken person's talaq takes effect, while that of a sleeping person does not?
A) Because a sleeping person cannot speak.
B) Because the mistaken person has discernment (tamyīz) which is a strong presumption of intent, while a sleeper lacks any discernment.
C) Because the mistaken person is still conscious.
D) Because the hadith says so.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when internal intent is hidden, the law relies on observable presumptions (mazinnah) such as the capacity for discernment, but this is limited to external judicial rulings; true accountability rests with Allah.

Coercion and Legal Obligation: The Hanafi Position
Hanafi analysis of coercion and taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on coercion and taklif, and apply the distinction between mulji' and ghayr mulji' coercion to determine the ruling on coerced acts.

Core Idea
Coercion does not negate legal obligation; the coerced still chooses between harms.

This passage tackles a fundamental usul dispute: does coercion remove legal responsibility? The Hanafis argue it does not, because even under duress, the individual chooses the lesser of two evils. This has concrete implications for acts like drinking wine under threat of death or killing under coercion. Understanding this debate clarifies how Hanafi jurists view human agency under compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of coercion
The text distinguishes mulji' (overwhelming, threatening life/limb) and ghayr mulji' (lesser harms like beating or imprisonment). Note how each type is defined.
Mulji' coercion is the key category for the dispute.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafis hold that coercion does not negate taklif at all—neither for the coerced act nor its opposite. The coerced person remains under obligation.
This is the absolute Hanafi view.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof
The proof is that the coerced person can still choose between the threatened harm and the coerced act; he selects the lesser of two evils. Examples: wine-drinking under threat of death becomes obligatory; killing an innocent Muslim remains haram and the person is rewarded for refusing.
The key is that choice (ikhtiyar) remains, even if constrained.
4
Contrast with opposing views
Some scholars say only mulji' coercion negates taklif. The Mu'tazila say mulji' negates for both act and opposite, and ghayr mulji' negates only for the coerced act itself.
Understand each position's logic.
5
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis rebut the claim of inevitability by distinguishing between compulsion (ījāb) and mere weighting (tarjīḥ). Obligation or impossibility by law or reason does not remove choice; it tips the scale. The ability to do the opposite implies ability to do the act.
This is the subtle point the text asks you to reflect upon.

مسألة الإكراه ملج وهو بما يفوّت النفس أو العضو) إن لم يفعل الفعل المكره عليه (وغيره) وهو الإكراه بغير ما يفوّت النفس والعضو (غيره) أي غير الملجيء (كالحبس والضرب وهو) الا يمنع التكليف بالفعل المكره Issue: Coercion is of two types: mulji' (overwhelming) which involves taking away life or limb if the coerced act is not done, and other (ghayr mulji') which involves what does not take away life or limb, such as imprisonment and beating. And it (coercion) does not negate legal obligation (taklīf) with respect to the coerced act or its opposite, absolutely. Some scholars said: Coercion negates taklīf in the mulji' type but not in other types. The Mu'tazila said: Coercion negates taklīf in the mulji' type with respect to both the coerced act and its opposite, and in other types it negates taklīf with respect to the coerced act but not its opposite. Our proof: The coerced act and its opposite are both possible in themselves, as they were before, and the doer has power over them. How could he not? Indeed, he chooses the lesser of two evils: if he sees the act as lighter than the threat, he chooses it; if he sees the threat as lighter, he chooses that. Thus, the doer is capable, so taklīf is valid. Hence, sometimes the coerced act becomes obligatory: for example, coercion by threat of death to drink wine – at that moment, drinking becomes obligatory, and he sins if he leaves it. And sometimes the coerced act is prohibited: for example, coercion to kill a Muslim unjustly – it is never permissible. In that case, he is rewarded for refraining, because the temptation to haram was present and he restrained himself. Similarly, coercion to utter words of disbelief: he is rewarded if he refrains, but if he does it, he does not sin (even though it is haram) because it is treated as permissible as previously mentioned. However, he does sin if he commits the prohibited act in the case of coercion to kill. And those who differentiate between mulji' and ghayr mulji' said: The coerced act in mulji' is inevitable (wājib al-wuqū') because the coercer forces the doer, and its opposite is impossible, so taklīf regarding both is impossible. We say: We do not accept that the coerced act is inherently inevitable and its opposite inherently impossible. Rather, the obligation and impossibility may be by divine law, as in killing and drinking wine, or by reason. Indeed, a rational person typically chooses what he sees as lighter, and obligation or prohibition by law or reason does not negate choice for the doer; rather, it tips the scale (tarjīḥ) towards action or inaction, not compulsion (ījāb). So reflect, for it is subtle. And the Mu'tazila said: Compliance is not possible in the taklīf concerning the very coerced act if he is coerced to do the commanded act; because performing it is due to the motive of coercion, not the motive of the divine command, so there is no sincerity and therefore no reward and no compliance, so taklīf is invalid due to lack of benefit. This is unlike when he performs the opposite of the coerced act and is commanded to do that, because that is stronger in responding to the divine motive, as he endures punishment for the sake of Allah. We say: You have admitted the validity of taklīf concerning the opposite of the coerced act. And the validity of taklīf concerning the opposite implies that it is within one's power (maqdūr). And the ability to do something is ability to do its opposite. Thus, the ability to (finish incomplete).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of the usul issue of coercion and its effect on taklīf, a major point of contention between Hanafis, Mu'tazila, and others. The Hanafi position holds that coercion does not negate legal obligation because the coerced individual still has the ability to choose between the threatened harm and the act; he will choose the lesser of two evils, so he remains a capable agent. This is proven by examples: under threat of death, drinking wine becomes obligatory (one sins by leaving it), while killing an innocent Muslim remains strictly prohibited (the person is rewarded for refusing and sin if he does). Uttering kufr under coercion is treated as permissible, but without reward. The passage also refutes the view that mulji' coercion makes the act inevitable (wājib al-wuqū') by emphasizing that obligation or impossibility can be by choice or by law, not inherent. The Mu'tazila objection about lack of ikhlas is countered by noting that the ability to do the opposite implies ability to do the act itself. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the principle is clear: the coerced person is mukallaf because he retains choice, however constrained.

Key Terms
الإكراه الملجئ (al-ikrāh al-mulji'): Coercion that threatens life or limb, leaving no practical alternative but to comply.
الإكراه غير الملجئ (al-ikrāh ghayr al-mulji'): Coercion through lesser harms like imprisonment or beating, still leaving some choice.
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): Tipping the scales in favor of one option, not compulsion.
التكليف (al-taklīf): Legal obligation addressed to a capable person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented, what is the ruling on killing an innocent Muslim under threat of death (mulji' coercion)?
A) It becomes obligatory to preserve one's own life.
B) It remains strictly prohibited, and the person is rewarded for refusing.
C) It becomes permissible, and the person does not sin if he does it.
D) It becomes recommended because of duress.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle: coercion does not negate legal obligation; the coerced person retains choice and capacity, and the ruling on the act depends on its nature and the degree of threat.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
15 min
The Effect of Ikrah on Taklif: Hanafi vs Mu'tazila
Defines ikrah and argues coercion does not bar obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that coercion does not invalidate legal obligation and evaluate the Mu'tazili counter-argument.

Core Idea
Coercion does not negate capacity – the coerced agent remains a valid subject of legal obligation.

This passage tackles a fundamental usul question: can a person be legally obligated to do an act they are forced to do? The Hanafi school argues yes, because the coerced person still has choice and capacity. The Mu'tazila disagrees, focusing on the absence of sincere intention. We'll explore the definitions, proofs, and practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Classify Ikrah Types
Identify whether the coercion is mulji' (threat to life/limb) or ghair mulji' (lesser harm). This determines the scope of the dispute.
The passage defines mulji' as 'ما يفوت النفس أو العضو' and ghair mulji' as 'كالحبس والضرب'.
2
State the Hanafi Core Claim
Understand that the Hanafi position is that ikrah does not prevent taklif for either the coerced act or its opposite, in both types. This is the starting point of the argument.
The text says: 'لا يمنع التكليف بالفعل المكره عليه وبنقيضه مطلقا'.
3
Map the Opposing Views
Note that some scholars and the Mu'tazila differentiate: for them, in mulji' obligation is prevented for both; in ghair mulji', only for the specific coerced act.
The passage mentions 'وقالت المعتزلة: يمنع الإكراه التكليف في الملجيء بعين المكره عليه وبنقيضه، ويمنع في غيره في عين المكره عليه دون نقيضه'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Proof
The act remains possible, the agent is capable and chooses the lesser evil. This rational proof (dalil aqli) establishes that obligation is not impossible.
Key phrase: 'لنا أن الفعل المكره عليه وكذا ضده ممكن... الفاعل قادر فيصح التكليف'.
5
Examine the Mu'tazili Counter-Proof
They argue that coercion eliminates sincere intention (ikhlas), making compliance (imtithal) impossible, thus obligation is futile.
The passage says: 'لا يمكن الامتثال في التكليف بعين المكره عليه... فلا إخلاص فلا يثاب عليه ولا امتثال فلا يصح التكليف به'.
6
Evaluate the Hanafi Response
The response uses two main lines: (a) some coerced acts are done from divine motives, and (b) power over the opposite implies power over the act itself.
See the final part: 'لا نسلم الإتيان بعين المكره عليه لداعي الإكراه لزوما... والقدرة على الشيء قدرة على ضده'.
7
Identify the Practical Thamarah
The difference manifests in rulings: for Hanafis, a coerced person may be obligated to commit a lesser evil (e.g., drink wine) and is rewarded for avoiding a greater evil (e.g., refusing to kill). The Mu'tazili view would exempt the coerced from obligation for the specific act.
Examples in the text: wine becomes obligatory, killing remains prohibited and agent is rewarded for abstaining, uttering kufr is excused but still haram.

(به) لانتفاء الفائدة (بخلاف ما إذا أتى بنقيض المكره (عليه وكلف به فإنه أبلغ في إجابة داعي الشرع) حيث صبر على التعذيب في سبيل الله (قلنا) قد اعترفتم بصحة التكليف بضد المكره عليه و(صحة التكليف بالضد تقتضي المقدورية أي كونه مقدوراً والقدرة على الشيء قدرة على ضده) فالقدرة على الضد قدرة على ضد الضد الذي هو عين المكره عليه فصار المكره عليه مقدوراً، وكل مقدور التكليف به هذا ولعله غير واف فإن الخصم لم يجعل المانع من صحة التكليف انتفاء يصح القدرة حتى يرد ما أورد بل أحدث مانعاً آخر وهو انتفاء فائدة التكليف، وهو الامتثال مع الإخلاص في النية، وهذا غير دافع له بل الصواب في الجواب أنا لا نسلم الإتيان بعين المكره عليه لداعي الإكراه لزوماً، فإن الذين بذلوا أنفسهم في سبيل الله لا يقدمون على الفعل إلا لداعي الشرع والعمل بالنية والعالم هو الله تعالى، فتأمل فهو الأحق بالقبول
Issue: Coercion is overwhelming (mulji') which is by what destroys life or limb) if he does not do the coerced act (and other) which is coercion by other than what destroys life and limb (other) i.e. non-overwhelming (like imprisonment and beating and it) does not prevent obligation of the coerced act and its opposite absolutely. And a group said: coercion prevents obligation (in the overwhelming) from it but not other. And the Mu'tazila said: coercion prevents obligation in the overwhelming in the specific coerced act and its opposite, and prevents in other in the specific coerced act but not its opposite) i.e. it does not prevent in the opposite of the coerced act. For us: the coerced act and likewise its opposite are possible in themselves as before, and the agent is capable of it—how is he not capable? And he chooses the lesser of two evils between the act and what he is threatened with. If he sees the act as lighter than what he is threatened with, he chooses it, and if he sees what he is threatened with as lighter, he chooses it. So the agent is able, thus obligation is valid. Hence, sometimes what he is coerced upon becomes obligatory (and obligation is a type of taklif) like coercion by killing to drink wine, for then it becomes obligatory upon him to drink (so he sins by leaving it) and sometimes what he is coerced upon is prohibited (like coercion to kill a Muslim unjustly i.e. like coercion to kill a Muslim unjustly, for it is not permissible at all (so he is rewarded for abstaining) because he found the motive for the forbidden and restrained himself. (Like for uttering the word of disbelief) i.e. as he is rewarded in coercion to utter the word of disbelief on the tongue if he refrains, but here he does not sin if he does it, even though it is forbidden, because it is treated as permissible as previously mentioned. And he sins in the case of coercion to kill by doing the forbidden. And those who differentiate between overwhelming and other said: the coerced act is necessarily occurring because the coercer compelled the agent to the act and its opposite is impossible (and obligation of both is impossible. We said: we do not concede that the coerced act is obligatory in essence and its opposite is impossible in essence; rather, obligation and impossibility may be by law as in killing and drinking wine, or by reason. For a rational person tends to choose what he sees as lighter, and obligation and impossibility by law or reason do not negate choice for the agent; rather, it is a preponderator for the side of action or inaction, not a necessitator. So reflect, for it is subtle. (And the Mu'tazila said:) It is impossible to comply with the obligation of the specific coerced act when coerced to that specific commanded act, because doing it is due to the motive of coercion, not due to the motive of law, so there is no sincerity, so he is not rewarded, and there is no compliance, so the obligation is invalid due to lack of benefit. (Unlike if he does the opposite of the coerced act and is obligated with it, for it is more effective in responding to the motive of law) where he endures torture in the path of Allah. (We said:) You have conceded the validity of obligation with the opposite of the coerced act, and the validity of obligation with the opposite implies that it is within power, and power over something is power over its opposite. So power over the opposite is power over the opposite of the opposite which is the specific coerced act, so the coerced act becomes within power, and anything within power can be obligated. However, this may not be sufficient because the opponent did not make the preventer of the validity of obligation the lack of power so that this objection would apply; rather, he introduced another preventer, which is the lack of benefit of obligation, i.e., compliance with sincerity in intention. This does not refute it. Rather, the correct response is: we do not concede that the coerced act is necessarily done due to the motive of coercion, for those who sacrifice themselves in the path of Allah do not engage in the act except due to the motive of law, and actions are by intentions, and Allah knows best. So reflect, for it is most worthy of acceptance.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the effect of coercion (ikrah) on legal obligation (taklif). It begins by defining two types of coercion: mulji' (overwhelming), which threatens life or limb, and ghair mulji' (non-overwhelming), which involves lesser harm like imprisonment or beating. The foundational text (matn) states that coercion does not prevent the validity of obligation for the coerced act or its opposite in either type. A group of scholars held that only overwhelming coercion prevents obligation. The Mu'tazila further differentiated: in overwhelming coercion, obligation is prevented for both the coerced act and its opposite; in non-overwhelming, only for the coerced act, not its opposite. The Hanafi proof (dalil) is that the coerced act remains possible, the agent remains capable, and his choice of the lesser evil demonstrates capacity. Various examples illustrate the point: if one is coerced under threat of death to drink wine, drinking becomes obligatory and abstaining sinful. If coerced to kill an innocent Muslim unjustly, killing remains forbidden, but if one abstains, he is rewarded. If coerced to utter words of disbelief, uttering is forbidden but treated as permissible due to coercion (like the case of 'Ammar ibn Yasir). The passage then engages with the Mu'tazila, who argue that coerced acts lack sincere intention, making obligation futile. The Hanafi response refutes this by noting that some individuals act from sincere motives even under coercion, and the ultimate knowledge of intention belongs to Allah. The discussion emphasizes that obligation is not voided by coercion alone; rather, the ruling on the coerced act depends on the nature of the act itself and the degree of coercion.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compulsion by threat of harm, divided into overwhelming (mulji') and non-overwhelming (ghair mulji')
ملجئ (mulji'): Overwhelming coercion that threatens life or limb
التكليف (al-taklīf): Legal obligation addressed to a capable agent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi argument regarding the capacity of a coerced person to perform the coerced act?
A) The coerced act is impossible.
B) The coerced act is possible and the agent is capable because he chooses the lesser evil.
C) The coerced act is only possible if the coercion is overwhelming.
D) The coerced act is possible only if the agent had prior intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, coercion does not strip the agent of legal capacity; the ruling on the coerced act depends on its nature and the degree of coercion, but obligation remains valid.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

The Slave's Capacity for Transactions: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi position that a slave has capacity for transactions; refuting al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a slave's capacity for transactions and evaluate the Hanafi proof using the concepts of ahliyyat al-takallum and dhimma.

Core Idea
The slave's intellect and direct address by Allah establish his legal personality for transactions and ownership.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i: Does a slave have original capacity for transactions (tasarruf) and ownership (milk al-yad)? The Hanafis argue that a slave possesses ahliyyat al-takallum (capacity for speech) based on intellect, and dhimma (legal personality) as evidenced by being directly addressed with divine obligations. Therefore, he can own and transact on his own. Al-Shafi'i holds that a slave lacks this capacity and can only act as a deputy of his master. By working through this dispute, you will see how usul principles generate different fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What is the exact ruling in dispute? The question is whether a slave has original capacity for transactions and ownership or only acts as a deputy.
The dispute is about the source of capacity, not about practical permissibility with master's permission.
2
State the Hanafi position and its core components
The Hanafis affirm two capacities: ahliyyat al-takallum (based on intellect) and dhimma (fitness to be addressed with rulings). Both are present in a slave.
Ahliyyat al-takallum is proven by the slave's intellect; dhimma by being addressed with obligations.
3
Explain the Hanafi proof (istidlal)
Intellect is not nullified by slavery – otherwise the slave's narrations would not be accepted. Being addressed with prayer, fasting, etc., shows he has dhimma.
The proof is rational (aqli) and empirical (being addressed with divine commands).
4
Consider the opposing view and Hanafi response
Al-Shafi'i holds the slave lacks original capacity. The Hanafi response: the slave's intellect and direct address are sufficient evidence of capacity.
The response does not detail the Shafi'i proof but counters by affirming the slave's status as mukhatab.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In Hanafi fiqh, a slave can own property and transact on his own (subject to master's rights); in Shafi'i, he acts only as a deputy.
This difference affects many rulings, like purchasing, selling, and owning independent of the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (العبد أهل للتصرف وملك اليد عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله ، فإنه عنده ليس أهلاً لهما، وإنما له التصرف وملك اليد خلافة من السيد لنا أنهما أي ملك التصرف وملك اليد إنما يكونان (بأهلية التكلم) وصحته (والذمة) وهي كون الإنسان صالحاً لأن يخاطب بالأحكام ( والأولى) أي أهلية التكلم إنما تكون بالعقل وهو لا يختل بالرق) بالضرورة (ولذا كانت روايته ملزمة للعمل للخلق) ولو لم يكن كلامه معتبراً بكون عقله مختلاً لم تعتبر روايته، بل يصير كالمعتوه (والثانية) أي الذمة إنما تكون بأهلية) الإيجاب عليه والاستيجاب له، ولتحققهما خوطب بحقوقه أي حقوق الله (تعالى) من الصلاة والصوم والكف عن المحرمات، إلا ما يفوت به خدمة السيد كالجمعة ونحوها ويصح إقراره بالحدود والقصاص) وتجب نفقته على السيد، وإذ قد ثبت أهلية التكلم المعتبر والذمة الصحيحة له صار أهلاً لملك التصرف وملك
Issue: The slave is qualified for disposition (tasarruf) and ownership of hand (milk al-yad) in our school, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. For in his view, he is not qualified for them; rather he has disposition and ownership of hand via deputyship from the master. Our proof: That both – i.e., ownership of disposition and ownership of hand – only exist through (capacity for speech) and its soundness, and (the dhimma), which is that the person is fit to be addressed with rulings. And the first – i.e., capacity for speech – only exists through intellect, and it is not impaired by slavery necessarily. Hence his narration is binding for action for the people. And if his speech were not considered due to deficiency of intellect, his narration would not be considered; rather he would become like an insane person. And the second – i.e., the dhimma – only exists through capacity for obligation upon him and entitlement for him. And due to their realization, he is addressed with his rights – i.e., the rights of Allah the Most High – such as prayer, fasting, and refraining from prohibitions, except what would cause loss of the master's service like Friday and the like. And his confession regarding hudud and qisas is valid. And his maintenance is obligatory upon the master. And since the considered capacity for speech and the sound dhimma are established for him, he becomes qualified for ownership of disposition and ownership.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This mas'ala addresses a usul dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i regarding whether a slave has original capacity for transactions (tasarruf) and ownership (milk al-yad). According to al-Shafi'i, a slave lacks this capacity; instead, any transactions he performs are as a deputy of his master. The Hanafis argue that both capacity for speech (ahliyyat al-takallum) and dhimma (legal personality) are present in a slave. Capacity for speech is based on intellect, which remains intact despite slavery – were it impaired, even his narrations (riwaya) would not be accepted. The dhimma is evidenced by the fact that a slave is addressed with obligations (prayer, fasting, etc.) directly by Allah, showing he is a recipient of the address (khatab). Therefore, he has the capacity to own and transact by himself, although some acts require the master's permission due to the master's right over his services. This is a classic example of how usul principles (ahl al-takallum, dhimma) lead to different fiqh rulings between madhahib.

Key Terms
أهلية التكلم (ahliyyat al-takallum): القدرة على الكلام المعتبر في رواية الحديث والأحكام
الذمة (al-dhimma): كون الإنسان صالحاً لأن يخاطب بالأحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proof in the passage, what evidence establishes a slave's dhimma?
A) The slave's ability to narrate hadith
B) The slave's intellect and capacity for speech
C) The slave being directly addressed with obligations like prayer and fasting
D) The master's permission for transactions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes a slave's capacity for transactions through his intellect and direct divine address, rebutting the Shafi'i view of mere deputyship.
